A NEW BOOK.
AUSTRALIA IN PEACE AND WAR.
By VV. M. FLEMING, M.H.R.
Some of the finest and most virile poems ever pub-
lished. Mr. Fleming has been called the second
Kipling, and certainly what Rudyard Kipling has
done for England and India Mr. Fleming has done
for our own country.
The earlier poems are splendid, but through the
latter portion of the book you can trace Australia’s
part in this war, and the unconquerable spirit of our
boys is seen in every line.
The introduction by Sir John Forrest, P.C.,
LL.D., M.H.R., etc., gives a personal
touch which greatly adds to the interest of the book.
It is a volume to read and re-read—and to think
about in between whiles. ORDER NOW! Obtain-
able from all Booksellers. Price 5/-

“EGCE HOMO .”
The Red Page
He teas comely '. and strong;
With tiny gold rings on his neck, where the
hair greiv low.
To me he was gentle always.
God! —he was the life of my life, beloved
beyond the power of language to express.
Fire escaped from Hell, and covered the
earth,
Devils came up from the pit, and gorged on
the helpless —
The men went forth to kill the devils and to
slake the fire.
I blessed him, and he went also.
He killed seven devils;
But they caught him and tied him to a stake;
They stabbed him with bayonets and grinned
and mocked,
And burned him with cordite —there was
phosphorus in his open wounds —•
They cut out his tongue, and maimed him
obscenely ;
Spat upon him. And God let him die at last.
I, Mary, Mother of the Man, buried him in
my heart.
Rows upon rows of crosses, pointing to
Heaven.
A world of flowers, and birds, and little chil-
dren,
The devils gone back to hell, and to torment,
And the fire put out.
I do not mourn for my crucified, he will not
return.
But I shall go to him.
BLACK BONNET.
Fiction
Welsh.
Caradoc Evans, author of that remark-
able realistic study of the Welsh in all their
Welsh My People, follows up that
triumph with a further assortment of horrors
and religious bigotry, entitled Capel Sion
(Melrose, per Dymock’s Library). Here is
still the grimmest realism, the most nauseat-
ing ugliness, and the vilest degradation of
religion; but this volume lacks the relief of
variety and the sense of humor, grim though
it was, that made the first book a work of
art. The stories are mere incidents, and
they are all in the one dreary key, dealing
with unromantic adultery, petty meanness
and gross superstition. Capel Sion is
written, too, in the curious diction of the
first volume —evidently achieved by a direct
translation, from the original Welsh in which
it must have been conceived —a diction
involved and difficult to follow, yet with
many of the Impressive qualities of the Old
Testament story.
In the first story Hannah Harelip deter-
mined to Seduce her young master in order
that she might become the mistress of his
house. She considered how to ensnare him,
and caused him to say: “Come, you small
wench, and I will fondle you.” Hannah
Harelip, we are told, “knew a little of the
ways of man,” and her method was simple
and successful. “Wench very all right you
are,” he said. And Hannah remarked:
“Frisky you feel, man.” But later she
became odious in his eyes. “What iobish-
ness you speech, you bad boar!” he said.
“Don’t you blohber old things, good maid.
Off you, then”; and he terms her “Dirty
smell of a mule.”
The best of the stories coneerns-'Ellen and
Lias Carpenter, who was a mighty man in
Sion, but who seduced Ellen and cast her
off. But her hate of him persisted Jbeyond
death. She made her son bury her in a
field: since she had failed to obtain justice
from the Welsh preacher, she meant to get it
on Judgment Day from “the Big Male,”
God. “Hap I shall hear the Trumpet before
-Capel Sion. Goodness everybody, there’s
things I shall speech to the Big Man about
Lias.”
But Lias was just as cunning. He bought
the field, and he bui’lt a hedge on all sides
of it so that none should trespass on it. “For
he, too, shall be buried in the field, and at
, the first sound of the Trumpet he will arise
and go up into the pulpit, and he will warn
the Big Man that Ellen Pugh was without
a name in Sion.”
Caradoc Evans’ first book was necessary
to eradicate the sentimental legend of
Welsh romanticism; this was done so drasti-
cally that a continuance of the savage treat-
ment is unnecessary. The first volume was
literature; the second is mere sordid real-
ism—rather monotonous and certainly dull.
And the reading public is not likely to
demand any further records of this tribe of
savages in West Wales —part of the poly-
glot peoples that inhabit Great Britain.
American.
That clever journalist who wrote Me—
the deliberately luscious and fleshly narra-
tive of an American girl who used her sex
to get her into compromising positions, and
yet escaped the consequences that in real
life would-be apt to occur, dallying on the
edge of dangers with an unconvincing inno-
cence, and making a good living out of it—
has evidently found the recipe popular. For
it has occurred to him that there are other
sisters in her family, who also can write
their experiences in love-making with an
equal suggestiveness. Marion: The Story of
an Artist's Model (Watt, N.Y., per The Aus-
tralian News Co., C.A.) is the alleged auto-
biography of “Me’s” sister, though it is
announced as by the author of Me. It is a
simple story of a simple girl who poses—but
only once in the nude—and quotes Ella
Wheeler Wilcox, and has a perfect figure:
“A perfect nude,” says one artist, “is not so
easy as people seem to think—one whose
figure is still young. Most models don’t
take care of themselves, and it’s the hardest
thing to find a model with firm breasts.
They all sag, the result of wearing corsets.
.... Your figure, I can see, is young and—er
—has life— esprit .”
But the story is much more convincing
than the melodramatic adventures of Me. ft
is the simple record of an attractive girl
who makes a living as a model, is made love
to, finds good male friends everywhere, and
ends up with the right man. It is realisti-
cally done, but the pictures are the most
exciting part of it.
A HSMRAIIAN.
Roy Bridges has never done such sincere
work as appears in The Immortal Dawn
(llodder and Stoughton). With a writer’s
instinct he has made use of several diaries
of returned Auzacs —these diaries which are
being sought for by the library of the Fed-
eral Parliament, and “will carry down the
years the echo of the laughter and the bur-
den of the pain of the Anzacs.” The inter-
est of these diaries, quoted and introduced
with apt comment by Roy Bridges, lies in
the diaries and not in the comment. They
are too real, too moving, too Australian, to
need much comment. And even in the edit-
ing of them Roy Bridges has failed to delete
unnecessary repetitions. But the remainder
of the book, consisting of pen-pictures of
Australia in war-time and of the pathos and
glory of the men who went, the men who
returned, and the women who waited, is
literature.
There is a picture of the Mallee country
that is finely done. Only a portion need be
quoted here, since the book will assuredly
find a large and eager public.
“The road went up the ridge as a ribbon
of fire to the sun. The paddocks, where the
wheat had died in the spring, rolled up brick-
red to the black pine clumps against the sky.
At every swirl of hot wind the sand w r as
blown up in yellow spirals; the spirals
whirled a rigadoon; and merging into the
one ochre-colored cloud crept down into the
roads. Red earth, dazzling blue sky, yellow
sun. The hard green Mallee scrub fringed
the road; stood in unrolled clumps, the green
unvarying in tint or tone —dull green against
the brick-red; black pines perched on the
ridges like big, lean carrion cr.ows. Sand
shifting as the oncoming waves of the sea ;
making great banks, where the Mallee scrub
stayed it; leaking through wire fences into
the tracks as iron-colored water tinkling
like a stream. If the wind died, the mirage,
a flickering fantasy to right and left-
lagoons of silver water flowing out over
the waste; gold-colored sands edging the
lagoons; out of them shaping in grey
smudges pine and eucalypt, shadowy, mys-
tical.”
But Roy Bridges can do more than fling
on the paint for gorgeous pictures; he can
etch, too, the shadows and the splendors
of the human heart. He gives us exquisite
little pictures of the divine commonplaces
of the war: the chap from the Bacltbloeks
riding down to enlist, the sullen bravery
of the lone mother left behind, the Last
Judgment of the casualty-list, the coming of
the parson with his news to the Outback
selection, the loneliness of the old folk wait-
ing for the rain—and their son, the bitter-
ness of the mother who has given her son
to the mother who hasn’t, the comradeship
that prompts a splendid lie to console a
slain cobber’s mother —these impressions are
perfectly limned with a sincerity and an
insight rare in our literature.
Roy Bridges has done a big thing. His
book deserves to stor'd on the same shelf as
The Straits Impregnable.
Pebbles on the Shore (Dent, per Dymock’s
Library), we are confidentially told on the
cover, is by a “well-known author, who pre-
fers to write under a pen-name (Alpha of the
Plough), in order to say what he chooses.”
A well-known author can say anything he
chooses under his own name. This well-
known author doesn’t say much, but he says
it genially. His little essays, evidently re-
printed from a Fleet-street journal, are
cheerful, gently humorous, quite English, and
always exceedingly conscious of the fact
that they are designed to fill a column once
a week without. annoying their casual
readers. As apparently English people like
this sort of thing in their papers, they ought
to like it in this issue of The Wayfarers’
Library (Is. 6d.).
The latest cheap editions published by Murray
(Is. 6d. out here) include H. A. Vachell’s Loot, Ger-
trude Atherton's Tower of Ivory, and Mary Cholmon-
d elw’s .Vo t • •rthr tan ding .
The Pacific-Our Job
O. Brunsdou Fletcher, in The New Pacific:
British Policy and German Aims (Mac-
millan; 7s. Od. in England), succinctly
sketches the history of settlement and devel-
opment in the Pacific, and suggests the possi-
bilities of the future. Tim volume -is bur-
dened with three prefaces, and is dedicated
to James Fairfax, “whose service and sym-
pathy have done much to help in voicing the
opinions of the people, and in developing the
resources of Australia”; and, incidentally,
the references to The Sydney Morning
Herald are so numerous that James Fairfax
would be but doing the proper tiling in
handing an iron cross to the author.
“The New Pacific! There it lies with its
tropical treasures and its colors of a terres-
trial paradise, its multitude of islands full of,
or crying for, people—potential wealth in
every degree of the lines of latitude which
define its nosition upon the earth’s surface.”
And Lord Rosebery said in 1883 that Aus-
tralia’s destiny was to be the trustee of the
Pacific. Brunsdou Fletcher tells the long
story, with a wealth of documents, many
striking pen-portraits of the men who blazed
the way, and a broad knowledge of the many
problems that still remain for settlement.
The trail of German intrigue and German
trade methods and German diplomacy runs
all over that vast expanse. In 1883 Aus-
tralia was already restless and very anxious
over German activity near her coast, and
Queensland took the drastic method of call-
ing Britain’s attention to the danger by
annexing part of New Guinea. “Then the
salient groups in the Pacific near Australia
were practically in Britain’s possession, and
she had .only to stretch out her hands to
secure them. To-day once more all the main
approaches to the Commonwealth and the
outlying points for defence are in Britain’s
hands, waiting the event of the war.” And
Germany has always recognised the value of
the Pacific, this “tropical empire waiting to
be developed,” and has always recognised
Australia as the basis and the capital of that
empire. Of the Solomons the author
enthusiastically says: “Compared with Fiji
this group is'declared to be like the treasure-
house of Aladdin beside the ordinary bank-
ing-house possessions of civilisation. Like
New Guinea it lies outside the hurricane
belt, and its native labor can be utilised....
Fiji and Samoa are fertile, but the Solomons
are dreams of fertility.”
“Germany’s policy in the Pacific has been
a mixture of bullying and palaver, threaded
with cunning and quite unscrupulous double-
dealing. . . .The Germans from the commence-
ment thought of the native as a chattel, gdod
to preserve' perhaps, and later on worth the
care and attention which one gives to acces-
sories to wealtii, but otherwise less than
human.” The British method has been to
preserve tlie'islands for the natives, with the
result that in Fiji the white man has to pay
rent to the Fijians for land that the Fijian
won’t work himself. The problem will be
to find a humane and fair mean between the
two methods.
Brunsdon Fletcher’s argument boils down
to this: “Germany can never be permitted to
return to her possessions; but the British
Empire, either through Australia or by
other means, must be prepared to make
greater sacrifices for, and to obtain a truer
understanding of, dominion in these wide
waters if development is to be fruitful and
sustained.” It will be Australasia’s job, and
it will have to be tackled. It will be a huge
responsibility, and will cost heavily, and
need skill and knowledge in the carrying out.
but it’s got to be done. Australia will not
only have to shoulder the White Man’s
burden; she will have to hump a whole ocean
and a thousand archipelagoes.

Paul Hookham briefly replies to Oliver
Lodge’s Raymond, or Life and Death, in
Raymond: a Rejoinder (Blackwell, Oxford,
Is.), taking exactly the same ground taken
by this page on its notice of the book.
Hookham’s contention is that mind commu-
nicates with mind, on this plane of earth, in
a way which is incomprehensible to our
normal setises and capacity of reasoning. “In
the presence of this unknown extension of
mental faculties any test of human survival
by the display of knowledge and identity on
the part of a dead person is impossible.”
We do not know the extent of the powers
o? a medium, and therefore we have no justi-
fication of setting a limit to those subliminal
powers. “If the medium can read the minds
of people in contiguity to her and known to
her we are not in a position to say that she
cannot read minds at a distance and un-
known.” What Lodge has to prove is that
-the medium’s communications could not by
any possibility have emanated from herself.
There is therefore no need for us to invoke
the spirits when the powers of the human
subliminal consciousness are remarkable
enough to produce all the results claimed.
The author goes on to outline the very inter-
esting Occultist theory of soul evolution
after death; and suggests that tlie soul after
death is avid to disengage itself from all
earthly interests and ties; and it may be that
these earthly passions and commonplace,
idle or frivolous, already dying, defires
struggle for a little while to live on through
a medium. Our troubles!
What Dots Bii.lzac Head";
Au unconscious witness to Billjim’s liter-
ary tastes is Beulah Bolton, secretary of
the Bush Club Book, who i-ecently told
us in the press what Billjim reads in the
bush. Last year this club sent over 7000
books to the lonely places of the continent.
Yet in the typical list given of books sent to
a camp the only mention of Australian
authors is the entry, “Australian Poets”;
and only two Australian books mentioned by
name find place in the whole article, Jose’s
History of Australia and An Anthology of
Australian Verse. Beulah Bolton claims that
she had been in charge so long that she
seems to be able to suit everybody’s tastes.
On this evidence Billjim’s taste for his own
literature is almost absent.
According to Gunner A. T. Jones, the firing-line
reads anything it can get hold of —and that isn’t
much. “The most-popular reading among our crowd
are the short yarns. These must be well
written and must never have the war as a subject.
Short yarns about boxing or any kind of
sport are popular, and also any kind of
good yarn about Australia which has not
too much sentiment about it. For my own part
I abhor the silly, sickly-sentimental yarns which
occur in some of the English magazines, but many
of our fellows read them with apparent enjoyment.
Practically the only U.S.A. stuff we read is a magazine
now and again, but some of the best short stories
I have read have been in the Saturday Evening
Post. Books of poetry are seldom read, but The
Sentimental Bloke is very popular. Very short pieces
of poetry about the war are acceptable, but they
must be true pictures and not contain too much
pathos. We do not have the time or inclination to
read novels. I can honestly say that I have not seen
above a dozen novels among all our crowd during the
time 1 have been with them, and that is about two
and a half years. If one gets a novel he swaps it
when finished for a mngadne, and that for another,
until he has to shift to n new position, when he
generally abandons everythin)? of that kind. Maga-
zines and papers are practically all we have, as they
are sent by post and are easily carried. News of
Australia is read with avidity; and the results of
boxing, racing and other sports are eagerly read
and discussed. As I have never left my unit since
1 joined in August, 1911, I cannot offer any opinion
about the reading in hospitals, etc.’’
“Bill Bowyang” from the front trenches: There
is no lack of newspapers, or of miscellaneous reading
matter, apart from periodicals. A society exists in the
Big Smoke for the purpose of supplying the troops with
literary matter. Under its benevolent auspices sack-
loads of books and magazines are despatched to
France every week and the contents distributed. No
doubt the committee has some method in the selec-
tion of this reading matter, but the items are often
extraordinarily mixed: My opinion is that the
sevenpenny editions of popular novels are most
appreciated, as these can always be slipped into one’s
pocket or haversack. Billjim is a great reader,
and when he returns home again he is going to take
more interest in literature than he did in the days
before Berlin Bill saw red.
“The Immature Male” : The cove who in a recent
B. mentioned illustrated papers was about right.
Here on the Peninsula (Sinai —not the glorious
original) the swaddy who goes on Cairo leave is
besieged with orders for “anything with tarts in
it.” La Vie ParisikXne and London Sketch are
just favorites, for Billj. has fallen hard,* both here
and in France, for that adorable, provocative, tan-
talising piece who winks and' smiles and dimpled
from the pages of these periodicals, and who is
known as the Kirchrier Girl. This dainty, elfish,
cheeky type of femininity is the goddess-elect of
the trenches; no well-furnished dugout is complete
without one or more of her; every discerning
swadd.v’s tent is decorated with her lovely legs; and
the shimmer of her silk-stockings dazzles one’s eyes
in every mess. Kirchner, who is naughty without
being nasty, subtle without being suggestive, is
almost as nopular as C. Chaplin, and a darned side
more artistic. " -
“A Billjim in France”: Funny books and papers
are what all the fellows like most, even the Funny
Cuts that the children buy for a. penny are eagterlv
read. All have an intense dislike for “blood and
murder” and war yarns. Personally, I scarcely ever
look at The Bulletin.
■ ©
The Fountain, by Julius West (Iris Pub-
lishing Co.. London, per; Dymock’s Library)
is stodgy fooling. The book is meant to be
a humorous initiation into the secrets Qf the
literary trade. Julius West heavily parodies
the Russian story and other “literary
stunts”; but unfortunately for him Stephen
Leacock has shown how that sort of thing
can be done. However, he has a useful
suggestion for the writers of biographies:
always give the date of your hero’s birth
wrong. “What happens then? Within a
week of the publication of your book a liter-
ary controversy will be raging in every
paper.. . . And the book will “have an enor-
mous advertisement.” Also: “Lives of
Empire-builders are fairly easily constructed,
as they consist mainly of speeches.... And
remember that whenever you find that you
have chosen a very dull subject, alter the
names, brighten it up by the exercise of the
imagination, and call it a novel.” There is
a lot more like this—and almost as painful.

You can’t
see the glass!
You can’t see the glass in a win-
dow that has been cleaned with
Bon Ami, it is so clear and free
from streaks and specks.
Bon Ami cleans by the wet-and- dry
method, like a metal-
polish. You rub a wet
cloth on the cake of
Bon Ami, making a
white lather. Cover

She Darkened Her Grey Hair
A Society Lady Darkened Her Grey Hair
and Stimulated Its Growth by a
Simple Home Process.
She Tells How She Did It.
A well-known society lady, who darkened
her grey hair by a simple home process,
made the following statement: “Any lady
or gentleman can darken their grey or faded
hair, stimulate its growth and make it soft
and glossy with this simple recipe, which
they can mix at home. To a half-pint of water
add loz. of bay rum and 1 small box of
Orlex Compound. These ingredients can
be purchased at any chemist’s at very little
cost. Apply to the hair every other day
until the grey hair is darkened sufficiently.
This mixture relieves itching and is excel-
lent for dandruff. It does not stain the
scalp, is not sticky or greasy and does' not
rub off. It will make a grey-haired person
look 10 to 20 years younger.”— Advt. [3

UNIQUE PROCESS FOR
RETAINING AND
REGAINING BEAUTY.
By Mlle. Charlotte Sirier.
Does your skin chap or roughen easily, or become
unduly red or blotchy? Let me tell you a quick and
easy way to overcome the trouble and keep vour com-
plexion beautifully white, smooth and soft." Just get
some ordinary mercolised wax at the chemist’s, and
use a little before retiring as you would use cold
cream. The wax, through some peculiar action, flecks
off the rough, discolored or blemished skin. The
worn-out cuticle comes off just like dandruff on a
diseased scalp, only in almost invisible particles. Mer-
colised wax simply hastens Nature’s work, which is
the rational and proper way to attain a perfect com-
plexion, so much sought after but very seldom seen.
The process is perfectly simple and quite harmless.
❖* * *
It is astounding the number of women who suffer
from unsightly growths of hair on the face, and it
will come as a piece of good news to know that
there is a simple substance known as powdered
pheminol which will remove it immediately and per-
manently. Mix a small quantity into a thin paste
with a little water, and apply to the objectionable
growths. In two minutes all trace of the hair will
have entirely vanished, and your skin will be as soft
and smooth as a child’s.
** * *
The hair should be allowed to breathe, and the
greasy film around each strand must be removed with
a mild non-alkaline shampoo. Soaps should be
tabooed. The very best solution for the purpose can
be made by dissolving a teaspoonful of stallax
granules in a cup of hot water. It stimulates the
scalp to healthy action, and at the same time leaves
the hair in that soft, fluffy condition so much
admired. Any chemist can supply you with an
original packet of stallax, sufficient to make 25 or
30 shampoos.
** * *
One need not resort to the very questionable ex-
pedient of hair-dye in order not to have grey hair
The grey hair can easily be changed back to a
natural color in a few days’ time merely by the
application of a simple, old-fashioned and perfectly
harmless home-made lotion. Procure from your
chemist two ounces of tammalite concentrate, and
mix it with three ounces of bay rum. Apply this to
the hair a few times with a small sponge and you
will soon have the pleasure of seeing your grey hair
gradually darkening to the desired shade. The lotion
Is pleasant, not sticky or greasy, and dors not injure
the hair in any way. ry

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Shell-Music.
You with the shell to your ear,
What do you hear,
Slim and so white
In the moonlight?
“Oh, I hear surging and shouting and sing-
ing',
The sea-folk at market—their little bells
ringing,
The tall weeds about them, the green world
above!
Oh, blithe are the pedlars of ribbons and
laces,
Yet, blither and sweeter, upon the wide
spaces
The footfalls of Love !”
You with the shell to your ear,
What do you hear,
Waves at your feet,
White and so siveet?
“Oh, I hear cooing and kissing and cooing,
The sound of sea-folk in their coral groves
wooing.
The red branches round them, the green
world above!
Oh, sweet are the songs of the witching sea-
daughters.
But sweeter, far sweeter, upon the wide
waters,
The footfalls of Love!”
You with the shell to your ear,
What do you hear,
Large eyes aglow,
Forehead of snow?
“Oh, I hear shouting and sobbing and sigh-
ing,
The sounds of the sea-folk in battle-rage
vieing,
The torn weeds about them, the green world
above!
The merman may shout as he stumbles and
slaughters,
But, ever and ever, I hear on the waters
The footfalls of Love!”
You with the shell to your ear,
What do you hear,
As you stand there,
Pale and so fair?
“Oh, as frost on the seaways the foam-spaces
glisten ;
My heart is the shell, to its. echoes I listen;
Red petals, rose petals around and above!
Oh sweeter, far sweeter, than little bells
ringing
Or market-men shouting, or mermaidens
singing,
The footfalls of Love!”
N.s.w.
RODERIC QUINN.
Tempest.
Along the coasts of life a storm of creeds
Rages; and windy voices evermore
Moan to their gods; grey controversies
pour
A slanting drench of words; and blossoming
deeds
Are blighted by the levin-hate that speeds ;
Curses go* hurtling; doctrine-thunders
roar;
And gusts of superstition slam the door.
Of any temple where sweet laughter pleads.
I stride the tempest. Gods go whirling past
Like shadowy scarecrows on the darkening
blast.
/My cloak of faith is tattered; yet I know,
Yet still I know that some' god rules the
skies;
For I have seen the light of light aglow
Far in the sunlit heaven of your eyes.
MAORILAND.
BARTLETT ADAMSON.
Girl-Gladness.
It’s holiday time on the hollyhock hills,
And I wish you would come with me, laddie-
love, now.
The butterfly-bells, from the Folly-fool rills,
Will ring if you listen and drop on your
brow.
So, dear, come along,
I’ve a kiss and a song,
And I know where the fairies are forging a
gong
To ring up the elves to a festival fair
Of sippets of sunshine and apples of air.
O laddie, my laddie, quick, run out of school
And away with a shout and a shake of the
head.
I’ll pick you a pearl from the pigeon-pink
pool
Where cuddles and kisses are going to bed.
Away, come away,
To the land of the fay,
For the afternoon tinkles your lassie-love’s
lay.
Play truant with Time and while Age is
asleep
I’ll give you the heart of my girlhood to
keep.
N.S.W.
ZORA CROSS.
The Soldier’s Room.
Sacred she keeps his room, a shrine for
prayer;
Placing before his picture brightest flowers,
Nor ever lets them wither with the hours.
But day by day renews them fresh and fair.
And reverent she will touch some trifle there,
At morn, or noon, or when the evening
hours;
A pipe, or book, as though the touch had
powers
To conjure up his presence from the air.
To-day she came and, kneeling, softly said
(As whispered words half round the world
could go) :
“Husband, a babe beneath my heart is fed
Within whose veins I feel your life-blood
flow.”
Between the shot-torn trenches he lies dead,
His winding-sheet the all-compassionate
snow.
TASMANIA.
H. W. STEWART.

Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be
refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby expressly
reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column all
contributions submitted, whether in the form of Politi-
cal, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or
Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects, and
not exceeding two columns in length, or, say, 3000
words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters
or Newspaper Clippings. All communications will be
regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions arc observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.

humorous or other topical drawings in line or icash,
of sufficient merit and interest and. adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will he paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but .unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn—will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, MAY 24, 1917.
THE WRECKAGE OF WAR AND THE
OTHER FELLOW.
The Bulletin is with all hands in not being
sure about anything these times; but it is
no less certain that the day after to-morrow
will last all day long than it is confident
that the thing called a Repatriation Move-
ment will, if monopolised by politicians, be
botched. Every day since the war broke
out (and long before) the Australian
public has had evidence of the gross
inefficiency of their numerous Parliaments.
These institutions have shirked their jobs.
In the administrative field their record
is one long catalogue of muddle and
waste. From their precincts have emerged
dismal complaints about the “failure of
the Australian people to recognise their
responsibilities to the Empire”—about the
necessity of private economy, of greater pro-
duction, of the encouragement of industry
and so on and so forth. It has all been a
mockery of the national life, for it has come
from men who have set the people evil
example. The transactions of the State and
Federal Parliaments covering the last three
years can be searched in vain for even a
superficial indication that the members
thereof were other than dodgers. The
life story of the last Federal Parliament
is one of men taking a prolonged holiday
and letting things go to the Devil. What
words of justification can be found for Can-
berra, for the transcontinental railway, for
the Northern Territory, for the Defenc?
Department? How many citizens are there
who with even a rucßmentary notion of this
country’s financial position can view
without disgust the record of the Post
Office or contemplate without dismay the
Commonwealth’s growing expenditure on red
cape? To expect that the Parliament'
responsible for these failures will, because
of a mere change in party strength, be any
less a shirker, is to be victim to hopes
which all experience of the past is a
warning. Party Government has made the
incompetence of Parliament notorious. Even
were the new Federal Parliament to honestly
tackle the legacy of muddle it has inherited
and set about making an improvement, there
is more than enough on hand to keep it busy.
That ( it should add to all this the business
of “repatriation” simply means calling
tenders for chaos. Anyhow. The Bulletin
looks with anxiety upon the wild project now
filling the mouth of Millen and others of
the Federal Parliament borrowing and
spending, with the assistance of the States,
indefinite millions on repatriation or any-
thing else. What can be pointed to in the
record of the Commonwealth or the States’
handling of the public cashbox to suggest
that these millions would not suffer the
same fate which befell other millions—that
the bulk of the money would not be wasted
and frittered away—leaving nothing but
aroma behind? It is all very well for
Millen to start up in the middle of an
election rumpus and say that “On behalf
of the Government I am authorised to state
that it fully accepts the responsibility devolv-
ing upon it to care for our soldiers and
sailors.” And it is in accordance with the
everlasting fitness of things that the yell
should be frantically cheered by the sur-
rounding crowd. But it is only the practical
side to this that is worth an old hat. When
this paper contemplates a practical side con-
sisting of glib talk about the expenditure of
over £22,000,000 plus more millions by any
crowd of politicians that can be named it
refuses to assess the national value of it at
more than Id.
Meanwhile, in spite of continual friendly
invitations to be specific and explain himself
and the scheme that is going to cost millions,
Millen, who is supposed to be running the
show, continues to do nothing more than
issue “replies to criticism.” The net result
of these replies is that Millen proves him-
self to have been misunderstood and it con-
clusively shows that it is impossible for
either himself or anyone else to understand
what he means. In an exchange of views the
other day between him and Ashford. N.S.W.
Minister for Lands, the one thing certain to
readers was that both were groping in the
dark. If it is not an absurdity that a Min-
ister should be going about explaining a
repatriation scheme in language which
itself calls for explanation—that everything
connected therewith should be in a con-
dition of paralysis—that the scheme should
evade all attempts by interested citizens to
capture it for examination —then humor has
lost one of its definitions. That a scheme of
repatriation within certain limits has long
been overdue in Australia cannot be denied.
But there are schemes and schemes. The
title is nothing. The usefulness of any
scheme must turn entirely upon its detail and
its administration, and no responsible citizen
can conceive of any scheme being satisfactory
to the Commonwealth that is not protected
at every point from manipulation by politi-
cians. If these millions that are talked
about by Millen and Co. are to be borrowed
and spent in the way suggested, either the
incompetence of Parliament will have to lie
guarded against by adequately utilising the
service of men outside, or there will be
another Canberra story. All the same, it is
an expenditure unnecessary. The burden on
Australia now is too heavy a one. To reck-
lessly set about heaping up the load is
reprehensible.
* * » *
There are a few matters the Federal
Government should not lose sight of. The
first of these is that the war is not over.
A second is that the responsibilities of Aus-
tralia to the dependants of the dead is not
yet known. Nor can the bill of obligation to
the wounded and the sick be yet added up.
There is the further consideration that any
scheme of repatriation must, if it be honest,
start at the problem of dealing with the
restoration to useful citizenship of those
soldiers and sailors whose physical or mental
efficiency has been impaired by serving their
country. It may put a heavy strain on
Australia to meet these national obliga-
tions. No doubt it will, and there is
no reason why before the Common-
wealth gets tangled into commitments of
millions of money for men able to fend for
themselves that part of the repatriation
movement dealing with those of our men
whose need is dire should not first be put
upon an assured foundation. It is there
that the great moral responsibility of the
Federal Parliament rests. Only an irrespon-
sible person can suppose that 'the men and
women of Australia would have anything
but admiration for Millen if he made
this his immediate objective. What are
the responsibilities of the country, any-
way, to the man who comes back from the
wars to civil employment, unharmed, ready
to take his place in the job common to every
other citizen? Is it any more than that he
should be able to get employment?
One cannot think so, nor believe that
£22,000,000 have to be borrowed to give
him an. opportunity of working up a
perspiration. Provision for him is intimately
associated with provision for his brother-
worker who will have to earn the pension
for the widow and. the man without a leg.
Australian industries will either reabsorb the
man who went away or fall down dead. If
they are going to live the problem of repatri-
ating the fit and the well is the problem of
the tariff and the unbuilt Australian ship
and the mine and the workshop and Aus-
tralians earning bread instead of borrowing
to acquire foreign fish and exotic confec-
tionery. Borrowed millions are no help in
this direction, because borrowed cash means
borrowed industry every time. To land
boodlers and the whole tribe of social
bloaters the idea of repatriating health v
citizens by plunging all hands further into
debt probably sounds grateful and comfort-
ing. But to most other people it sounds as
if the political mind reckons the day after
to-morrow is going to be the same as yester-
day. and that the problems vital to the
solvency and the future of Australia can be
let go hang. So far as this paper can see.
one aspect of repatriation is being wildly
exaggerated, and the other, which cannot be
regarded too seriously, is being merely
tinkered with. Millen is putting his cart
in front of fhe horse, or winning- the war
before it is over, or something.
[To express the views of The Bot/Eetin, by S. H.
Prior, F. J. Kirby- arid D. M. Wright, 214 George
Street, Sydney.]
THE DOOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Many experts consider that the internal unrest in
the German Empire wi11... .ultimately prove the
most potent factor in its downfall.
Hun they have called me, spoiler of all,
Raptor of maidens and wealth and lives,
Follow my trail by the blackened, wall
And the wail of violate wives.
Pitiless, arrogant, three wild years
1 have pillaged the minster and fouled the
sod,
Shaken with laughter at death and tears,
Jeered in the face of God!
Now, with my back to a dark wall penned—
Wherein is a low door, heavy-barred—-
With back-flung glance I await the end.
And back-flung foot, on guard !
Stirrings and rustlings, ghosts of sound.
Reach to my ears through the oak behind,
And a laxness girdles my shoulders round,
And my eyes with blood are blind.
Speed to the onfall, even so
As you sped to your fate in a time before,
Ere the planks shall rive, and my numb brain
know
What waits behind the door!
The shot bolts bend and the lintels creak,
And the scaling and straining iron bands
That traverse the bellying panels, speak
Of the pressure of unseen hands.
Fronting and flanking, before, beside,
Safe at your distance awhile at least,
Your gathering legions tramp and ride
Like vultures that scent the feast.
Boastful, and brave as a brute is brave,
As I have lived shall I also die —
With never a pang for the griefs I gave,
Or the soul I have lost thereby !
Yet shall I sickle you many a row
In the harvest of havoc, and lies, and lust,
Ere I reel to the shadows—as all things go—
And my blond head beat the dust.
Savagely sullen, uncaptivate,
Tawny and bloodied, steeleu to endure,
Stricken, but great as the brute is great,
1 stand to the end —be sure!
Hither and there, in your Senate-halls,
Chattering, senile, greybeard men
Gossip of deeds ere the winter falls
And the snows be come again!
Hence I have watched them, the while they
diced
With Death at the table, each to each,
Naming me robber and Antichrist.
Forging the sword of speech !
See where 1 stand for the taking! Strike
Suddenly, secretly, where you will!
Though it shall cost you the price, belike,
Strike for the heart and kill!
AN hen you have slain me—if so ye may !
Fashion a grave for me, wide and deep
Hide me securely with turf and clay >
And grey stones piled in a heap ;
Build you a fire above, around
The marks of my blood in the sodden
plain—
Lest from that seed and the shattered ground
I shall arise again !
Sear me with' steel, and my blood with fire,
Yet shall your children’s children tell
How that I trampled your best to mire,
Made of your worst a hell.
Lessoned you first that the gentle game
You cherished from old to the times that
be,
Calling it war, was to war the same
As tears to the winter sea.
Read you the craft of the greater chase,
Burned on the hearts of the things you
love—
That he would stand by the War-God’s grace
Strikes not with a silken glove!
Lore I have taught; you, whereof not least
Shall this go down to the lesser day—
How should ye fear to outvie the least
If a bestial game ye play?
And wherefore baulk at a deed half-done.
Like dull-brained clods with a purpose
dull?
I have whetted my talons, and seen the sun;
I have ravaged and feasted full!
Tending and tilling my herds and lands
The folk of my little duchies strove.
Offering duty with eager hands —
I cared not for their love! —
Gentle of heart as their hands were hard,
This big, soft people of simple worth.
And I plotted to crown theip. aftenvard.
Salt of the white man’s earth:
Therefore I took them, and bade them sing
Songs of the sword for their vineyard-
tunes,
Ordered them, hot for the conquering.
_ln a myriad proud platoons.
Now, with their patience overpast
In the hate of my rule that this late day
brings,
They are my judges, whose love I cast
Out from my scheme of things!
For their facile kin that I took and used
Who are rotting bodies beneath the mud.
For pride of caste, and for power abused,
They cry for my living blood!
The shot bolts quiver, the planking strains.
The jamb-stones weaken and split and
flaw,
The sluggish blood from my slow heart
drains
Like snow in the springtide thaw.
Shoulder and sinew and slacking hand
Struggle to answer the spurring will.
Swaying on wide-braced feet I stand
Failing, but fierce, to kill.
Come, for I wait, and I fain would die
Slain by the mercy my foes afford.
Rather than blame of my blood should lie
Red on my people’s sword!
For out from the portal my dulled eyes see.
Through tottering timbers that twist and
splay,
Plucking the pins of the hinges free.
. Tearing the bars away,
Wraiths of the wrongs of an iron past
Over the clamoring threshold pour.
And I learn, in the long, red wrath at last.
What waits behind the door!
VICTORIA.
J. ALEX. ALLAN.
A RELIGION OF LAUGHTER.
Dear Bulletin, —The recent demand of a
prominent Scotchbyterian cleric for a
general brightening up of religious church
services suggests that the element of
mirth has been sadly left out of the
programme. This was perhaps inevitable.
The religious systems that have mastered
the world all came from Asia; and Asia is
a very serious continent, with a poor idea
of a joke and an amazingly poor sense of
the ridiculous. Yet while all the so-called
moral forces have been at work, while men
have been summoned to faitli and prayer
and self-sacrifice, the plain fact seems to
stand out about a mile over all the contro-
versies and creeds and commandments that
laughter is the one saving thing in this world,
and that the deeds of men towards om*
another are black or white precisely in the
proportion in which laughter is absent or
present. “By their deeds ye shall know
them’' may be an excellent gauge of the
moral standard of a people; but “By their
grin ye may measure them” would have as
much practical force- and be a good deal
easier to apply.
The old Puritan -who wrote “For
the world, I hold it a place not to live in.
but to die in ; an hospital, not an inn,” put
the solid gloom on things in a manner that
can still win applause where the elect meet
to deplore the laughter of the times. But
with modern laughter has come sanitation,
better social laws, wider individual freedom,
a diminution of crime, a kindlier feeling
between man and man, and a thousand things
which no religion of blackness and human
despair could ever attain. And all this makes
it tolerably evident that mirth must be the
centre of any religion which can put its spell
on the great future. The solemnities are
done with. The force that can make a man
live with a grin on his face and die with
wholesome laughter on his lips is the one
which offers salvation to mankind. For
mankind is wholly ridiculous, and man at
his highest and best knows himself to be so.
The top-heavy animal with tufts of his
primeval fur still adhering to him in places
who puts a tail to his dress-coat because his
own was worn off with sitting down to ponder
the amazing absurdity of himself, cannot be
taken seriously in the light of moon and
stars and icecream and the Kaiser and the
victory of the Win-the-War party. Judged
on the animal plane, man looks futile and
insane. Most of him could never catch his
prey in a forest teeming with game; and if
he denied his canine teeth and declared for
pure vegetarianism he couldn’t pick out with-
out the aid of books and a chemist’s labora-
tory the leaves and fruit that would sustain
life from those that meant sudden death.
The thing that a wild pig knows by instinct
man couldn’t learn in 12 years of careful
study; and a kitten three weeks old has a
better chance of living on its own resources
1 ban a bank-clerk turned loose on a desert
island. Judged on the intellectual and spiri-
tual plane, however, man looks a lot worse.
That the person who is supposed to appre-
ciate poetry and art and music, and to be
capable of continuous and organised think-
ing. should still go out after his enemy in
exactly the spirit of a wild boar or infuri-
ated bantam rooster, is bad enough ; but that
he should glory in doing so and regard as the
top heroism the sacrifice of his own life in a
bloody mauling at the hands of the opposing
human pack, appears to be the final and
supreme jest of the ages.
To get the right-angle on things and
see where the laugh really comes in
means genuine progress. If the world
in general, and the Kaiser in particular,
had possessed a sense of humor the
present war could never have started. Ger-
many was pretty solemn —its love for the
always-ridiculous sausage is proof if it. In
its alleged humorous newspapers it mistook
the mere mention of insanitary things for
wit, and grunted heavily over its beer at the
plain obscenity which other peoples recoiled
from as poisonous. But even with the Ger-
man countenance permanently wreathed in
dullness, the war could hardly have been
possible if other nations had appreciated the
absurdity of things. Britain, bowing in fat
of Cobpen and singing “Rule
Britannia” to the accompaniment of waving
paper flags, was an amusing circumstance in
the universal vaudeville. But Britain was
supremely unconscious of anything ridicu-
lous in her attitude, and went on nursing a
Nonconformist Conscience, and reading
Rudyard Kipling, and making vague bleat-
ings about_ “Empire,” while the foe sharp-
ened his. mirthless gun. One good and honest
international laugh might have saved the
situation; but the article was not supplied,
though the material was lying everywhere
around out of which a million laughs might
have been made. If the purpose of religion
be ; as the very name asserts, to bind some-
thing fast, and that something is presumed
to be morality, the effect of mirth on -the
individual is clearly to produce the ethical
uplift that all religions have striven for, but
■which only in rare instances they have suc-
ceeded in producing. To a man with a pro-
perly-developed sense of humor there can be
no need for the Ten Commandments, nor for
25. _ The absurdity of crime is the moral pro-
tection against it. That a human creature
in a bobtailed coat with hair on the top of
him, and feeble and useless claws on the
ends of his fingers, should go abroad with his
feet in boxes made out of portions of cow
and steal the affections of another human
creature with more hair and even less claws
who belongs to somebody else in another bob-
tailed coat, ps obviously too ridiculous to be
thought of. So the divorce-court recedes as
the sense of humor grow r s. The laughable
inanity of theft, murder and lying must also
cause these arts to disappear from human
reckonings. The doctrine of living for your
neighbor never quite caught the fancy of
mankind; but the desire to preserve your
neighbor because he clowns so absurdly and
well is a fine incentive to the world-wide
brotherhood of cheerful lunacy which seems
to be the best the race can ever hope for-
And so in comes about that the man who
can make us laugh is the pure prophet, and
that the great priesthood of mirth must give
the world its future impulse towards right-
eousness. The awful calamity of machine-


made jest which theatrical conspirators ex-
ploit for deadly solemn greed of gain is
merely a passing phase of the upward evolu-
tion. In the brave days to come the real
comedian may arise; and. even though it
cost a revolution of blood, a theatrical mana-
ger may yet be forced to the front whose
mind is less dreary than the funeral of an
undertaker. Then it will not matter much
whether there is a clown in the pulpit or a
harlequin at the reading-desk ; for the church
of the people will be where the larger laugh-
ter is steadily on tap. And, while the fat
sides of the jolly future shake, the old out-
of-work Devil will blow hot on his solemn
Nonconformist Conscience and wonder at
the new Grace of God that has given man a
truly workable religion at last.—Yours, etc.,
Curse o’ Moses.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Enterprise’s Habit of Moving Meals
About.
An alarmed squeak was heard the other day
from Melbourne and another from Maori-
land. It transpired (1) that so much but-
ter had been hurried away from the Com-
monwealth that the supply in store for
local consumption was running short; and
(2) that owing to a shortage of transport
Maoriland stores were bulging with butter,
ind great apprehension was felt by some-
one or other that if the collection inside
was not ?ot rid of Cocky would have to
trant Strawberry a holiday while the
country fell into ruin and decay. Where-
fore the Australian cow proprietor was
urged to extract increased quantities of the
raw material for butter-making from his
animals and fill the void, and the Maori-
land Ministry was exhorted to acquire more
ships to carry the surplus to Britain or a
watery grave. This is a very interesting
situation. It seems that Australia has
been able to get rid of so much butter this
year that butter will have to be imported'
to give the population a chance of lubricat-
ing its crust. Conversely—Maoriland butter
has moved off so slowly that, unless the
accumulation melts or the local inhabitant
is encouraged by the dealers’ increasing prices
to devour larger quantities, the stacks of
butter over yonder may topple over into the
Pacific. And as Maoriland has just bought
1,500,000 bushels of wheat from Australia,
and the Commonwealth has enough grain on
hand to feed the populace and myriads of
mice for several years, supply, and demand
all round appears to be tied up in a knot,
and the necessity for much lifting about and
pulley-hauling boxes and bags about is firmly
established. Where the advantage is to
Australia of rushing butter a wav over the
waves and dragging more butter' back, and
to Maoriland of being covered with butter
and short of wheat, may be left to be
explained by the Argtjs. In the meantime
there is a point about all this business which
should give the numerous Governments of
Australia a tip about how employment may
be “pi’ovided for the people” in the event
of Cohen turning sour. This is to export
all the. cheese, jam, butter, metals, leather,
wool, timber, hides, fruit, boots, hats, collars
and everything else grown or produced in
the six States, making use of any article
that had not travelled 6000 miles in a ship
a penal offence. Then the national require-
ments could be met by buying these articles
somewhere else, thus giving employment to
thousands of citizens carting things about,
boxing them up, prying them out of cases,
driving waggons and vans, acting as tally
clerks, building premises to hold and take
delivery of consignments in and out, writing
out insurance policies, preparing claims for
rebates, losses through theft, depreciation or
short delivery. The whole continent would
throb with excitement and business. Mean-
while the use of ships becomes necessary to
recover for Australia some of the butter
which should not have been sent away, and
achieving for Maoriland the supply of wheat
that was not grown because butter which
cannot be got rid of was, manufactured in-
stead. And if the meaning of all this is
not that an excessive quantity of shipping
is being used to do twice over things which
could easily have been omitted while the
ships were allowed to do what has not been
done while they were doing these other
superfluous jobs, then the time is ripe for a
foreign-trade fiend to explain the general
disorder.

“The Situation,” Seen Through Blue*
tinted Glasses.
In the early days of the present offen-
sive we talked of blowing the Germans
off the face of France, or words to that effect.
It suited slackers to assume that British
artillery had asserted an immense superiority
and would, whenever it was called upon,
smash any possible German position to
smithereens. But there was small comfort
for slackers in some of last week’s news from
the Western Front. Beach Thomas wired
to England: “The weight of the Ger-
man artillery is still a feature of the situa-
tion.” And what said the Council of Work-
men and Soldiers’ delegates at Petrograd?
“It is evident that German Imperialism,
after defeating the Western Allies, will turn
on us, seize the country, and enslave Rus-
sians.” In the face of Thomas’s statement
and the pessimistic Russian prediction, it
seems absurd to talk of blowing the Huns
out of France this year. As for the reports
from Holland of “Socialist” speeches in the
German Reichstag, they must be read in the
light of a prior warning from Switzerland.
Two of the untrustworthy Socialists, so-
called, talked of a “revolution” and a “repub-
lic,” and they were not promptly arrested
as they would have been had they meant what
they said. Apparently Messrs. Scheide-
mann and Ledebour were leading players
in the “stage-managed revolutionary move-
ment” w-hich, according to the warning issued
from Switzerland three weeks before, was
about to be put up by the German Govern-
ment in order to fool the Allies in general,
and the Russians in particular. Note how
carefully the German censorship deleted a
passage from Chancellor Bethmann:Hol-
weg’s speech, in order that the deletion
might be emphasised when it was subse-
quently cabled from dear old Amsterdam.
The Chancellor artfully cried, “I will not
be diverted from the proper path by Herr
Schiedemann’s threat of a revolution.” It
was just the theatrical performance that the
Allies had been told to expect. Methodical
German craft, or artfulness, appears crude
enough when the test of shrewd suspicion is
applied to it. It works by means of con-
spiratorial newspaper reports and the aid of
trembling neutrals. The Chancellor doesn't
talk to the Parliament; he talks to the world,
and the lie that he wants to rub specially
hard into the world’s credulity is carefully
deleted from the speech that is wired to
Amsterdam, with a view to the inevitable
report of the omission that will follow. What
has become of the “famine” in Germany
these last few weeks, and the shortage of
metals which was said to be acute nearly
three years ago, and the separate peace that
Austria was said to be seeking at the
beginning of this year, and all other com-
forting developments which existed only in
reports from Amsterdam? They were
myths to suit the slackers and blind the
eyes of optimistic patriots to the truth that
the British Empire is in a tough plight and
that German designs on Australia and Africa
are as much alive as ever they were, although
their growth has been stunted. Germany
counts on recovering her lost colonies for
certain, and she expects to have powerful
naval bases in the Pacific in the not-distant
future. She argues on the war map, whilst
the slackers argue on their cold feet that
the fight is still being won by inches on the
Western Front.

Weighing Christianity.
W. M. Fleming, M.H.R., writes :
Re “Christianity Deceased” (B. 10/5/’l7). When
you approach the heart of the matter, you say, “The
gentle and beautiful religion became a creed.” Cer-
tainly with many, possibly with most, it did become
a creed. That is exactly what led to this ghastly
war. That the priests and peoples have so largely
made a creed—a cruel, bitter creed —out of, as you
rightly put it, a “gentle and beautiful religion”—
condemns not the religion but man's wrong-headed-
ness. You know of throwbacks. We stockbreeders
are constantly meeting with them amongst the lower
animals. This war is a throwback in human exist-
ence. It is not that Christianity has failed, but
that so far man has failed to absorb it, that he has
evolved along flic moral side much less rapidly than
he has travelled along the road of mechanical inven-
tions and commercial expansion. We have got
things out of focus. A little more of the religion—
perhaps a little less of the creed —and we would
have avoided the war. Man is a fighting animal; and
with a wider and fuller acceptance, a truer under-
standing of Christianity, he would have long since
learned that there are other things much more worth
fighting than is his fellow man.
That the officials of Christianity have, for a
generation deplored its decline, is not strictly true.
They have deplored the loosening hold of the priest-
hood. They continue to do so with more and more
grief. That by no means proves that our religion
is losing its hold. Christianity began and remains
not a matter of priests but of public morality; and
you certainly hit the mark when you state that: “This
noble faith is unable to prevent the nations plunging
into a dreadful war,” and “For the last three years
the world has plunged back into the welter of bar-
barism, preceding the beginning of the Christian era.”
That is, I think, the complete answer to your con-
clusion. War in some shape is inevitable, and will
always remain so; but this fratricide amongst civil-
ised peoples is the outcome of the teachings of those
who would revert to the religion of Thor and Womx.
As already stated, it is a reversion to type. And
when a good stockbreeder gets a reversion to an
undesirable type he eliminates all chance of that
reversion propagating its kind. He does not weakly
sit down and say that his plan of breeding is a
failure. Guided by experience he goes on to better
things by wiser handling of his material. There may
possibly be a better religion than Christianity in the
yet undreamed-of wonders of the future, but, if so, it
is a long way off. This war has proved, not that
Christianity is a failure, but that man has not yet
understood it sufficiently to be capable of absorbing
its full beauty, as he certainly will need to do before
he can be ready to receive any possible higher and
holier thing that may yet descend from Heaven
to bestow its blessings on the human race.
To which The Bulletin replies that if,
after 1900 years of Christianity, “man has
so far failed to absorb it,” there must be
something wrong either about Christianity
or mankind.
A Plea for the Liberation of Kings.
“Pat O’Maori” puts a case:—
Is there no philanthropist who will start a crusade
for the emancipation of kings? Slavery is abolished,
serfdom exists only in a modified form in a very
few countries, and everywhere oppression is growing
scarcer and man given a show to stand on his own
feet and assert his own will. Consider the case of
a constitutional sovereign. He is born into absolute
and abject servitude to an idea, and, although thou-
sands nominally do his bidding, the bidding itself
must be exercised in so narrow a field that it amounts
to nothing. In return for the complete and life-long-
loss of his liberty a king is allowed to wear a gold
hat and whiskers —the latter having become the in-
signia of waybacks and monarchs. A king cannot
pick his own wife, shape out a career for himself or
wiu fame, honor or genuine affection in any walk of
life. He cannot be an expert at games, because any
loyal subject' would hesitate to beat a sovereign ; he
cannot be an athlete and know the thrill of contest
in a public arena; he cannot hope to shine as a poet,
a musician or a painter because anything he did
in those lines would win applause beyond all his
deserts merely on the grounds that a king did it;
No king may get drunk in the cheer of ordinary
pubs, lose himself in the bush, tour with a company
of down-at-heel theatrical barnstormers, taste the
fare at anarchistic cafes, take a hand in a debate
at the Y.M.C.A. rooms, make love to a dainty young
thing in the summer moonlight of the beach, or do
any of the ordinary things permitted to any free
citizen. A king cannot choose his religion, vote at
elections, stand for Parliament or reap any of the
harvest of liberty for which the great martyr in the
cause of Man died. Much has been written of the
democratic advance made when despotism gave place
to limited monarchy; but the real question which
has not had the limelight but which deserves to be
answered is whether any democracy lias the right for
its own comfort and safety to so far degrade a man
as to make him a king. Mercy, which is now the
prerogative of crowds and not of monarchs, ought to
be exercised here and a vast movement inaugurated
for the liberation of kings. If the glittering and
servile functions of the constitutional monarch fill
any real or valuable part in the scheme of things, an
automaton of some kind might surely be invented to
wear the crown; but, without attempting to labor
the point, the job of kingship could surely be
intrusted to someone who would not find the restric-
tions of office intolerable as long as the royal bones
or regal hay were in plentiful supply. The cause of
kings is the cause of manhood. No member of the
world-wide democracy has a right to impose on
another burdens which he would he unwilling to
carry himself.
A Great Industry and a Chance for
Australia.
There is talk about the Federal Govern-
ment inducing someone or other to start on
a shipbuilding campaign, with a view to
“mitigating the effects of the submarine and
supplying Britain with food.” The subject,
is being inquired into, and more will be
heard about it later on. Meanwhile there
are more or less futile yarns going round
concerning the construction of wooden ves-
sels and composite vessels, and a local genius
has come forward in Melbourne with a
scheme for carrying on the inter-State trade
of Australia with barges, towed round the
coast like a string of sausages by one or
two steamers after all the others now in
commission had been despatched to the Atlan-
tic. Pending information about the velocity
with which vessels of the barge or any other
type can be run off the stocks in Australia
it may be taken as probable that the amount
of mitigation inflicted on the submarine by
the _ arrival of Australian-built ships iu
British waters is not likely to win the war.
And it is quite possible that if J. Bull gets
to the stage at which he has to place depend-
ence on the arrival of these craft with grub,
the situation generally will look mighty blue.
The building of ocean-going ships fit to carry
perishable products is not a business to be
put into full blast by mere talk. All the
same, the idea of getting this enterprise on
to a practical basis is one the Federal Gov-
ernment should be encouraged to persevere
with. Fiddling about with windjammers
would be mere waste of time. It is the
steel-plated, high-powered steamer that the
Commonwealth wants now and will require
in large numbers when the war is over, no
matter how it may end, or when. The
material is here in Australia, the continent
is encircled by wet and there are innumerable
suitable places for building ships far larger
than have yet been launched from Austra-
lian yards. Work and imagination alone
are necessary to expand the equipment
already on the premises. The demand fox-
shipping at the end of the war will be as
insistent as the howl for rain in'a drought.
There will be a shipping famine, and unless
the Commonwealth gets busy noio the nation
will pay through the nose for the service of
ships built and owned in other counti-ies.
There is opportunity here for the Govern-
ment to do big things, and if it does then:
the gain to Australia will be enormous. Other
countries build steamers. Why not Austra-
lia? This paper knows of no reason. Bill
it has a strong suspicion that to make a
start with the intention of building Austra
lian freighters and passenger steamers at the
same gait as a vessel called the Brisbane
was put together would be plain lunacy.

The Religion of Billjim.
'‘3227”: From time to time during the past
three years there have appeared articles
descriptive of the characteristics, emotions
and tastes of Bill.jim. Only a few of
these, however, have mentioned his religion,
and then in such a manner as to suggest
that at heart he is irreligious. He is not.
His language may be in the strenuous cir-
cumstances more lurid than in pre-war days
and his morals a little slackened (not too
much stress on this, though, please), yet his
hold on deiinite faith is stronger than before
the riot. Nearly every member of the A.I.F.
possesses and uses some simple book of devo-
tion issued by one or other denomination.
These are much more popular than Bibles
because they are not so conspicuous.
Billjim seldom kneels down in the publicity
of his billet or hut to say his prayers; yet.
on the other hand, unlike the Tommies, he
will never interfere with a man who does.
Then, too, Billjim has no time for blas-
phemy. The roughest soldier wall call on
a shickered mate indulging in blasphemy to
"cut it out.” Our Bill is taking a greater
interest in his own particular ideas of
religion than he ever did before. He judges
his padre as he does his mate, and provided
the padre comes up to the standard of a
“sport” Bill will work both with and for
him, even to the extent of outwardly con-
forming to practices he had well-nigh for-
gotten or even to attendance at extra ser-
vices out of parade hours. As to the con-
tention that the churches have not come up
to Bill.tim’s expectations-ywell, Billjim is
more than satisfied. In their choice of
padres the churches have been either fortu-
nate or careful, or both, for they have cer-
tainly given of their best. In dug-out or
billet the Australian soldier very frequently
drifts into informal arguments that range
over a wide field of religious subjects, and
practically all hands and the cook stand for
the orthodox. The fact that the boys can
swear while discussing sacred subjects does
not denote any irreverence. It is merely
their unconscious way of showing all-in
sincerity. Believe me, Billjim is as sincere
in his religion as he is in everything else. .

The Tribulations of an Awakened Bear.
For a week or two after Czardom collapsed
gasometers laden with hot air were explod-
ing all over the world. The fall of an old
tyranny looked like something to be enthusi-
astic about. Wherefore earnest democrats
rejoiced at the astounding spectacle of a
nation moving in a day from medievalism to
constitutional freedom, arriving into 1917 at
a bound, so to speak, from the Dark Ages.
Had not the earthquake been caused by the
spread of democratic ideals among the Rus-
sian millions, and by their hatred of Ger-
manic influences and their fear that unless
they took charge of the situation the war
would be waged with insufficient intensity
to prevent disaster? They sounded all very
well, these yarns about a Bear casting off
his chains and governing himself and impart-
ing more ginger to the fight. But what was
going to happen next was in the circum-
stances of the hour a lot more important
than what had occurred to the Romanoffs.
In its historical setting the revolution of the
other day may probably be an imposing inci-
dent. At present it looks a calamitous sort
of affair of which the end may be anything.
It may even only be a phase of the revolu-
tion in which Russia has lived for genera-
tions, and may yet be doomed to endure for
more generations. History teems with the
records of revolutions that were merely a
step towards ruin—with as many more that
only emerged from chaos after prolonged and
bloody struggles. If the revolution suc-
ceeds in Russia R will be the most amazing
thing of the kind ever known, for there is no
parallel to such a disturbance. It would
mean a people who know nothing of repre-
sentative government suddenly putting this
device of modern democracies into working
order—a people, too, who in the mass are
illiterate and superstitious. What the world
sees now is a disordered rabble vaguely grop-
ing about in the dark. The lesson of it all
seems to be one that is as ancient as this
weary old earth—that the force that wants
to sweep something away without first know-
ing what is going to occupy the vacancy is
calling tenders for tragedy.
Tasmanian casualties in the recent Labor
debacle are ex-Speaker Woods (“John
Drayman”), ex-Lands Minister Belton,
and ex-“ Whip” Ben Watkins. Of these,
Woods and Watkins have been members of
the State Assembly since 1906; Belton
dates from 1909. All three, with the late
C. R. Howroyd (vrho died before the declara-
tion of the poll for Darwin, after defeating
Kink O’Malley), resigned from the State
Parliament on the strength of an Electoral
Bill having passed both Houses giving them
the right to resume their seats if they were
unsuccessful in the Federal contest. " This
Bill has been for months awaiting the King’s
assent, but whether George V. is .too busy, or
whether the Bill has been submarined some-
where, nobody seems to know. In any case
it is doubtful that the assent will arrive in
time to obviate the need for holding the by-
elections necessary for the filling of the
vacancies created by the four resignations
mentioned, and for the vacancy left by the
recently-deceased Burgess. Tassy’s present
Electoral Act provides that the writ to fill
the latter kind of vacancy must issue within
10 days of the death of the member, and it
has been so issued. The same Act permits
(or compels) the delay of the writs for the
other seats until the Federal poll is officially
declared. The Crown Law authorities have
wrestled with the tangle and have decided
that the simple plan of issuing writs for the
whole five vacancies on the same date, thus
saving time and money, could not be followed
because of the fat-headed wording of the Act
A Territory for a Peanut.
Randolph Bedford writes :
The Commonwealth Parliament, busy as it may be
with winning the war, will have to take at least
one clay off to settle two domestic problems—that of
Canberra, and more importantly and urgently, that
of the Territory. The Territory is most important,
because its development will be the big factor in the
maintenance of White Australia, a principle on
which Cook, Irvine, Watt and others of the success-
ful junta are known to be more than suspect. The
people who made the worst fist of Canberra were
all imported—the spread-eagled O’Malley now for
the good health of Labor purged from it; the
raucous Webster, who wasted quarts of money
in alterations at the deplorable Post Office
while saving a few pounds gathering up ends of
string and drippings of sealing-wax; and the calam-
itous Hindmarsh, unfortunately re-elected on his
promise to Win the War. The fight at Canberra was
the fight between Australians and an imported man
whose plan, so far as I could see, of the work
already done, and of its contempt for contour and
natural features, is the plan of a city very fair to
see from an aeroplane and damnably uncomfortable
to live in. There is only one Government for Can-
berra, government by commission during the con-
structional period; a commission of an architect, an
engineer, a real estate man, and an agricultural man.
As helpers, the Government might call in a member
or two of the Inter-State Commission, which, since
its tariff report was put into cold storage, seems to
have dropped its bundle, or fallen down a crack or
hidden itself in a gasometer.
The position of the Northern Territory is more
serious, more dangerous, more urgent of supervision.
The administration of the present crowd demands
searching inquiry, but all the charges can be passed
here with this tabulation: Lunatic waste of money on
experimental farms ; the loan of a Government ship
to a missionary that became wreckage (the ship, not
the missionary), the semi-wreck being then repaired
at Government’s expense; the third degree on a
native prisoner to make him plead guilty on a
murder charge ; the growing slackness of the Aborig-
ines’ Protection Department; the general hostility of
an imported administrator and his imported staff to
Australians in the Territory. My evidence of these
things may be capable of rebuttal, but there is a
concrete case of disloyalty to the spirit and the
letter of Australian law and Australian sentiment
which I know of my knowledge. Hughes, who
once dared not mention Imperial Federation, now
says that the Imperial Federation policy is a policy
for the whole of the British Empire which should
cover every phase of our national, social and
economic life. Our “national life”—Australia gov-
erned partly by a Parliament in which Australian
representation would be negligible; our “economic”
life—a tariff amounting to the Freetvade policy of a
Welshman who once, and once only, was a Protec-
tionist, and that in support of a duty on grindstones.
P. McMahon Glynn is hereby asked to publish
any papers in the old External Affairs Depart-
ment relating to the proposed purchase of
the Territory by a syndicate if they be not
destroyed; and Frank Tudor is asked to get to the
last comma of the offer. Australia's first line of
weakness is in its expensive Customs House crying
to foreign goods “welcome” ; its second line of
weakness will be found in any inclination to listen
to syndicates anxious to buy an Empire for a
peanut. I demand the official head of Dr. Gilruth,
without bloodthirstiness, but as earnestly as Marat
demanded 100,000 aristocrat heads for the good of
the country. The proper Government of the Terri-
tory is by a commission of three Australians—pref-
erably Queenslanders, or men with a knowledge of
the North, and I demand that an imported Excel-
lency shall not be reappointed.
