The Lee Government has also discovered
that separate elections must be held for each
seat, even where two vacancies have occurred
at the same time in the same electorate, as
is the case in Denison (Woods’s and Bur-
gess’s). So there are to be five separate
elections for five seats, with the almost
inevitable result that the Liberals will secure
the whole batch, though they are only fairly
entitled to one of the five (Burgess’s). Four
Labor constituencies (quotas) will thus be
disfranchised for a couple of years or so. not
because those constituencies, or quotas, have
changed their minds, but because under the
Speck’s peculiar electoral law, other con-
stituencies. already fully represented in
Parliament, may have a- say in something
which doesn’t in the least concern them.
The British Pantry and the Fight
Everlasting.
The news about the submarine menace
sounds better. Whether John Bull is bag-
ging any considerable number may be
doubtful, but there seems at least reasonable
ground for concluding that he has devised
methods of attack which induce Fritz to
refrain from sticking his nose up from below’
with the original frequency. Still the
figures with reference to reported sinkings
are of very superficial value. If it. w r ere
known how many vessels came within the
torpedo zone in given w’eeks, how many of
these w’ere food ships, what quantity of grub
disappeared, and how much reached shore, it
would be possible to get the hang of the posi-
tion. In the absence of these essential facts
the only thing to rely on is the reported
utterances of Ministers, and the essence of
these is that the position is better than it
was but is still unpleasantly blue. It may
turn out that the effectiveness of the
blockade will turn quite as much upon the
degree of thoroughness with which the food
available is distributed, as upon the smart-
ness of gunners in hitting periscopes. The
Germans hold to the view that there is no
radical cure for the submarine. This may be
so; but organisation on land may go a long way
towards providing a palliative in the mean-
time. For the present, Britain seems to be a
long way more certain of three plain meals a
day than of what is going to occur the day
after to-morrow in the direction of Poland
and over beyond the Carpathians. Possibly
Russia may wake up. Whatever transpires
in the neighborhood of the trenches will be a
much more reliable indication of the Bear’s
doings than conflicting yarns about political
fuses at the capital. If w’hat is occurring to
Von Hindenberg at his Western address
was afflicting him on the other side of the
disturbance he would certainly be a very
worried Hun. That he is getting severely
mauled thereabouts is much less open to
argument than how much more mauling he
can stand. According to Lord Derby the
war is not going to be over until Uncle Sam
hurls as much of himself as can be made
available into the scrum. This may possibly
be an expression of opinion for the special
encouragement of enlistment in Yankeeland,
but it looks a mighty long way nearer
the truth than the forecasts about the
enemy collapsing on or before October 14.
If there are any signs of him collapsing they
are not visible to the naked eye. Aus-
tralians will probably have observed that in
Canada a proposal to reinforce the Canuks
by the selective draft is in the region of
practical polities. Apparently the Canadian
Ministry neither funks its job nor has a
theory that the time to start winning the
war is when some overwhelming disaster
arrives. All of which is an illustration of
the difference between doing something and
blithering about it.
“0.K.”: The series of mutual contradic-
tions between Premier Hughes and his
former colleague, Mahon, has resulted in an
interesting revelation concerning enemy
aliens. A year ago, as now appears, Robert
G ARRAN drafted three supplementary War
Precautions Regulations inimical to enemy
aliens in their business dealings, or industrial
. pursuits. The regulations provided that the
consent of the Attorney-General must, be
obtained before certain kinds of legal actions
could be taken in court, thus: —
1. Any action upon a contract for the sale or
delivery of goods which was certified by the Attorney-
General to be, in his opinion, unfair by reason of
the defendant having entered into any agreement or
undertaking with the Imperial Government or the
Munitions Board.
2. Any action upon anything said or done, imply-
ing that the plaintiff was an enemy subject or a
person of enemy origin or association, etc.
3. Any action against an employer or association
for refusal to employ or associate with the plaintiff,
on the grounds that he was or had been an enemy
subject.
When these and three other regulations were
put before the Cabinet four of them were
promptly approved and passed into law. But
Mahon told Garran that the Cabinet had
held up items 2 and 3 for further considera-
tion. One member, or more, of the Govern-
ment objected to any obstacle being put in
the legal way of an alleged enemy subject. 01
person of enemy alien connection, who had
been called a German over the back fence
or had been refused employment on the
ground that he, or she, was a German.
This tender regard for the feelings and
interests of Germanised citizens with enemy
alien sympathies has been a matter ior com-
plaint against the Defence Dept, all the
time. What right of action has an alien
disloyalist against an employer or unionist
worker who refuses to associate with him
these times? The Huns at war have robbed
thousands of inoffensive foreigners of their
homes, happiness and liberty and as long
as it isn’t actionable to call a Chinaman a
Chow, or to ostracise him from the indus-
trial circle on account of his nationality. an
Australian prejudice against Huns and their
sympathisers is no reasonable cause for legal
action. At least a suggestion of the
sins of their nation should be visited
upon its children, yet the Australian
Cabinet took a fortnight to make up
its mind that a German disloyalist
had no claim to damages against the em-
ployer or union that might refuse _to let
him mutter his hymn of hate in their com-
pany. The revelation is not sufficiently
explicit.
“Jacko” : The belated admission of Minister
for Education James that the N. S. Wales
continuation-school system has shown itself
a failure, that “those who most need a con-
tinuance of education do not come within its
influence” is cheering, I rejoice too that he has
awakened to the fact that “after the war
Australia will have to compete on industrial
and intellectual lines with the rest of the
world.” This wheels the whole subject on
to the operating-table. James wants to
know why only a mere 2000 of the 10,000
16-years-and-under youngsters who leave
school annually do not attend the continua-
tion classes? The Department itself is
large!}' to blame. While at school the kid
is indiscriminately treated to all kinds of
intellectual fancy goods, and when he has to
start work he finds that he has a lot of
unsaleable knowledge in stock. So he has
to start his education which he thought he
had finished. It takes an exceptional
youngster to plod along to a continuation
school at night; the ordinary boy or girl
shirks it, and so remains half-educated. The
remedy lies within the Department itself.
Let it take its primary and secondary cur-
ricula down and substitute new _ ideas
in tune with the altered conditions.
Since the typical school-kid is permitted
to leave his studies for any blind-
alley job. I would suggest thht he be
encouraged in any direction which suits his
temperament and talents. The Minister says
that his Department is “contemplating
insistence on pupils leaving school being
placed in avocations that would fit
them for life.” Let him give up contempla-
tion of the impracticable and start right
away with the grafting of the junior and
senior technical college courses on to the
primary and high schools, and permit the
kids to divide their time between the prac-
tical and the academic, with the wasteful
cramming in things that don’t matter cut
out. That’s all we need to give the young-
sters a chance of becoming valuable Aus-
tralians.
“Viator” : The last hundred miles of the
East-West line are now being laid over lime-
stone plain, straight as an arrow. There are
330 miles without a curve —the longest
straight run on earth. When the last spike
has been driven, the Overland will move
from Augusta to Kalgoorlie in 24 hours;
Adelaide to Augusta in 13 hours; Kalgoorlie
to Perth in 16. At the Augusta end the
line is already bringing in revenue from
cattle traffic.
The late Louis Ellis, formerly Sheriff of
Melbourne, went to Melbourne from Sydney
in ’46, when the settlement by the Yarra
belonged to N. S. Wales, and was generally
referred to as “the chief town of Port
Phillip.” Ellis’s quarters were at the corner
of Collins and Market streets, and Johnny
Fawkner’s paper was published next door in
Collins-street. Over the way, in Market-
street, were the stocks; and Ellis used to tell
how he had seen two men fixed up there.
His first billet was in the office of Duerdin,
the solicitor, whose son was one of the
founders of the Yorick Club. Ellis’s connec-
tion with the sheriff’s office began in ’52,
when he became a junior clerk, and two
years later he became Deputy Sheriff at 22,
under Claude Farie. He stuck to the rope-
haunted office till ’95, when he retired, after
43 years’ work —three of them as boss. His
wife was the daughter of a Melbourne par-
son, and one of his daughters became Dr.
Constance Ellis.

“London Pride,” introduced to Australia
at Melb. Royal on Saturday, explains its
popularity in its birthplace. It is an idyllic
romance of Cockney life, and its humor is
racy of the soil. The trouble with the pre-
sent performance is that most of the actors
are out of their element. Even Miss Atherton,
who puts all the necessary feeling into the
part of Cherry, is much too refined in her
style for a gal as thinks nothin’ of driving
a donkey cart. She idealises the coster girl
whilst Percy Marmont is presenting a bloke
who is as near to truth as you want it on
the stage. The two principals are admir-
able in the melodramatic scenes more than
in the broadly humorous bits. To some of
the others the spirit of the play is so alto-
gether foreign that the general effect seems
bound to improve with practice, wherefore
further remarks are held over for a week.
“High Jinks,” which practically amounts
to dry drinks in its bearings on the
soul, is having one more revival for
a week at Melb. Majesty’s. The pay-box.
being the supreme judge of good and evil
in managerial enterprise, has decreed that
“Jinks” shall crop up from time to time, like
green pasturage after rain, for an ever-
hungry public to feed upon. It w T as rushed
as usual last Saturday. The notable differ-
ences in the cast from last revival were
Bantock as the long-lost husband of Connie
Ediss. Greene as the occasional visitor
alleged to be a Frenchman, and Maude Fane
as the mademoiselle whose frailty is taken
for granted in accordance with musical-
comedy tradition. Dorothy Brunton, Field
Fisher, Workman, Connie Ediss, Marie Eaton
and others were in their former positions, and
the chorus lent enchantment to the scene as
of old- Next Saturday will begin the last
chapter of the season, entitled “The Three
Twins.”

Young Cuthbort Tunks a Coster was, and golden was
his heart.
’lsself an’ pardner kep’ a store, likewise a donkey-
cart ;
Wif’ coal an’ coke an’ corlyflowers,
An’ uvver fruits wot man dewours,
Y r oung Cuf improved the shining hours.
And Cherry was his tart —
His honest tart.
A sort o’ tenderlike regard for Oufbert Tunks she
bore.
Fair Cherry at a fact’ry worked; a toffy frock she

On Sundays when she useter go
A-teasin’ ’im in Hunter’s Row,
Till 'e went orf to fight the foe—
Yus, ’listed for the wor—
The bloomin’ wor.

The Coster’s moke.
Act 3 found Cufbert on the job, in warfare known

Much better as a fighter than ’e was at torkin’
French;
Then, like an ’ero in a play,
He meets ole pardner Bill one day.
The store’s no more, an’ Bill can’t say
Wot ’appened to the wench —•
The plucky wench.

and brisk,

He wanted leave. He couldn’t wait.
A luckless pal had met his fate;
Cuf bent acrost ’is shattered mate

But Cherry 1 When she shouted “Cuff!”

with corpses is being impersonated on the

c f the gunfighter, or killer, he eventually

\ revival,' after about a decade, of ''The
Marriage of Kitty,” at the Criterion (byd-
nev), on Saturday, proves that our theatri-
cal taste has changed. The comedy seems
thin and tired; the frequency of the solilo-
quies, the paucity of dramatic situations,
and the bluster and dragging burlesque with
which the tiny plot is eked out serves to
show that, even with the help of long inter-
vals, needless, because there are no bars
open, such a production needs an excuse.
That excuse is provided by the first appear-
ance upon the Sydney stage of a distin-
mushed comedy actress, Marie Tempest.
Even so, the choice of such a play, whether
it be the management’s or Marie Tempest s,
is curious.
* * * *
It is a compliment to Australia that so
eminent an English actress should wish to add
to her already almost world-wide triumphs
the applause of this continent; but the com-
pliment is a little frayed at the edges. Hoi-
Mane Tempest, secure in the applause .of
her London worshippers, has taken the i*isk
of unduly delaying her appeal to a new and
therefore critical audience. Candidly speak-
ing, Australia has not been permitted to see
this great actress at the supreme height of
her undoubted powers, nor, so far, has Syd-
ney seen her in a part suited to her
maturity. But, with an equal candor it
may be confidently said that the art of this
comedian is impervious to circumstance, and
that in her Sydney is privileged to see at its
best the difficult art of pure comedy. The
public is apt to put down to an actress what-
ever other assets she possesses, such as youth
or personal charm, as part of her artistry;
but though Marie Tempest has an extremely
individual and picturesque charm of her
own, her art is beyond such mere personal
aids. What the audience at the Criterion
sees is a pert, elfish, brisk, saucy, mischiev-
ous, little Cockney lady, with an infectious
laugh and a delightful personality, and with
a range of gesture, pose and vocal tones that
remain as a revelation. Doubtless as the result
of her long reign in London Miss Tempest
seems to have over-emphasised the nuances
of her voice. There was more subtlety, in
her .conception of the part when this writer
saw the piece first in London. But the
excuse for the play is a perfectly good
excuse: to see Marie Tempest is, for our
audiences and for our actresses, a liberal
education.
* * * *
The excellence of the cast is undoubted.
Graham Browne, as the foolish baronet, is
a perfect English ass, given, perhaps over-
much to burlesque, but a finished and cap-
able actor, and brilliantly shouldering with
the star his share of the finest work in the
comedy. Sydney Stirling provides one of his
most conscientious impersonations, though
he is much too eager to stress his points.
Leslie Victor, who appears under another
name in the programme, but is fortunately
too well known and too individualistic in his
work to hide his identity, presents a part
that is without flaw; and Millie Engler,
as a maid, makes the most of her
sense of comedy. It was almost unkind
to place a young Australian actress like
Gwen Burroughs in contrast with the
perfection of Marie Tempest’s work. The
former, even with the advantage of a fine
presence, betrays the crudity of the novice.
The staging of the interior of the villa at
Lake Geneva is a charming arrangement of
artistic furniture in a beautiful color scheme.
Nellie Stewart, now on the boards at Syd-
ney Tivoli in “Sweet Nell of Old Drury”
(one act per week), has astonished her
fellow-Australians and captured the press.
The fine artist has accomplished a successful
return to the footlights, and the supporting-
company is worthy of her. On Saturday
night Act 11. goes on. Talleur Andrews is
singing on the mixed side of the bill, which
also includes brisk Gwen Lewis and her
piano.
“The House that Jack Built,” at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney), will be demolished by
the management in three weeks’ time. Any
small boy or girl who has not yet been taken
to the show had better begin sapping and
mining operations against the gold reserve at
home.
Gilly with the motor-horn voice and the
cherubic smile is still delighting large audi-
ences by turning to the right at the Palace.
There are rumors that a playwright named
W. Shakspeare is patiently waiting his turn
to appear at the Grand Opera House; but,
judging by popular evidence, William is a
much less important dramatist than Walter
Howard, and must wait off-stage until the
audience begins to tire of “The Story of the
Rosary.”

Adelaide shows have brightened up with
the coming of ‘‘The Cinema Star” to the
Royal, with the old strong cast. The Tivoli
has changed one Andersoniau melo. for
another, “The Man She Loves” being
the thriller of the moment. Of the
Dandies at Queen’s Hall Herbert Walton
seems to be the strong man. The
Majestic has a new team, including Ermakov,
who shoots skilfully. Courtney is a patter
man on the humorous side, and James Dunn
makes strange farmyard noises. The pic-
tures go by players, mostly ; Billy Burke at
West’s, Petrova at Wondergraph, a baroness
at the Pav. The Royal advertised in ambigu-
ous fashion that June Caprice could be seen
undressing for a swim; but this critic’s
baldheaded grandfather, on being sent, to
look into the matter, reported it as a bitter
disappointment.
Harry Hewlett, killed in France, was an
old Sydney Grammar boy who went through
the Gallipoli turmoil with the A.I.F. from
Landing to Evacuation. He was given a
commission with the Suffolk regiment, and
had been a fortnight in Flanders when the
Last Post sounded.
The honorary Jay Pay still does all he
knows to improve the morals of Melbourne
and push along the movement for the uplift-
ing of the criminally-inclined. The North-
cote bench, with four J’s.P. sitting, did its
bit the other day. A girl of 16 was lined up
for snaring an umbrella and selling it to a
dealer for one shilling. The honoraries at-
tended to her lapse from grace in short order.
They sent her along for three months’ gaol!
Quite like old times.
“Cr&me-de-Menthe”: I’d like to meet the
Australian sergeant who said “Australian
horseflesh could not stand the English
winter, and had to be substituted by Cana-
dian horses.” My regiment left Aus-
tralia two years ago fully-equipped with
Australian horses, which, after standing
the test in Egypt and suffering only a 5
per cent, loss from sickness (mostly due to
the bad feed), came on to France 12 months
ago. We have struck some of the worst
climatic conditions possible for a horse to
experience, including the coldest winter for
50 years, and a temperature of 7deg. centi-
grade of frost. Besides the ordinary hard
work, we have “trekked” on three occasions
a distance of 108 miles in six days, and had
bad weather every time. For the last four
months we have lived in mud from 6in. to
14in. deep. Yet the number of horses evacu-
ated from “climatic conditions” has been
only 8 per cent, for the 12 months! I have
discussed the Australian horse with British
cavalry officers on many occasions, and the} 7
all assure me that they do their best to
obtain any Australian horses, whether
“riders’ draughts” or “mediums” in prefer-
ence to their own English-bred remounts. If
we leave a horse sick we never get it back.
Did anyone ever hear of a horse-thief taking
anything but the best?
AT POVERTY POINT.
Schubert, the Noo Yark manager, favors
the ultra-effeminate type of young man in
his musical-comedy chorus and office-staff,
and the profesh speaks of them as the most
ladylike body of “guys” in the theatrical
world. When the news of America’s war
movement came to hand a Yankee actor in
Melbourne said he was tickled to death by
the thought of Schubert’s brigade going into
action with bows on their rifles.
“Stargazer”: The several tradesmen who
constructed “Canary Cottage” in the belief
that they were creating a musical farce evi-
dently put their heads together and agreed
upon one thing at least, which was that as
the war could be looked to to produce an
extraordinary shortage of marriageable men,
tin; competition for those remaining would in-
evitably be keen and crudely barbaric, so the
action in “Canary Cottage” must reflect this
condition of things with something approach-
ing brutal directness. No man in Canary
cottage makes any particular love to any
individual woman, till the last few moments
necessitate a clearing up of. the mess; but
throughout the women lavish direct, un-
veiled and often red-hot realistic passion on
the men. The hero is beset by three women,
none of whom cares to employ restrained or
indirect methods, while one hurls her ample
self at him, openly, unabashed and
even clamorous. Her reiterated appeals and
assaults, with the very thinnest pretence of
humor, are to me fearful and wonderful con-
sidered as amusement offered to a civilised
audience presumedly sane and allegedly
sober.
May Martin Wirth, of the Australian
circus family, has been astonishing Chicago
at the great Coliseum, where they clear four
stages and two rings when it is time
for the dashing little horsewoman to skip in
for her act. The average daily attend-
ance at the giant showplace is between 10,000
and 12.000," and the Australian girl is star
of the long bill.
“Nil”: —-
When Hubert Henry Davies makes out in his play,
“Outcast,” that Miriam became a prostitute because
she was “seduced” and “left with a baby” (B.
10/5/T7), I beg to ask “Whaffor?” It is a useful
literary superstition (and dramatic ditto) that when
a young woman “goes wrong” she is thereupon cast
out by her stony-hearted parents to die with her
chee-ild in the gutter. As a matter of fact, this
seldom occurs outside melodrama. I have had
amongst my patients scores of unmarried girls who
have “fallen.” And so far from becoming harlots
as a “necessary consequence,” they have faced the
music and been cared for by their parents and the
State, become mothers, reared their children care-
fully, and often married and been good housewives.
One does not want to encourage or condone extra
marital sexual relations, but the stage fiction that
such condemn a girl to prostitution or starvation is
a vicious lie. About 90 p.c. of prostitutes are such
from choice and inclination—in-born sexual perver-
sity. Of course, it goes without saying that parents
should teach their daughters (and sons) sexual con-
tinence.
The allusions in the Gilbert-and-Sullivan
operas frequently trip up young critics
who write in haste. New York Sun lately,
talking of W. L>. Howells, remarked that
“Patience” “irreverently coupled his name
with that of Henry James, while parodying
a type of young man of the period—the type
of the young man they were supposed
to use as characters in their stories.” Of
course Gilbert’s line, “A Howell and James
young man,” referred to a popular London
drapery house, and meant simply a young
blood of the shopman tyne. Neither Henry
James nor W. D. Howells was well enough
known in London in ’Bl to be worth poking
fun at from the stage.
“SMas”: In the course of one of the musi-
cal comedies trundled upon the stage in Mel-
bourne by the “So Long Letty” collection
a large batch of young ladies came squealing
and capering on the stage, dressed in a
choice selection of nighties, scattered from
their beds by a false alarm of tire rung in
unblushingly for the purpose of introducing
a chorus in full bedgear. A great “Oh-h-h!”
of alarm and startled modesty went up from
the audience on each evening and obviously
everybody thought the display shocking in
the extreme. The peculiar feature of all this
lies in the fact that for the first time during
the evening the damsels in the nightdresses
were fully and adequately clad. We are
approaching that extreme when clothes of
any sort on a comic opera or revue chorus
will be considered distinctly improper. “So
suggestive, my dear!”
“E.T.”: A librettist in England has made
a comic-opera book out of Julius Knight’s
and the late Lewis Waller’s old stand-by
“Monsieur Beaucaire.” It is shortly to be
staged in London with music by Messager,
who composed “Veronique,” “The Little
Michus” and a dozen other tuneful scores.
“R.”; Marie Tempest is reported by Mel-
bourne Herald to have read plays jpy the
cartload in England and is unable to get one
to suit hex'. If “The Marriage of Kitty” is
regarded by her as suitable she should be
able to get plays by the ton. The plot is
but an Anglicised and attenuated extension
of the dilemma of Ko Ko, Yum Yum and
Nanki Poo, and the construction is as loose
as it could be made. The juvenile and the
lawyer tell each other all the things they
knew before, on the lines of those old dramas
whose stage furniture was limited to two
chairs upon which one or two characters sat
to tell the stories of their lives and then got
off so that two others could tell the stories
of their lives. Melbourne papers told me
that “Miss Tempest is a finished with-
out a flaw. Mr. Graham Browne is an artist
in every sense of the word. They present
the perfection of art” ; and in so doing, Melb.
papers, from any point of view, talked piffle.
I never before saw people with such a news-
paper reputation so disappointing. Marie
Tempest at her best reminded me of Emma
Temple and at her worst a small edition of
Connie Ediss.
In the new revue at the Palace (London)
there is no Scene One at all. It begins
with Scene Two, “so as not to embarrass
any Smart people who have inadvertently
arrived in time for the opening.”
“Masetto”: Melbourne’s oldest continu-
ous show place, the Temperance Hall, cele-
brated its 75th anniversary recently, Johnny
Gardiner, an old-time minstrel, being the
chief notability on the bill. The old con-
cei’t room has heard some good stuff in its
time. Fred Ireland on the piano, Nick
Feuillade with his violin, Edward Amery the
basso, “old Jack” Matlock, Harry Allnutt
and others earned much applause at “The
Temps,” and some proud vaudevillains who
wouldn’t own to it now got their first laughs
there.
“Cockaigne”: “Flayer’s” account of the
late Harold Ashton’s stirring appeal to the
sub. on behalf of the “thin” show (B.
10/5/T7) l’eminds me of Oxenford, drama-
tist and accomplished man of letters who was
appointed to do the theatres for London
Times, under editor Delane. The period was
not ripe for fi-ee and enlightened criticism,
and when managers came complaining to
Delane, he said wearily to Oxenford, “I
know what you write is quite true, but I
cannot be bothered with these fellows.” He
was never bothered any more. Oxenford
l-eached down the syrup.

tralia, is the lessee. Brieux’s play is creat-

“8.J.” : “Blanky” in the mouth of Pshaw’s
Eliza Doolittle caused a sensation, but its
presence in a new Bull play, “Home On

“First Old Man”: “Outcast,” now
being played at Sydney Criterion re-
introduces after the lapse of a good
few years the name of Hubert Ileni-y
Davies to Australian playbills. We have
seen nothing from the pen of the neat and
elegant English dramatist since “Cousin
Kate” was shown here in 1904, or there-
abouts. Though perfervid amateur admii-ers
oft wi'estle with the polite comedy scenes of
his “Mrs. Gori’inge’s Necklace” and “The
Mollusc,” “Outcast” is Davies’ latest, but
the piece, like all the London pre-war plays,
may safely be classed as out of date.

THE PROBLEM SOLVED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There are said to be several small theatrical com-
panies doing baekblocks districts in Victoria and
N. S. Wales who gladly accept payment in the
shape of eggs. The rates are: Front seats, one
dozen; back seats, half a dozen. The eggs are
packed in boxes and sent to the nearest profitable
market.
We were a melancholy band,
Towed round the smalls behind a prad.
In halls and barns about the land
We gave the yobs “The Fatal Hand.’’
We slept in beds when biz was glad,
And ate the horse-feed when ’twas bad.
One time we showed a little pride,
And stood to “Best Seats Half-a-crown.’’
Those times admittance was denied
To any fellow who applied
With less than sixpence, money down;
These times we curtsey to a “brown.”
Of late our gifted management
Has solved one trying problem more.
Once was when eggs of doubtful scent
On us unhappy pros, were spent.
Grown wise with trials long and sore,
These nights we take them at the door!
VICTORIA.

PENFOLD'S
WINES
A soldier-lecturer ;it Casino (N.S.W.)
gave a sample of how the rank and file of
the German army are mere automatons. A
llun officer and two privates were captured
and brought to the base. They were given
cups of tea. but the privates made no attempt
to drink, bn inquiry it was discovered that
no German soldier may quaff in the presence
of his officer. Billjim urged the pair, and
they were about to take a mouthful when
the swashbuckler seized the two cups and
hurled them and their contents in the faces
of the men. Both of them immediately stood
to attention with the utmost precision and
saluted. The assaulting party was promptly
ordered into irons, but before this was done
two glasses of beer were produced and
handed to the privates to drink in front of
the bully, while he stood with a couple of
bayonets thrust in uncomfortable proximity
of his ribs. Then he was put in hardware
without any unnecessary tender touches.
BUSINESS AS USUAL.
MISTRESS: “Do you intend to continue permitting my husband to hiss you?”
MAID: “Not without a reasonable increase, Ma'am!”
In the February Cokxhill Ella G. Sykes,
an Englishwoman with the Y.M.C.A. in
France, awards Billjim. the Chesterfield
Cross for deportment. She puts it nicely
thus: —
Australians and ilaorilaliders invaded our hut and
we women took to them at once. As a rule tnej
were splendid-looking men, with clear-cut features,
frank eyes and beautiful teeth. The hut manners of
the newcomers were beyond reproach, were
always addressed as "madam” or “lady,” and
rewarded with a salute and a beaming smile if we
did any little extra thing for our customers. *-el-
tainly this charm of manner was in strong contrast
to many a Tommy or Jock, who gave their ordeia
curtly and imperatively, with never a "please or
“thank you.” We never had a rude word or look
while in the hut, nor did a single mail ever swear in
our hearing.
There was some more, but that will do for
one helping.
“J.R.”: "O.K.’s” idea of limited mon-
archies for Central Europe without titled
aristocrapies (B. 10/5/T7) wouldn’t work
after the war. None of the States, big or
little, are fit to take care of themselves and
very few kings will be plying for hire. When
the old-time baron had the “right of pit and
gallows” and could lawfully imprison or
Jiang anyone who came on his land he was
a standing nuisance. Now he is merely a
sitting one in the House of Lords.
“E.F.” : More importance than is evident
on tin* surface of the horror attaches to the
German corpse-factory question. The fact
is important that the first stories of this
essentially Ilunic device for turning the
‘‘cannon fodder’’ into cattle food and machine
oil were flatly contradicted in tin* llun Par-
liament. Fritz of authority professing scorn
arid indignation, declared that so absurd a
yarn should never have been passed as
“highly probable” by Lord Robert Cecil, who
subsequently remarked that the official Ger-
man contradiction didn’t amount to a row
of pins. The yarn was thus resolved into a
test of Fritz’s truthfulness. Now it is stated
that a German military order, picked up on
the West Front, confirms the ghoulish story
beyond all possible doubt whatever. The
order requires full particulars of identity,
and date and cause of death, to be supplied
with each bundle of dead meat that is forth-
coming for “corpse-utilisation” purposes.
German casualty-lists, as regards all non-
commissioned stiffs, are presumably checked
at the factory, and one supposes that a
widow who keeps a pig is allowed a piece of
factory output to help fatten the animal
with. She never can tell hut what her late
husband is in this way making liis last final
contribution to the support of the. family.
* * * ♦
From somewhere near the Canal
Here we are on the brink of Hades. Away from
civilisation, away from the bright lights, the good
times and the great feeds. From the beforementioued
joys we have descended —no, been violently thrown
down to a place where the flies eat you during the
day and the fleas keep you awake at night, and
where the queue of soldiers waiting at the door of
our solitary beer canteen sometimes measures some-
thing like a quarter of a mile, along a white powdery
road which winds like a gigantic snake across a
wilderness of arid brown, scorching sand, bounded
on all sides by a sky of dazzling blue, and showing
no signs of life excepting those long strings of
camels, which writhe across its breast like huge
centipedes.
It’s sand, all sand. here. Sand that, reflecting the
blinding rays of an intense sun, sears and blisters
HIS EMANCIPATION.
HE: “So you reject me! Hang it, I’ll go
straight away and enlist."
SHE: “How lovely! You always said I
would make a man of you."
the face, and boils the eyes, and cracks the lips and
parches the throat. Sand which, when the simoon
comes, is whirled into the air in tons, and forms a
pall so dense that you can hardly see your hand in
front of you.
The magnitude of one's thirst is nothing short of
marvellous. From the fabulous sums offered for a
modest “pint” a single barrel of beer would, 1 think,
just about set up - quite comfortably an enterprising
publican for the rest of his days.
Our daily programme: Reveille, 5.30 a.m.; fall in
and water and feed up. Three horses are given to
each man to water and feed. Breakfast, 6.30 a.m.
“Water and feed” goes again at a quarter to 12.
Dinner comes on at 12.30, and consists of tea, bread
and jam, and whatever you may have had the money
to purchase at the canteen. Breakfast consists of
porridge, tea, ham or bacon. A little sugar—a very
little sugar—is mixed with the porridge, or burgoo.
After dinner, parade at 2.30 p.m., groom our
horses and clean up. Water and feed again at
5 p.m., then tea at 5.30. Tea is tea and stew.
After tea, if you haven’t to go on piquet, you can
do what you like—go to bed or spend your money
at the canteen. Every week we are given an issue
of tobacco and cigarettes.
With all the sand and flies the life is healthy. Full
dress here means a short pair of pants, a thin cotton
singlet without any arms, a hat and a pair of shoes.
We’re as brown as the niggers, but not a quarter as
dirty.
* * * *
“Petifi”: A word with the N. S. Wales
Cabinet while it is swinging the,rope. A 19-
year-old boy in Auckland (M.L.) was awhile
ago sentenced to a long stretch in
gaol for attempted murder. It tran-
spired that years back he fell on his head,
and it was theorised that his homicidal ten-
dencies were due to a resultant pressure on
the brain. An operation was conducted, and
proved that the pressure was there all right,
and now the doctors seem confident that it
having been removed the patient will be a
normal man in future. It makes you wonder
how many unlucky wretches in the past have
been dragged to the hangman when what
they really required was a visit from the
surgeon.
“Country Ped”: I’d admire to know where
“Peg-Leg” (B. 3/5/T7) got his information
that there is a cinematograph in nearly all
Victorian country schools. Not four per
cent, have them—town or country. The
position is this: In a few country districts
the committees of three or four schools
clubbed together and bought a “home” outfit
for the joint use of their schools, and since
war broke out many male teachers have pur-
chased a similar machine, and now tour their
districts earning money for the State
Schools' Patriotic Fund, which amounts to
over £170,000.

what our troops did on

shows that our line
extended as far south
as that headland. I
know that “Trooper
Bluegum” used to mis-
leadingly head his

but I will swear that
not one line did he
ever write there. (I
h a p p e n e d to know
where his dugout out
was —a good mile from
where he fancied he
was when writing.)
On one occasion a
small detachment of a
Q’land Batt. made an
attempt to get a foot-
ing on this stronghold
by approaching it in
boats under cover of
dar kn ess, but the
attempt failed utterly.
I can safely say that,
after that, there were
only two men, myself
and my patrol mate,
who ever got any-
where near the old
fort, and I can assure
you that we did not
advertise our presence
there, either. Our
nearest lines, at any
time, were a good 1500 yards from Gaba
Tope.

THE MEETING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I said three years ago
My heart would stir and glow
If I lay dead
And o’er my head
A hair of yours should blow.
This morning, passing you,
1 said, “How do?”
Your praises once I strung
Like shining pearls. You wrung
From every sense
Such eloquence
As tipped with gold my tongue.
To-day 1 jerked my head.
“How do?” 1 said.
Hereft of warmth and light
The world was then all night,
If on the grace
Of your pale fact*
You dropped a veil so slight.
This morning met we two.
I said: “How do?”
VICTORIA.

“F.”: It’s a better sign when the parsons
begin to look intelligently at the venereal
disease horror. That some of them are doing
so was evidenced at the recent Sydney Pres-
byterian Assembly, where one delegate awoke
to the fact that “the time had come for the
church to depart from the attitude of regard-
ing infection as a punishment for sin, inas-
much as there were so many innocent suf-
ferers” ; another pilot advocated education
in sex hygiene; and one other opined that
every applicant for marriage, male or female,
should be compelled by law to produce a
clean health bill. If these opinions epito-
mised the corporate view of the Assembly
when it finally resolved “to urge the Govern-
ment to at once deal drastically with the
scoui’ge” it looks as if the kirk is shedding
some of the cobwebs.

“The Oldest Inhabitant” adds a postscript
to C. H. Bertie’s printed story of Yaucluse
(B. 10/5/T7), the picturesque old estate
just inside Sydney Heads: —
It was John Jamison’s father, Dr. Tom Jamison,
that Henry Hayes, elopement specialist, fell tail of.
Dr. Tom went to England as a witness for Johnston
on the court-martial, and died soon after. The son,
John Jamison, came out to see to the father’s estate
and effects. The estate included a grant on the
Nepean, where the son built Regentville and enter-
tained lavishly. On one occasion he gave a fete to
which he invited 500 guests, and that was long
before the railways. He owned a big patch of Syd-
ney in George-street, which in 1830 he cut up,
Jamison-street being his southern boundary. He had
a fine town mansion, in which, in 1835, the Bank of
Australasia commenced business. The knighthood in
the first instance was bestowed by the King of
Sweden, when Surgeon John Jamison cured the Queen
of some distemper. The Prince Regent gave the
souvenir an English endorsement for services on hoard
a war vessel during an epidemic. The holder died
in the first ’4o's, and is buried in the Anglican
Church graveyard at Penrith. lie was early em-
ployer of llenry Parkes (1840) —£80 a year and
rations. Parkes put in six months at Regentvilie,
and his .description of the knight’s accommodation
for his laborers is not edifying.
A lot of pedagogic persons in Australia,
ever ready to draw a wide distinction between
learned German professors and common
lluus, are advised to read an article pub-
lished in a Berlin magazine by Prof. Oswald
Flamm. He complains that too many crews
of sunken neutral vessels are being saved
from drowning by squeamish sons of the
German sea. He reckons that if all neutral
ships were submarined without leaving a
trace of their fate, sailors would be too
terror-stricken to venture into the “blockaded
zone.” Flamm is a significant fact in Ger-
man philosophy.
“Denny Mooney” : Being
what is commonly termed
“a wounded hero,” I decided
to apply for one of the
soft, heroic snaps reserved
for same. On presenting
myself at a large bank I
was asked my age, height,
length of service, character
on discharge, jobs since
leaving school, religion
(whatever that had to do
with it) and Dad’s posi-
tion. I thought I had the
Grand Inquisitor silenced
when I told him Dad had
been with the same firm
for 15 years. But he came
back with: “What did he
do before that?” I shook
my head wearily, and he
frowned and made a note
of the shake before inquir-
ing feverishly about the old
man’s nationality. I badly
wanted to tell him that he
was a Pekinese Scotchman,
but restrained myself in
time, and said simply:
“Irish!” The Inquisitor
then looked over the mass
of information he had
wrung from me and
droned: “We will write
you in a couple of days’
time.” He was as good as
his word. In a couple of
days I got a polite note to
say that the bank had no
position to offer me. Per-
haps I wasn’t , wounded
enough. Or I might get
them to reconsider me by
having an ear off and
changing my religion.
Perth has its smelling-salts to its
nose and its pipe in full blast. When the
sewage system was introduced a few years
ago the Government engineers, regardless of
public protests, insisted bn placing the filter
beds on the river at Burswood Island (not
two-and-a-half miles from the Town Ilall)
and the septic tanks at East Perth just
opposite. From there the effluent flows past
the city and on through the 'loveliest reaches
of the Swan to the sea. Things have never
been entirely satisfactory, and they’re not
improving. The other day a representative
deputation waited on the Minister in charge,
James (“Moo-cow”) Mitchell, and told him
that the stench from the septic tanks was
appalling, that East Perth could stand it no
longer, and that something would have to be
hedged and quoted authorities to prove the
done immediately. “Moo-cow” demurred,
flower-like innocuousness of sewage effluent
in general, but he promised to have the pre-
sent system perfected. The deputation im-
patiently waved its pipe and smelling-salts.
What the people want is the purification of
the river and a sewage farm well in the
background, and before long they’ll get it or
suffocate in the attempt.
A mighty strong case of falsifying income-
tax returns was sheeted home to one of
William Hughes’s compatriots recently.
John Williams, managing director of the
Clayton Tinplate Co., and director of other
trading concerns in Wales, was found guilty
of faking the returns of profit of the tin-
plate business, as well as of his private
income, whereby the revenue had been
defrauded of nearly £3OOO. Williams was
a J.P., and a shining light in local religious
and political parties. The Judge at the
Glamorgan Assizes, referring to the prisoner
as a communicant member of the dissenting
Church, called him the meanest of hounds
and fined him £4OOO, with an additional con-
tribution of £lOO towards the cost of prose-
cution. The fine was promptly paid. The
moral is open to interpretation.
“Inky” ; Crucifixion is carried out all right
in the Australian army. I’ve been there.
But it’s nothing to worry about much. You
only get it (in the A.1.F., I mean) for some-
thing pretty hot, or for a stolen good time
that’s worth it. In my case we had hard
luck. Amiens was out of bounds after cer-
tain hours, but some of us used to go in
every evening - on board a passing Tommy
motor. Great big motors, these. VVe used
to catchy them home again, in the dead of
night. You could never see anybody in them,
as the lads were sleeping and happy at the
bottom. One night, however, they were all
singing as we rolled into the village where
we were billeted. The lot were bagged, and
some of us went from “the carpet” to behind
the barb-wire. After that there was no singing.
They tie you up for two hours a dav. and
some men are quite used to it. Of course
it is rotten; but most hardcase Billjims who
actually undergo “crucifixion” don’t give a
sou for it —when it is over.

“Third Signaller”: The boys were seated
at their camp mess-tables digging and hack-
ing- at a paving compound supposed to be
fig pudding. One youth who had been doing-
something in the carpentering line was
using a saw to carve his portion. In
marched the prim English orderly-officer.
“Any complaints?” rasped the rawboned
sergeant-major. “Any complaints at this
table?” The pudding-carpenter slowly
straightened his back. “No complaints, only
the saw’s a bit on the blunt side, old
man.” The horrified Englishman passed that
table afterwards.

“Eighteen-Pounder”: The Australian
Army Pay Corps, consisting of men not fit
for active service, were given a uniform con-
sisting of A.I.F. khaki tunic and long
khaki trousers—usually named “slacks,” to
distinguish them from other units. Many
of the men, however, discarded the slacks
for various reasons and wore “mounted” or
infantry trousers instead. This came to the
notice of the officer commanding, and out
came an order: “To N.C.O.’s of the Aus-
tralian Army Pay Corps. It has come to
my knowledge that N.C.O.’s are not wearing
their trousers. This must cease at once.”
“Billy T.”: A female relation of mine
went on a sea-trip for her nerves recently.
Her greatest comfort was a sable coat
valued at GO guineas. At one Australian
port where the boat was tethered to a pier
the lady trotted below to read a batch of
letters she had just received. She had pre-
viously left the 00-guiuea sable on her bunk.
Sitting reading she discovered a draught, and
hastily scrambled into the beloved coat. Pre-
sently, while absorbed in news of home, she
felt a tug at her shoulder, and turped
sharply expecting to find someone. There
was nobody in the cabin but herself, and
she resumed her reading. Again came
the tug, very emphatic, and not to be denied,
and even as the frightened woman half-
turned it came again. A man of the boat
had been killed in this port on the previous
trip, and the lady thought in a flash that the
slain man had come back to haunt the
boat. Before she could summon her energies
to scream, the tug at her shoulder was re-
peated, a sharp, petulant, prolonged pull.
Then my dear friend screamed. She
was a white-faced, shrieking, half-demented

creature when assistance rushed pell-
mell into the cabin. But there was
no foolish delusion, there had been in-
sistent tugging at the lady, and by nobody
in the cabin. A bent wire nail was found
stuck firmly in the back of the sable coat,

been frustrated by the return of the owner
to her cabin and her act of putting on the
garment before the narty lingering below saw
a chance to haul in his catch.

SLEEPY HEAD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When mother blows the candle out,
Beyond the window-bars,
I watch a gentle lady sew
Her tapestry of stars.
And though I cannot see her draw
Her shining needle through,
What jolly patterns she does make
Of silver, rose and blue 1
So cunningly her long thread moves
With sparkle, loop and twist,
And in the darkest spots she puts
Stitches of strange white mist.
I wonder if I stayed awake
Till midnight, in some place
She’d lift the sky a tiny scrap,
And let me see her face.
But when I waken up again,
’Tis morning, bright and cold,
While on the window sits the sun,
And blinks his eyes of gold.
QUEENSLAND.
A. GORE-JONES.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Dr. J. B. Cleland, the N. S. Wales Royal
Society’s new president, was born in 1878.
and is the eldest son of Dr. W. L. Cleland,
of S. Aus., and a grandson of the late John
Hill Burton, D.C.L., Historiographer-Royal
of Scotland. After getting his M.D. ticket
he.spent some years in England and devoted
his time to cancer research at London Hos-
pital. Later, he was Government Patho-
logist and. Bacteriologist in Westralia and
soon made a name by discovering the try-
panosome of surra in some imported camels.
Previous to that no one suspected that the
part of India from which the smelly animals
came was infested by the deadly stock dis-
ease. In 1908 he was appointed Principal
Assistant Microbiologist at the Sydney
Microbiological Bureau, and when that in-
stitution and the Health Board were amalga-
mated in 1913 he became Principal Micro.,
etc. For some years, in company with divers
scientific gents., he has been on the trail
of the life-history of one Onchocerca gibsoni
—an elusive character blamed for causing
worm-nests in cattle.
Tasmania’s second Victoria Cross winner,
Captain P. H. Cherry, of the 26th Battalion,
died on the field where the honor was won.
He had attained previously the M.C. Every
officer in his company of stormers had met
with a casualty, but he drove the enemy out
of the village he was ordered to take, organ-
ising machine-gun and bomb-parties as re-
quired. He beat back counter-attack after
counter-attack, and, though wounded next
morning, he stuck to his post until he was
killed by a shell. Cherry was an orchardist
and a champion - fruitcase-maker of his
State at 19. Enljsting before he was 20, he
became drill-instructor at Claremont camp.

Too young for a commission, he left as
Q.M.S., and rose from regimental-sergeant-
major to second lieutenant on Gallipoli. He
was invalided to Egypt, went to France with
a machine-gun corps, and was wounded and
mentioned in dispatches at Pozieres. After

captain. He was 22.
Louis Ellis, chiefly notable in Melbourne
as being the pai’ent of Constance Ellis, one
of Victoria’s pioneer woman-doctors, is dead
at Malvern. Nearly a quarter-century ago
Ellis retired from the job of sheriff, which
he had held down for the previous quarter-
century, during which time he had the pro-
fessional distinction of making the neces-
sary arrangements for swinging Ned Kelly
off. He stopped at 84. He used to say that
Nature must have meant him for a shei’iff,
for, when he was only 13 years of age, he
wagged it from school to see John Knatch-
bull. or “Fitz,” hanged in Sydney for the
murder of widow Jameson.

W. D. L. Macdonald. Maoriland’s Minis-
ter for Agriculture, lately a visitor to Aus-
tralia for the purpose of acquiring wheat
in the event of the local cocky omitting to
grow, enough, drifted into politics as a mild
sort of Liberal, He hails from the Bay of
Plenty, and some of his constituents reside
in the vicinity of boiling lakes and agitated
volcanoes and geysers which shoot up in the
middle of the night and fall down next
morning. All this fills Macdonald with
a great trust in the expediency of not being
in a hurry. He is a rotund, placid person,
who positively refuses to move out of a
walk, and decorates the Ministry with appro-
priateness. What he can never understand
is why the other chap keeps on making a
row day after day, when life would be so
much more pleasant if he only kept quiet.
George Mclntosh, dead at Chatswood
(N.S.W.), was born in the same district
83 years ago. and for most of the inter-
vening time he had been expounding the
Word according to Anglicanism. Two
curacies in Sydney and a spell as a
parson near Newcastle were his only
lengthy absences from the Cumberland fruit-
growing area. Mclntosh retired from active
soul-saving 10 years ago, when stationed at
Pennant Hills. Two daughters are in China
carrying the glad news to Ah Sin. A son
is wearing a captain’s stars with the A.I.F.
An interesting figure in politics will be
Colonel Jim Rowell —everyone calls him
“Jim” —who has got into the Senate at the
first attempt. Gardening was his .profession,
but so’diering his hobby, and he worked up
to the highest position South Australia could
give to an amateur warrior. He saw blood
under Kitchener in South Africa, but was
considered just too old (though he does not
look it) for anything but transport work in
this war. His son Sydney was the first S.A.
cadet into Duntroon, and his nephew, Col.
Frank Rowell, died on service in the Medi-
teri’anean.
“R.P.”: Justice a’Beckett, of Victoria,
once put J. L. Purves to sleep. The terror
had been pulled up and tried to hit back with,
“Of course, if your Honor, from your exalted
position, chooses to accuse me of misleading
the Court....” “Let us get on, gentlemen,
with the disputed facts of the case,” was the
rejoinder with just the slightest emphasis on
“disputed.” J.L. got on.
Killed at Pozieres another of the heroic
band of the Lone Pine lads —Sergeant
C. G. Nankivell, of the 4th (N.S.W.)
Battalion, whose sistex-, Joiee Nankivell,
secretary to Dr. “Bones” Deeper, master
of Trinity College, Melb. _ University,
has written a number of stirring patriotic
verses which should some day reach book-
form. Nankivell, says one of his officers,
“was endowed with that great quality,
bravei-y, to a degree seldom possessed.” A
Neerim, Gippsland, boy, he enlisted in Syd-
ney, took part in the Landing, was pro-
moted and mentioned for rescuing wounded
under fire, and was decorated with the Mili-
tary Medal. He was mentioned for further
awards in France when the end came.
“Billy Blue”: Apropos of Walter O.
Hawkins, the old-time parson (B. 10/5/’l7).
The roll call of State-aid parsons, or chaplains,
as they were first called, is now reduced to
the last member, the Rev. Septimus Hunger-
ford. Born in 1825, he came to_N. S. Wales
in 1828. He was ordained in 1853 by
Bishop Tyrrell. His first parish was the
whole of New England, with his headquar-
ters at Armidale. From there he went to
St. Thomas’s. Enfield (Sydney), and retired
in 1895. The “roll” was established by
Governor Bourke’s Church Act of 1836, and
thereunder parsons were paid £lOO to £2OO
a year, according to the number of narish-
ioners. The Act was abolished in 1863 by
Charles Cowper, then Premier, who was
the son of one of the oldest parsons paid
under the Act.
Sculptor James White has a commission
from Melbourne Hospital to do a marble
bust of John Grice for the institution, to
which Grice, like his late partner Somner,
has been a very good friend. The
model, in soft clay, or glyptokos, is a con-
vincing likeness of the rather starchy Mel-
bourne citizen; and White is confident that
he will get even more life and character into
the work when he finishes it in the, stone,
two or three months hence.
“Nestor”: Down in the Bismarck Archi-
pelago a while ago I met one of the most
charmingly simple Old World gentlemen it
has been my good fortune to fore-
gather with. On the wall of his house
was a painting of the late Alfred
Stephen. “Sort of grand-uncle,” he said.
My friend is the offspring of one of
the Stephens’s and a French wife. At the
time when boys want to go to sea this youth
went. He was run down to the Islands, and
left on Nauru, to be picked up when the
ship came back. She never came back, and
the lad was marooned there for two years
before another ship showed up. This was a
whaler booked for America, but by that time
the ex-sailor didn’t want a passage. He
married and settled, raised a family. That
was befoi’e he knew that on his little 12-mile
round patch was £250,000,000 worth of the
best phosphate stuff.
_ “Rustler”: -One of the most versatile poli-
ticians in the Victorian Assembly is Achiles
Gray, member for Ivorong. Before entering
Parliament he for a time managed the huge
estate of Gray Brothers, and, later, practised
as a successful veterinary-surgeon. As an
entertainer he is excellent, especially shining
as a humorous reciter. Whilst lying in bed
with a broken limb he invented an apparatus
which is largely used where rain-water has
to be stored in tanks. It provides for the
first water from the roof, which is dirty, to
be run away, and the clean water to be
turned into the holders.

“Catchline”; A genius for mateship is
possessed by Jim Carey, 32, swagman, whose
watchword seems to be “somewheres
anigh my chum.” He was rejected 13
times, after trying to enlist in every
State in the Commonwealth, and figur-
ing that something must be wrong he
underwent an operation and tried the recruit-
ing-office again. All correct this time. Carey
has humped his drum all over Australia, and
has been away with remounts between times.

Died of wounds in France, A. T. B. Bru-
ford, lance-corporal—one of five brothers in
the A.I.F. who are great-nephews of the late
Field-Marshal Roberts. Their grandfather,
Archdeacon Beamish, also deceased, married
Roberts’s sister, and presided over Anglican
collection-plates in Victoria for many years.
Bruford was born in Victoria 29 years ago,
and was in the Bank of Australasia at
Mirboo North (Vic.) before enlisting.
Sergeant Leslie M. Menser, who lately
died of wounds in France, learned the goose-
step with the old 2nd N. S. Wales Volunteer
Infantry Regiment, and was wounded while
serving with the Australian Bushmen in the
Boer scrap. Afterwards he was a mine
manager for the S.R.C. Exploration Co.,
Transvaal, and when Kaiser Bill started out
on the road he’s surely going, Menser joined
the Heidelberg commando of General Botha’s
South African Mounted Division as ser-
geant-major.
Trainees of Captain Eric Harrison, officer
in charge of the Vic. Flying School at Point
Cook, have done justice to his teaching and
that of Captain 11. Petre, who was for-
merly associated with him. Petre has won
the Military Cross in Mesopotamia, and has
been twice mentioned in dispatches; Lieut.
F. H. McNamara, now in Palestine, has been
recommended for the V.C.; Lieut. Murray
Jones has the Military Cross, and Sergeant
C. V. Heath the Distinguished Conduct
medal.
Passed out recently, George Langhorne
Lethbridge, 90. He was a native of Parra-
matta, educated at the local King’s School
and at Mount Radford College, Exeter. Eng-
land. He returned to N.S.W. in 1845, and
had some experience on his father’s proper-
ties in Singleton and Barraba districts. At
Currangandi, on the Horton River, he lived
for 30 years in a bark hut. In 1854 he set
out, with others, on an exploring expedition,
as far as Peake Range, being the third out
after Leichhardt. For 40 years he lived at
Bridgeman, near Singleton, where he was
a noted breeder of stock, particularly sheep.
He never married.
Lieutenant H. .T. Dell has been mentioned
as. putting up something like a record in
f|uick promotion. He enlisted in Launces-
ton as a private last October and has won
his star already. His great grandfather was
the Sergeant Dell who was sent by Colonel
Patterson with a party to explore the upper
reaches of the Tamar in 1806.
Amongst those who exercised the fran-
chise at the second ballot for the Hawkes-
bury was Mrs. James Sullivan, senior, of
Wilberforce, aged 90 years. She was one of
the oldest Hawkesbury natives, and lived to
see the fifth generation of her family. The
ballot was taken on April 14, and on Mon-
day, April 24, the old lady passed out, leav-
ing 140 descendants.
POOR COLUMBINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Blue is for happiness, they say,
Pierrot wore blue when he strode away
To fight for France.
Blue is for tenderness, they say,
The sky was blue on that sunny day,
So bright his lance.
Blue is for truth, they say,
Bless Thou the truth, Bon Dieu, I pray,
Send down Thy glance 1
■ Blue is the Virgin’s cloak, they say,
For blue means tenderness. Oh, may
She pity Franee 1
Blue were his laughing eyes, amd gay,
Shot through the heart, he lies, they say,
In Western France I

What a money-maker it is! Six years ago
the capital was £300,000. Then, the visible
reserves having exceeded the capital,
£350,000 of ’ the undivided profit was
capitalised, and later on bonuses amounting
to £02,500 were paid. That left, the share-
holders £257,500 to provide in order to make
up the present capital of £1,000,000. The
idea, when the capital was more than
doubled by the issue of bonus shares, was
to reduce the rate of dividend from 10 to a
less conspicuous 5 p.c. The very conser-
vative management hinted that it wouldn’t
consider it was doing anything out of the
way if it laid by as much as it paid out in
dividends. But, alas! this ambition has not
been realised ; for while the directors have
enriched the shareholders by £357,500, the
visible additions to reserves have amounted
to only £345,350. In six years the divi-
dends have thus exceeded the lay-by by over
£12,000. When the directors think over it
they may realise how reckless they were in
pushing the dividend up from 5 to 7| p.c.
Still they can plead that, so far as last year
is concerned, they were well under flhe mark
and let go only 48 p.c. of the profits. And
even then they represented 5 p.c. as divi-
dend and 2£ p.c, as bonus, thus giving a sort
of hint that the 2| p.c. mustn’t be regarded
as a permanent institution.
And, in view of contingencies, they are
right. The world is in a state of flux, and
almost- anything may come out of the
crucible. Nobody knows what is going to
happen in the Pacific Ocean, where, in spite
of all the missionaries, “Beepy” is better
known than John the Baptist. So, too, trade
with the North—our North, running up
through Java and Singapore to the busy tin-
dredging Malay States and on to Japan—is
very uncertain. Our trade that way ought
to be enormous. We should find the best
market for most of our products there. The
hundreds of millions of people up yonder are
not all the very low-wage people they were
a few years ago. They are developing new
tastes —tastes for white man’s tucker in
particular—and they are demanding and
getting much higher wages in consequence.
Thus far Australia has not done a quarter
as much as it might to collar this new trade.
Into a camp in one of the Malay States
there came at Christmas half a dozen or so
hams, all the gifts! - of Chinese having busi-
ness relations with that particular co. All
those hams were English—and Australia is
only a hop, step, and a jump away. How-
ever, even though we are neglecting our
opportunities, the Northern trade has in-
creased since the war; and either directly
or as agent B.P. has benefited very largely.
No doubt, also, though it hasn’t been easy
to get Islands produce away, the disap-
pearance of the German flag from the
Pacific has helped to build up "the magnifi-
cent profit of £161.466 for the year. That
Profit, by the way, is exclusive of £lO,OOO
which is put by to pay the staff a 10 p.c.
bonus, “payment to be made as far as
practicable in Commonwealth War Bonds.”
During the year most of the figures have
grown. The position as at March 31 was:—

‘'Other assets” consist mainly of copra, etc.,
on hand and shipping, consignment and
other advances. Extension of the co.’s field
of operations, coupled with difficulties of
ocean transport, would account for increases
in this line and in merchandise stocks. The
co.’s liabilities have been more or less
correspondingly increased. The co.. owing
to the war, has been unable to arrange for
the. building of any new steamers; but to
assist in the collection of Island produce —-a
business in which the German had got a big
say—the co. has placed contracts for a num-
ber of wooden auxiliary yessels; alid Uncle
Samuel. should have three of them ready
inside six months. Later on, according to
present appearances, there will be a tight
tor the trade in which B.P. is chiefly
engaged. But if the far-seeing, vigorous, yet
extremely economical B.P. can't hold it for
Australia nobody can.

Tooths put all the summer into one half-year
and all the winter into another; and that
makes the profit figures take almost as curly
a course down the table as the highway fol-
lows from Taree to Gloucester. The next
comparison, which deals only with the sum-
mer half of each year, -and doesn’t take heed
of special appropriations, gives a clearer
view:—•
Profit on Management, Balance
Brewings, Wages, Excise, of
Mar., etc. etc. Profit.
1913 .. £326,966 £176,035 £150,931
1914 .. 373,807 191,745 182,062
1915 .. 381,861 193,088 188,773
1916 .. 296,227 182,455 113,772
1917 . . 267,071 180,885 80,186
It doesn’t look good; and peace when it
comes is not likely to restore things to their
old level. “The effect of six o’clock closing,”
the directors say, “lias been to considerably
reduce the sales of bulk beer; but the in-
creased sales of bottled beer have to some
extent lessened the deficiency.” Also: “The
co. has suffered considerable loss owing to
the reduction of rents under the provisions
of the Liquor (Amendment) Act 1916.” The
poor old war was used as a stalking-horse to
bag the six o’clock bird, and the present law
will prevail only “during the currency of the
war. and for a period not exceeding six
months thereafter.” But the majority in
favor of six o’clock closing was so over-
whelming that there is practically no hope of
a reversal of the decision under any condi-
tions. In fact, we shall probably feel such a
pinch after the war that there may be a
bigger vote in favor of six o’clock ‘closing
then than there was when war-money was
plentiful. War or peace, the world
seems to be tightening its grip on the throat
of the liquor trade. In the old days of easy
peace there were three local-option votes
the N. S. Wales Act of 1905. In
on™’ wlien the first w as taken, there were
3023 pubs in the State. The first vote
wiped out 293; the second, 28; the third, 23.
It is a remarkable pendulum that doesn’t
swing both ways; and to most movements
there is a reaction. But there doesn’t appear
to be any in. this liquor business. The
closure of one pub seems only to make it
easier to close another. So, in America the
arrival of prohibition in one State seems
only to make it easier' to turn a neighbor
dry, until now there are either 23 or 24
publess members of the Union. What the
end is going to'be in Australia this paper
declines to guess. It is quite certain, how-
ever that the trade can be worked in a
much more intelligent and economical way
than it has been in the past. This co’s
management apparently recognises this • for
there is a directors’ note: “The plant will
be entirely modernised by the various addi-
tions to the buildings and alterations to
plant and machinery which are now nearing
completion.” It is to its costs that the wise
brewery co. will proceed to direct its atten-
tion.
Tooths, at any rate, stand in an apparently
strong position to face the probably hostile
future. The principal balance-sheet items
have moved this way in 10 years:—
Mar., ’O7. Mar., 'l7.

The balance-sheet contains a note to the
effect that none of the capital is represented
by goodwill. Bht many a co. has part of its
capital represented by an unrecognised form
of goodwill. Thus “Breweries, etc.,
£i 5 538,795,” includes the value of pub pro-
perty ; and it is quite possible that a good
deal of the book value of some of the pubs
is represented by nothing tangible—that is
to say, is goodwill. The position may there-
fore not be so good as it looks on the surface.

For some years past it. has been doubtful
whether the co. wouldn’t have >to change an
old-established custom, and the last 12
months’ experience doesn’t lessen the doubt.
To make £15,134 and pay away £24,590 in-
volves altogether too deep a cut into the
funds. Actually 'the co. now has smaller
resources in capital and reserves than it had
seven years ago. True, the past year has
been more unfavorable than the average.
Partly on account of the large number of
city fires for which the I.WAV. is blamed,
and partly because of the exceptional hail-
storms. in the N. S. Wales wheat districts,
the.co. had a loss rate of very nearly 50
p.c. But though that is much above the
average, the loss rate is in general far higher
than it was in the early days of the co.,
when with a very small capital the manage-
ment felt justified in taking only very
selected risks. Nowadays the co. is an office
to take risks and not to turn them away, so
that in the past 10 years the premium in-
come has just doubled. It took the co. 33
years to get a premium income of the first
£36,000; the second £36,000 has -been got in
The rule of the co. is to pay, first, a divi-
dend of 8 p.c., and then to distribute 20 p.c.
of the premiums as a bonus, half to the
policyholders and half to the shareholders.
But of late the business has grown so
rapidly that, whereas 10 p.c. of the pre-
miums meant a dividend of 5£ p.c. in
1911, it means now a dividend of 9§ p.c.;
and this 9§ p.c. is paid notwithstanding
that the profits were much smaller last year
than they were in 1911, when the bonus
based on premiums meant a dividend of only
5i p.c. Plainly, then, this basis of distri-
bution is all wrong. It is not the turnover
that should count, but the profit on the turn-
over. Consider these figures -

In the three years 4 895-1900-1905 the under-
writing profit averaged 35.8 p.c. of the pre-
miums. In the past three years it has aver-
aged only 13.6 p.c. Yet the same proportion
of the premiums has been paid away
throughout; and the proportion has been so
high that the co. lias not even been reserving
its interest earnings. There is one thing to
be said in extenuation of the present exces-
sive distributions: owing to the compara-
tively slow growth of the business in former
days, coupled with the comparatively large
amounts then laid by, the funds are more
than sufficient to cover present risks. The
net investments and bank balances are equal
to 320'p.c. of last year’s premium income-
and this is approximately twice as large a
ratio as that of the average British office.
Ihe co. thus offers excellent security for
people with risks to place ; and it is an Aus-
tralian office, every inch of it, with a prior
claim therefore on any Australian business
that is offering.

The directors put it quite frankly—“a con-
servative valuation of stock shows a profit
of £16,268.” Stock and work in progress
represent no less than £97,756; and it
depends upon the valuation of these assets
whether the profit is much or little. Though
the past year’s figures are much better than
those for the year before, the dividend was
still not fully earned; and it does not seen!
likely that there will be an early recovery.
The co. had a very fine run to start with,
and in its eight years has paid 102| p.c. ill
divs., besides accumulating a handy reserve,
But most of those were years of heavy pub-
lic borrowing and general inflation, and the
co. was in the way of benefiting very largely
from the construction boom. The Uext
eight years are much more likely to be d
period of sackcloth and ashes.
One cannot, of course, predict anything
with certainty, for very much depends Upoxi
the price we shall get for our staple products.
At the best, however, this paper cannot
see buoyant conditions ahead; for apart
altogether from the vast sums of borrowed
money which the Commonwealth has spent
on non-productive enterprises—including the
war the new works on which N. S. Wales
lias spent £40,000,000 during the last few
years are not at all likely to at
once carry their own interest burden;
and that means a heavy load of taxa-
tion. This is going to affect the spirit
of the community. And unless this
co. can find now outlets for its abundant
enterprise it is likely to he Bit pretty hard.
The directors realise that, apparently ; for
after referring to the “declining business and
keen competition,” they say they are “alive
to the importance of securing fresh avenues
for the employment of capital as it is re-
leased by the realisation of stock which
cannot be replaced at reasonable figures.”
Financially, the co. is in a very easy posi-
tion. Its liabilities, including £lO,OOO of a
mortgage, total only £18,981. Of the co.’s
total resources, £54,500 is in premises and
plant, leaving £170,482 —or a trifle more
than the capital—represented by stock, work
in progress, cash and investments, the last
consisting of shares in Automatic Tele-
phones, Ltd., and Lift Inspection and Insur-
ance, Ltd.

Block 10 is one of the very few Broken Hill
cos. that never put up a smelting plant. The
South, Block 14, British, even the Junction,
ran furnaces at one time or another. Block
10 always relied on custom works ; so when
the war started it was in the soup. The old
market for its concentrates being lost, its
mill was closed down from August, 1014, to
May, 1916. That explains the raggedness of
the bottom-end of the foregoing table. Even
before that, though, results were rather
uneven, as they generally are with a mine
which has to potter about looking for ore.
Certainly this co. can see a couple of hun-
dred thousand tons or so ahead : but some of
it isn’t easily got at. Probably a lot more
than 200,000 tons will be raked out; any-
how. the directors have thought it well worth
while to put up a flotation plant to treat the
tailings from the ore that is left in this mine
and in Block 14. The latter co. couldn’t see
quite enough ore to justify the erection of a
milling plant of its own; so Block 10 is'to
take its output at the rate of 1000 tons per
week. Block 10 will thus be kept busy for
the next five to seven years, provided nothing
dreadful happens to the metal markets, and
that isn’t likely; after that it will, on pre-
sent appearances, .have to scratch for a
living.
Seeing the end approaching, and having
saved up a tidy lump of cash, the directors
for a good many years looked around for a
new mine, but got hold of nothing that pro-
mised well till Misima (Papua) came along.
Block 10 has 69,666 shares out of 150,000 in
that co.; it got this interest for £11,200
spent on the mine and £30,000 put to work-

That’s better, but not as much better as it
should be, considering the price of lead, silver
and zinc. The co. has been one of the least
fortunate of Broken Hill main-line proposi-
tions. The mine is at the dislocated north
end of the lode, and a few years ago, when
it was made clear that it could have only a
very short run, its management decided to
try to prolong the co.’s life by going in for
ore-treatment. Young Tom Palmer, the g.m.,
had got round him a staff of very enterpris-
ing Australian youngsters, and they unques-
tionably found out far more than all the rest
of the world knew about slime treatment.
The co. was therefore induced to purchase
large quantities of slime from the mills of
other cos., and it is mainly out of this pro-
duct that the last half-year’s profit lias been
made.
During the term 46,979 tons of slime and
4178 tons of crude ore were treated, as com-
pared with 33,540 tons of slime in the pre-
ceding half-year. The co. sold 7073 tons lead
concentrates and 10,875 tons zinc concen-
trates ; and this has enabled it to substan-
tially reduce its debt for purchased slinjc.
At March 31 its position was : Cash, £18,685 ;
concentrates and slime, £96,746; debtors and
sundries, £45,199; total, £160,630. Liabili-
ties, £38,215. That shows a surplus of
£122,415, apart from whatever the mine may
be worth and whatever profit there may be in
the untreated slime.
It will be noted that the greater part of the
profit made during the past half-year has
been written off. Most of this was very
doubtful profit: £11,476 of it was spent on
the opening up of the mine. The balance is
debited to plant and mine maintenance. The
true profit may be less even than £8331,
because the working life of the plant will
probably be very short. The machinery mav
be good enough, but it is doubtful whether
the co. will be able to buy slime for treat-
ment. Every other co. now knows as much
about slime-treatment as the Junction North
does; and there is no essential thing or pro-
cess which is the exclusive property of that
Company. Still, though there are no
bright prospects ahead, the chances of share-
holders getting a little of their money back
fair The co. is in 180,000 shares paid
to 2os. 3d. The market, however, refuses to
value the shares at half a sovereign, or
£90,000 for the lot. Which seems a pretty
serious reflection on the management, seeing
™t fjre co. shows a present surplus of
±122,416. It says, in plain words: “You
addition to the mine and plant.
±1_w,416 now; but we can trust you to waste
or lose £30,000 of it before it reaches the
shareholders.”. And, on past experience, the
market is entitled to take a gloomy view of
what it is likely to get from the Junction
North.
* * * *
The Sulphide Corporation is the most pro-
gressive of all the Broken Hill cos., not ex-
cluding even the B. 11. Prop., which has put
a million or two into steel works. Twenty
years ago the Sulphide people tried to estab-
lish the zinc-making industry in Australia;
but they got no help from the politicians of
that day, and the attempt had to be aban-
doned. However, they were good patriots—
Ivmtore, a former Governor of S. Aus., was
chairman—and, dissatisfied with the flow of
the zinc-smelting business to Belgium and
other parts of the Continent, they promoted
a co. to smelt their ore in England, offering
a guarantee for 10 years. The co. has
throughout given a hearing to every new
idea in metal treatment. In the hunt for
means, to reduce costs and improve extrac-
tions it was lately introduced to a process,
the invention of two young Australian engi-
neers, Seale and Shellshear, for agitating
and aerating pulped ores without mechanical
stirring. The flotation process, upon which
the Broken Hill cos. depend for the separa-
tion of the metals after the first crop of
silver-lead has been taken out, is now pretty
generally understood. It consists of froth-
ing up the finely pulped ore and introducing
tar, oil and acid to the mixture —the tar and
oil to take away the lead ; the oil and acid
to float off the zinc. To froth the ore it has
hitherto been stirred ; and that costs money.
The new idea, first developed at the B. H.
Junction North, is most appropriately called
the cascade process, the pulp, with the oil
and acid introduced, being aerated or frothed
by falling, cascade fashion, through a series
of nozzle-shaped iron pipes, from one box
to another. The system has already proved
itself, and besides making as good a recovery
as was possible with the mechanical stirrers,
effects a great saving in power and in wear
and tear, which means costs.
Practically all the Broken Hill mines are
now making experiments in aeration ; and,
outside the Barrier, Wallaroo and Moonta
has decided to cut out the mechanical agita-
tors ; is going into figures on
the question; and Minerals Separation, the
co. which owns the patents covering the
original process arjd the improvements, has
sent A. L. Court to the U.S.A. to experiment
on the copper ores there.
As reported a month or so back. Broken
Hill is simmering again, and the pot boiled
over at the Junction North last week. There
was the usual dispute over the pay of a
handful of men, and this co.’s slime-treat-
ment plant had to be shut down. There is
worse coming—mainly because of the persist-
ent weakness of almost every department of
Government administration on the Barrier.

So far as the market is concerned, Bodan-
gora (N.S.W.) is quite out in the cold be-
cause crushings gradually got poorer. The
