The two Adelong (N.S.W.) shows are
threatening to dip into better ground. Last
week’s return of the big show was 212f0z.
This gives more than a decent margin of
profit on which to start dividend-building.
Next, the Adelong Creek’s No. 2 plant puts
in a 104 oz. record, so that it looks as if it
may have at last dodged into the strip of
rich stuff for which it has been angling for
months. If No. 1 could be but on to the
better bores higher up the creek the chances
are that it would soon cease to be a dead
weight; but even consulting-engineer Moline
can’t arrange a “tank”-like flit. Possibly
that is why Gordon Lyon has disappeared
from the Melbourne Block to reappear at
Adelong.
Tyrconne] North Co., which lost money in
North Queensland, has gone to a spot
beyond Laverton (W.A.) to try for a re-
covery. The show that it has got hold of
on appro, is stated to have a 4ft. reef worth
an ounce, and to have other formations
from which several thousand pounds have
been gouged in the past. The locality is one
of rich patches and many pitfalls.
Hansel Mundy, alias Chaffinch, has
thrown up its option at Goongarrie (W.A.)'.
The Board wanted an extension of time, but

The hopelessness of long-winded geologi-
cal reports and the absurdity of sending
along a geologist to an outback leaseholder
to tell him that his slate wall is amygda-
loidal schist lias been realised by Minister
for Mines Robinson (W.A.). Instructions
have already been issued to put into under-
standable words all geological literature
issued by the Department, and many other
matters interesting to the prospector, whose
dying breath is generally a curse against
Mines Department standoffishness, are re-
ceiving attention.
At present water is. the subject of most
importance to the various cos. at Westonia
(W.A.). Edna May Co. has an elaborate
electric pumping-plant installed at the 300 ft.
level, from which Central, Deeps and Con-
solidated say they receive little if any
benefit. It is all a difficult matter to prove
in a legal way, but some day the Edna May
pumps may cease working, and in that event
adjoining cos. will have it demonstrated to
them that the Edna May pumps have been
of great assistance indeed. Meanwhile argu-
ment might be suspended.
* * * *
Edna May, Centrals and Deeps are all
crosscutting at various levels for the pay
streak, and as each strike occurs the market
will respond in a manner dangerous to the
uneducated. The innocents have had a bad
time with Westonia stocks recently.
* * * *
Commodore, Meekatharra (W.A.), is to
have a double-barrelled management. F. L.
Bell, one time under Mararoa Henderson,
steps into dead Parramore’s shoes, and V.
Hawkins, of the Fenian, becomes consulting
engineer. The Board also provides a Cd.
call as a war-time luxury. Why? Is it
because the battery tests of the Macquarie
lease don’t tally with the sampling?
James Hebbard, of the Sulphide Corpora-
tion’s B.H. Central mine, has been let off
the chain to run the rule over the New Langi
Logan, Ararat (Vic.). It isn’t his first visit
there, and he figures as one of the biggest
holders in the show ; and if rumor isn’t lying,
there is a big bale of Langi scrip lying in
the cupboard of the Gibbs Bright crowd,
who are the Australian agents for the Sul-
phide Corp. Hebbard . gets on to the Langi
just in time; for although the staff has been
pulled off development work and shot into
the stopes in order to stave off calls, the
results have been poor. The truth is that
even the 2oz. dirt in the Cathcart Central
lead can’t be mined at a profit if it has to
be yanked out as wet as a sponge. As the.
co. has only about 2500 ft. of this lead to
sweeten up the Langi • gutter dirt, it is
madness to mine the dirt so green for the
sake of escaping a few calls. Hebbard doesn’t
want for pluck; so he will probably tell
them to go in for hard development work
instead of board-room mining. The big strain
on the pumps at No. 2 shaft ought to be eased
so as to get the water away from the Cath-
cart gutter, and the Langi lead ought to be
crossed from the southern end of the. No. 1
shaft workings to cut off the water from
No. 2 pumps. That, too, would give an idea
of the gold right across the big lead. The
last outsiders who tried their bands at
reporting on deep-lead shows came from the
Hoover-Moreing camp, and were led by
Waldemar Lindgren, the big Yankee geolo-
gist. Even the exceptionally brainy Hoover
could not make a do of alluvial work. So
Hebbard will be shouldering a heap of
responsibility if he tries to go beyond the
solid engineering job of setting out a work-
ing programme for a wet, untried mine.
Louis Hansen, formerly manager of
Birthday Tunnel (Yic.), has been pegging
out in Ballarat West and E. G. Vaudrey
has taken up an end-on section. Old John
Leishman, George Buchanan, W. Dalrymple
and astute little Arty \Jckery are suspected
of being the Fatmen behind the pair. The
Welkin reef goes through the two blocks,
and if a decent prospect is panned off this
team has more than a pound or two to put
into the ground—or the market. In the
dim, misty past a lot of pigrooting on the
same line failed to unearth anything much,
so Ballarat is exceedingly languid over the
new intrusion. But, then, Ballarat is lan-
guid nowadays over everything. A few of
the more wideawake want the Plateau shows
amalgamated, and' are looking for a Teddy
Dyason to do it; but it would take an Edison
to galvanise that district, even if a fusion
could be arranged.
On Cloncurry (Q.), Cuthbert, Elliott and
Corella have all blown in, and Cuthbert’s
157 tons of blister from 2079 tons ore isn’t
too bad, for it is nearly S p.c. Corella is
employing three shifts, and the smelter should
get a good deal of customs ore, for there are
many small shows on the river. Elliott will
draw- ore from Hampden Consols, the Great
Australia and the Dobbin, and the bottom
levels generally show a fine width of stone.
The Great Australia was a famous surface
mine in the early ’seventies: it produced
oxides and native copper in rich pockets, and
the more-recently discovered low-grade calcite
and dolomite will come in handy as flux. The
Dobbin is developed from 200 ft. to 380 ft. in
depth, and some wide stopes of medium-
grade are available.
* * * *
Corella copper, Cloncurry (Q.), has taken
up an ironstone lease for fluxing purposes.
There are always a number of fossickers in
the Corella district, and a handy smelting
base near the river is certain of a supply of
customs .ore if returning charges * are
moderate. The Rosebud mine doesn’t con-
tain large reserves, and ore left in the upper
levels is difficult to get at. Fortunately the
Corella district contains a number of surface
shows, and the co. has—so far as its finances
permit—adopted a similar policy to Hampden
and Mount Elliott in securing options. The
Sunset, Better Land and Jubilee copper
shows are said to offer attractive prospects.
*.* * *
Ore going 1.05 p.c. tin has meant bread
and scrape at the Mt. Bischoff Extended for
many a long day. Hence the cry of joy over
the white metal moving to £250 per ton; for
if the parity of that is available here, the
co. may surprise us all with a div. Last six
months’ working was a close shave, for it
took £10,516 to earn £10,898. The reason
is that the country is as tough as jasper, and
costs can’t then be kept very low when the
shoots are only up to 21 inches wide. The
co. has been using up cash to ram in a new
tunnel, and it rejoices in having got on to
the Haulage lode. The proud Board thinks
mny provide a valuable ore supply.
Well, it will have to improve a lot, for all
it shows now is a dirty face 12 inches wide
in well-defined walls 3ft. apart. This
isnt much to crow over; but John Ditcli-
nurn s little flock is a patient lot.

The U.S.A. Steel Corporation did mar-
vellously in 1910. Gross receipts were
£254,921,900w0rking exes., £184.607,500;
net income, £70,314,400. Against this
£6,746.200 is charged for depreciation, leav-
ing the enormous sum of £63.568,200 avail-
able as profit. And there are others. For
instance, Bethlehem Steel declared a divi-
dend of 30 p.c., and there is an overflow
or. unfilled orders for practically all iron
and steel products, particularly 'shell steel
barbed wire. A few days ago Sir
William Robertson, of the British War
Department, stated that in about five weeks
no less than 200,000 tons of ammunition
had been transported over the railways in
France, and in addition rorne . 50,000 tons
of inetal is required for road-making.

Sydney, May 21, IPI7.
My Dear Akim. —
Dr. Laura Forster, who died from heart-
failure after an influenza attack at Zalesli-
chiki (Galicia), was a daughter of the late
William Forster, who sat in N. S. Wales
Parliament in the early days. He was Pre-
mier for about live months in 1559-GO. Dr.
Laura got her diploma at Berne (Switzer-
land), and early in the Big Scrum joined the
Belgium Field Hospital at Antwerp. Later,
she went to Petrograd, then to the Caucasus
with the Russian Bed Cross, then to Erze-
rum, and finally to her last call at Zales-
chiki.
The Papal Delegate, Archbishop Cerretti,
is packing up his handsome purple millinery
for an early departure to Borne. The
picturesque Italian is going through an ex-
haustive round of farewell parties in con-
sequence. The G.-G. and his lady gave a
good-bye lunch to the traveller at Admiralty
House, and the Tom Daltons, of North Syd-
ney (they belong to the Orange family) gave
a garden party to the prelate on a gorgeous
afternoon.
An American-Australian League ,of Help
is sorting out socks and other comforts
in a fervor of enthusiasm. Associated the
other day, these ladies are rallying
over their knitting needles, to take
counsel as to practical ways of helping their
countrymen in the trenches. The first lot
oi' Uncle Sam’s boys will be in that universal
address by the time Mrs. Brittain (wife of
the U.S.A. Consol) and Mrs. Plogsted, pre-
sident, have rolled up their sleeves for full
action.
Marie Tempest, still wonderfully attrac-
tive in spite of a rather gruff voice and the
years that the locust hath eaten, wears some
fascinating stage-clothes. There’s a pink
confection topped by a gauzy hat of the type
Romney loved to paint. Also, a wisp of
ninon, probably classed as a tea-jacket, that
goes with a froth of lacy skirt. The final
triumph is a pastel-tint made of stuff that
looks like a drapery of thin cloud trimmed
with an insertion of cobweb. In this she
elopes with her husband (I’m telling
you about “The Marriage of Kitty”), cov-
ered by a solid British travelling-coat and a
fearsome hat of tam-o’-shanter descent. The
Criterion was full to the uttermost chair on
the first night. In the manager’s box Kathleen
MacDonell’s red locks shone above a wisp
of green drapery. She was with mother.
One of the Philip Morton girls sat demure
in raven upholstery in the opposite pen, and
contrived to wear the new forehead bandeau
without detracting from her sleek locks. Mrs.
Spencer Brunton, with her eldest boy in
khaki —he has just left the Grammar School
—was in the stalls. Mrs. Charlie Waldron,
the handstome Steer Bowker lady, the
family of Levy, and most of Potts Point’s
diamonds and precious paste were also pre-
sent. Mr. Arthur Allen shepherded his
nice-looking girls, and amongst others were
Mr. Reggie Allen and his lady. Furs and
velvet predominated.
Concerning coiffures, the narrow black
band our Nicest Girls consider necessary to
their beauty these nights is about the most
unsightly decoration of the season. It is
tightly strapped round the forehead; the
hair is dragged well off the alleged intelligence
department, and the effect is, generally speak-
ing, a mixture of early Egyptian hussy’s and
modern chorus lady’s get-up.
Nora Kingston Moore, daughter of a re-
tired naval officer hereabouts, has kindly
accepted an engagement-ring from Sergeant
Groves-Pearce, of the A.I.F. He’s a Dor-
setshire man. Nora and sister Marjorie
have been untiring amongst the entertainers
who at the Rawson Institute liven up Jack’s
shore-leave.
Do you remember Boadicea Monk, the Syd-
ney girl who married Lieut. Stanley Fre-
mantle at Oxford a little while ago? He
got back to the trenches after 10 days’ honey-
moon, and now his wife spends her time cheer-
ing up wounded soldiers in Blighty. Mostly she
drives them about in her car when" the
weather permits. Mrs. Monk, still on the
other side (it’s a good time to stay on dry
land), is ladling out hot soup, etc., in a
Y.M.C.A. canteen, and otherwise looking
after the comfort of munition-workers.
The concert to push the funds of the Good
Shepherd Home (Ashfield) had too many
’•iiil'ty chairs, and that doesn't cheer the
appearance of our bleak Town Hall on a
winter’s night. Kate Rooney charmed those
who attended when she sang “Abide with
Me” (Lilian Frost at the organ). Mrs.
Bertie Kelly, in lilac striped muslin, e-locuted
about Mabel held in a strong embrace while
her young man led her across the “jewy
fields.” Carrie Lanceley’s tuneful soprano,
and reinforcements supplied by Sid Mac-
donald, Marsh Little and Warwick McKen-
zie completed a rather drab programme.
One of the recently-decorated nurses, Miss
Gertrude Fletcher, is a Maorilander who
.was trained in Prince Alfred Hospital, Syd-
ney. She’s had an adventurous career since
she washed-up dishes in the early stages of
her F.A.H. duties. She saw war-service in
the Boer argument. When the row with
Prussia began she was appointed matron of
a war hospital in Devon. Lately she’s been
looking after wounded Atkins while lie’s
being soled and heeled in the big Richmond
. Military Hospital.
Three daughters of Dr. Chappie (now
M.P. for Stirling in the House of Commons)
are doing their whack for wounded on the
other side. Yet it seems only the other day
that they were long-legged kiddies in Wel-
lington (M.L.), and winning prizes at young-
sters’ swimming carnivals.
According to a hard-working Red Grosser
(Mrs. Jim Ashton) the ordinary citizen
doesn’t know what the Male Voluntary Aid
is doing in time. For instance,
when a train goes north to Bris-
bane with returned wounded the Y.A. goes
all the way to look after them. Trained
in First Aid and Home Nursing they
give good service to the sick Kangaroo.
Last week eight men were called up
from the Newtown Voluntaries, to be
on Central Station to take charge of the
Red Cross outfit. Most of these sturdy
helpers are working for their living—yet
they provide their own smart uniforms and
do duty on days that count as holidays in their
employers’ books—to wait on Billzac, or ten-
derly carry the cot cases at Wallangarrn
from one train to another. At Brisbane
Mrs. Price, of North Quay, entertains
the men who are thus able to return
nearly all out-of-pocket expenses the Red
Cross may have advanced. This is about the
first public mention these unostentatious
workers have had during 18 months’ solid
graft. Perhaps it may inspire some of our
leisure-loving club-men to get out of their
arm-chairs on a similar trail.
The Conservatorium held' a big audience
to welcome Alfred Hill as the 1917 conduc-
tor of the Apollo Club. In the long ago it
was the Maorilander who batoned the old
Liedertafel. That was before Germany
turned hog on us. The Amateur Orchestral
Society, which appeared to be very dead at
the end of 1916, is again alive. It joined
melodiously up with the Apollos the other
night to get the best out of David’s Sym-
phonic Ode, “The Desert.” The result was
an admirable ensemble of voice and orchestra.
Newbury, the tenor, had the solo that every
tenor is supposed to dote on—“O Lovely
Night”—and was happier singing it than in
the subsequent chant (or bellow) of the
Muezzin. The second half of the programme
was the brighter for Carrie Lanceley and
Frank Hutchens.. Here’s a May Moore
photo of Hill as he looks to-day.
Humphrey Bishop, a well-known English
baritone, is passing through Sydney on his
way to Java, Japan and India. S’Africa
may be included in the tour. Two Sydney
girls will be auxiliary attractions of the
concerts. One is Marguerite Edwards, a
clever young pianist. The other is Mary
Geary, a contralto. She is remarkable for
her likeness to Nellie Stewart. So you
needn’t be told that she has a mop of golden
curls and a piquant nose. ,
Years ago Fred Leist, then one of
Sydney’s best black-and-white artists, took
stock of his prospects. He didn’t like them,
One day he suddenly confronted a friend and
shouted, “I’ve done it! I’ve scratched
together enough to have some study at the
Academy Julien in Paris. After that, the
deluge if you like!” Then the artist, one
of the best of good fellows, took his smile
and his swag to Paris. When he got to
V ' T ° Poadi)n he had a struggle, but lie
won through, and . his signature began to
appear regularly in the leading English
illustrated publications on work that stood
out fresh and bo d. compared with the more
conventional British artists drawings.
Though the war has hit Leist probably as
hard as it has hit any other of his fellows
iu Chelsea, his cheery smile is iu the
old place. Here is a photo taken just before
he left Sydney, The Bulletin had made
some remarks about the artist’s modesty at
that date. In a little painting of a girl
and a cow, no legs were visible. The lady s
skirts and the grass hid the animal’s limbs, '
so everything was exceedingly proper. Leist
had this nhoto. taken as in the act of paint-
ini? the nietnre
’
The R.A. Society’s rooms were like ant-
beds the other afternoon, when art patrons
surged round to inspect Edward Officer's
pictures. The form of patriotism which
enables the patrons of genius to give, say,
£25 to the Red Cross, and get a good picture
of the Outback. country . (this is Officer’s
strongest work in a varied collection of 61
exhibits) is made feasible through the artist s
generosity. A year s hard work is repre-
sen ted at this show.
Melba bought in 1916 (and returned to
be sold again for the Red Cross) one of the
iinest of the collection, “Plains of the Dar-
ling.” In this, as in all his best efforts, the
Victorian cleverly depicts the golden haze
of a summer day out West, with a tempera-
ture of 110° or so in the shade. In “Even-
ing Light,” snapped up at once, you get the
burning glow of the sunset that beats like,
hot coal. Mrs. Box secured a French
scene. S. F. Mann (Melbourne) is going to
keep “The Banks of the Darling." Mrs.
limes Watt, Mrs. Hebden and Miss Icely
were in the first rush of. buyers. Women
were so keen that it was like a hat sale, till
the V.A.D.’s, Mrs. Maxwell Allan. and Miss
Sliand, with the Red Cross on their pinnies,
straightened things out and booked the names
and addresses of those who felt the call to
unload 10 or 12 guineas to the cause. Lady
Helen opened the show, and A.D.C. Haskett
Smith was the ornament “in attendance."
Sister Baxter, just back with a men-
tioned-in-dispatches to her credit, is likely
to go off again, on a date I daren’t mention.
Besides, I don’t know it.
Eva Mylott, of the Titian thatch and deep
contralto, is about to hand herself over to the
care of one Hutton Gibson, whose address is
Chirago.
The Clarence Bridges, who have three
sons doing service for the Empire, expect
shortly to take the sea-track that leads to
India, where sons Wallace and Lionel are
on frontier duty. Clarence, the third boy,
is back again in home waters, after sundry
years “somewhere” with our navy.
Massed bands will serenade the. earlier
arrivals at the Town Hall this Thursday
evening on the occasion of the concert to
raise money for the Soldiers’ Entertainment
Committee. Mrs. Verbrugghen, Humphrey
Bishop, an English baritone on the wing, and
a backing of Tivoli and J. C. Williamson
talent will make up the evening’s programme.
Good-natured Joe Wolinski, a Sydney
artist who has been in Uncle Sam’s land of
late, recently in New York hauled two small
children out of freezing water into which
they had fallen. One dropped through the
thin ice on the surface, and an older girl
went to his aid. The Australian, painting a
snowy landscape from his adjacent studio
window, rushed to the rescue. That accom-
plished, he carried them home, his clothes
half-frozen on his body. None of the adven-
turers, however, were any the worse for the
dip. Wolinski’s name is down for the Car-
negie medal.
Billy Murdoch, the Australian pianist who
used to play such a rattling good game of
tennis, now makes music in the Grenadier
Guards’ band.
Army Service depot enthusiasts have bor-
rowed two perambulating coffee-stalls (the
real thing, and no mistake!) to attract six-
pences on Red Triangle Day. Mrs. H. Y.
Braddon and Hilda Caro are the boss refresh-
ment vendors, and their sign is: “For every
cup you buy from us our boys in the
trenches will get three from the Y.M.C.A.”
Then the A.S.C. girl will tempt you with a
sandwich. After bolting 46 coffees and ham
you are awarded an Indigestion Cross and
given a free pardon.
The Red Cross is snaring tons of news
papers and magazines by big wire cages at
the Central Station and elsewhere. Outback
station-masters might note. After a long
train journey the yawning traveller clings
to his rug, his suitcase and his hat.
lie’s looking for a bin for his papers, and the
Kangaroo wants them.
"Any old linen to give away?” Some-
where a voice is calling, and I fancy it comes
froth the Town Ilall cellar. That’s where
they make white mice (not the kind that
gobble the wheat) and other surgical ban-
dages for the Front. Anjr_ old sheets, table*
linen and soft rags to bind up Billjim’s
wounds? But don’t send dirt and mildew
in your package.
Have you noticed that our girls’ boots are
lengthening more than ever'this winter? Yet
there’s talk of a shortage in leather!
A Field Artillery reinforcements’ comforts
fund committee did Trojan work selling
tickets for their I'ecent show in St. James’s
Hall. Two lady scouts got rid of about
200. There’s a handsome cheque to come.
The 2nd D.A.C. and 22nd Howitzer com-
mittee expect to bank about £54 as the pro-
fits from their flutter in St. James’s Hall
on April 27.
Red Triangle Day (June 1) heralds its
approach in the shape of huge baskets with
the Y.M.C.A." anneal tied round their middle.
It will take a Tush of local millionaires to
fill these wicker caverns. I’ve seen several
of the receptacles lurking in quiet pi’emises
already, so somebody hopes to catch moun-
tains of cash.
Some of the outlying Red Cross
convalescent homes have had troubles
not anticipated by their generous founders.
One, on the north side of the Harbor,
is likely to be closed in consequence
of a bad element, usually “pommie,”
that has been not infrequently “in-
valided” before it sa.«- J *the firing-line. An
ex-non-com. in the A.I.F. writes in caustic
accents of the men who have done the
damage. “This place is ideal—to the Aus-
tralian soldier. But it’s no good to the
imported crowd, who never knew such luxury
in their lives. Most of them came out as
deck-hands, firemen, cook’s-mates, etc., on
tramps before the war. Then they ’listed.
When their objectionable conduct can no
longer be tolerated even by the gentle
V.A.D.’s, the local bobby has to be sent for
to get them off the premises. We push them
out of the place, but , still hang
about, cadge from local residents, and come
bounding back full of liquor and language.
Then the police-station receives another
urgent call for help. Yet everything here is
as perfect as it can be made hr the ladies
who have for two years put up with this sort
of thing. The decent Australian soldier is
likely to he.nr the front door slam on
all and sundry before long. The commit-
tee is disgusted, and there’s to be a meeting
of subscribers to wind-up this beautiful
haven for sick men.”
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

HOW TO NEUTRALISE DANGEROUS
STOMACH ACIDS.
Few people besides physicians realise the
importance of keeping the food contents
of the stomach free from acid fermentation.
Healthy, normal digestion cannot take place
while the delicate lining of the stomach is
being inflamed and distended by acid and
wind—the results of fermenting food in the
stomach. To secure perfect digestion, fer-
mentation must be stopped or prevented, and
the acid neutralised. For this purpose
physicians usually recommend getting a little
Hsurated magnesia from the chemist and
taking half a teaspoonful in a little hot. or
cold waiter immediately after eating. They
recommend Hsurated magnesia because it is
pleasant to take, has no disagreeable after-
effects, and quickly stops fermentation, neu-
tralises the acid and makes the sour,' acid
food bland, sweet and easily digested.
The regular use of Usurated magnesia—
be sure you get the Usurated, as other kinds
of magnesia are not so valuable—is a guar-
antee of healthy, normal digestion, for it
overcomes and prevents that acid condition
which alone is the cause of trouble. —Advt.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, May 21, 1917.
My Dear Yarrie. —
The Stanley dame headed the big meeting
of the British Red Crossers at the Town
Hall last week to arrange the details of But-
ton Day—May 24. The council room was
packed with important and less im-
portant people. Sympathetic references
were made to the late Vice-President
Felstead. her Ex. feelingly referring to his
loss. She wore a hat trimmed with gold
lace, blue ribbands and pink roses. She
implored the V.A.D.’s to rally round Mrs.
Woolcott. Mrs. George Fairbairn sat at
the council table with a military black tuft
shooting up from her hat, and the Hennessy
dame smiled in pink at the head of the table.
Her Patriotic League was praised in every
direction. Mrs. Woolcott (organiser) looked
serious under a pancake hat of straw. Her
lawyer husband sat at her elbow. Mrs.
Godfrey sat with her daughter and Mrs.
Crevelli was adjacent. Mrs. Sadlier came
from the hardworking Austral Salon. Mrs.
Robins (from the Y.M.C.A. Soldiers’ Home)
was perfectly garbed in blue serge.
The Italian Red Cross is to be helped
forward next month with a big show. Re-
member that there has been more hard fight-
ing on the mountains.
Ada Reeve is making a special appeal for
the Anzac Buffet. She has thrown £lOO of
her own money into the hat, and donors are
invited to send either goods or cash. She
will auction the goods on the stage at a
later date. About £3OOO a year is needed to
keep the indispensable Anzac Buffet going.
The preparations for Ye Olde English
Fayre are exciting. Sassiety women, used
to having their own sweet way, are fretting
at being pinned to historical accuracy in
dress. Quite a dozen dames who want to
strut as Queen Elizabeth are irritated
because the authorities rule old Bess out of
it merely because she was dead at the period
portrayed. The flappers all want to be Puri-
tans, while the women of the Arts
and Crafts are trying to key themselves up to
wear the leather jerkins and pants of crafts-
men of the time.
The Cavaliers who are to ride along the
blocks of the city on Friday, Saturday and
Monday at the beginning of June are grow-
ing in numbers. Dorothy Low and Rene
Murray will lead the procession, and David’s
sister will sprout a fine-pointed red-gold beard
to match her hair and black-velvet masculine
garments. Rene Murray already has shorn
her black hair into cute short ringlets, and
has hobbled a show-horse from the parental
country stables. The Mounted Police are
lending their fine horses for the occasion, so
there should be a good turn out.
The Red Cross Gymkhana at Moonee
Valley on Saturday drew a big crowd. There
was a hunt over three miles of big fences, in
which more than 30 riders and a pack of
hounds started. Miss McCulloch, wearing
grey, was perched on a grey horse. Mrs.
Embling ''was out, also Violet Hiskins and
Eileen Fitzsimmons. George Armstrong wore
one of the two oink coats, and mamma
Melba went proudly into the saddling-paddock
to see him mount. She was encased in a
huge fur coat, high spatted boots and a straw
belltopper. The diminutive Fitzclarence
lady with her wore a cute little tricorner
hat and a flaring overcoat of black cloth,
edged with seal and showing a deep purple
silk lining.
At the beginning of the gallop one of the
Melbourne Hunt Club horses dropped dead
from heart failure, his rider managing to
get clear with a bruised leg. At the back
of the course Sergeant-Major Curran had a
bad spill and injured his spine; and toward
the end Captain Holgate came a cropper,
which looked very serious. He was riding
superbly on a black horse and was well in
the running until he reached the home fence.
The accident brought the Hunt to an abrupt
finish.
Essendon women supplied and sold the
tea and cakes, under the guiding hands of
Mrs. Cole, Mrs. Goldsworthy, Mrs. Mott,
Miss Buller, the Kemp girls and Miss
McGill. They were all very smartly dressed
in black-and-white and pink, and one sweet
girl who found ninepence hard to count said,
“Sixpences for tea are much more easily
counted! Why couldn’t they charge six-
pence ?”
General Williams had a tea-party, and
Dr. Niall and Dr. Murray Morton hosted
others. Mrs. Watt and daughter Mrs.
Bonnie Symons also indulged in the cheer-
ing cup. Allister Clarke did the judging and
a big party of his womenfolk were in the
saddling-paddock.
The Madame Melba Free Kindergarten is
out after help for the winter. A jumble sale
by auction is being arranged for June, and
donations of clean clothes and household
effects will be welcomed at 23 Clifton-street,
Richmond.
Roy Bridges, compiler of the vivid Gallipoli
war-book, “The Immortal Dawn,” modestly
says that Be is not a “soldier-journalist,”
but is simply a journalist who is doing his
best with his pen for the Australian Repatria-
tion Fund. His book—a patriotic labor of love
—is the selected result of other Australians'
first-hand battle impressions as recorded in
diaries and letters, and all the cash pro-
ceeds go to the fund.
Poor little Bertie Manifold, the hope of
the Camperdown family,, has been killed in
France. The rich young warrior bequeathed
£3OOO to the Geelong Grammar School, to
endow scholarships for the sons of Old Boys.
Bertie himself was a rather young Old Boy
of the school.
Tennis champion Norman Brookes has
gone to Mesopotamia to do Red Cross work.
The death of Frank Carse in France has
saddened a very rich and hospitable section
of Toorakese. lie was a grandson of the late
Faulltiner, of Riverina. Mrs. Frank and her
very small baby girl are in England, and
another baby, a two-year-old, is staying with
the grandmother at Fairlie House. Carse
was a lawyer who stood for a seat at the
Federal elections before last. He was wounded
in the Gallipoli fighting, recovered and went
on to the trenches in France.
The dance invitations issued by Margot
Mcßean and Mrs. Frank Brett are can-
celled. The two hostesses are of the
Faulkiner clan, and the death of Frank
Carse has nlunged the whole family into
mourning.
Mrs. Guy Sherwood, back from her travels,
has been staying at the Oriental. The
bereaved lady has been in deep mourning
since the death' of aviator-son Athol. Her
second son, also with the Flying Corps, is
reported missing.
Melbourne lost one of its brilliant boys last
week —Frank McCutchan, a son of the secre-
tary of the Harbor Trust. In the early days
of the great trouble the War Office asked
Australia to send three young engineering
graduates from each of its principal Unis., to
take commissions in the Royal Engineers,
and McCutchan, a Wesley boy, was one of
the Melbourne three. He has been working
in France most of the time since then —and
dreaming of the Australia that he passion-
ately loved. When not engineering he made
hobbies of chess and music.
Dr. Klatchko, the good-looking Russian,
lately returned from Westralia, lingers
on club steps in Collins-street in mufti. Well-
cut grey tweeds have taken the place of his
khaki blouse. Perhaps he'll revert to uni-
form when the Bear ambles forward again.
Rupert Clarke is back in Melbourne,
debonair as usual, but with a limp, a walk-
ing-stick and looking a little greyer. He is
showing off the newest male fashion in
tweeds —a real waist with a full skirt to the
short coat. We’re apparently going to have
a revival of the fashions of Dickens’s youth.
The widow of Bert Armytage is back in
Melbourne with her small girl, and is putting
up at brother-in-law George Chirnside’s
humpy at Werribee. She and the exquisite
Chirnside dame were two of the Watson
girls of Gerogery (N.S.W.).
Lady Susan Fitzclarence, who came from
’Frisco with Melba and is staying at Coombe
Cottage, is the young widow of a soldier
killed in France. She is a handsome little
lady.
Melba had a birthday on Saturday, but
w T ould not celebrate it because of the illness
of' daughter-in-law Mrs. Armstrong, who is
just convalescent enough to travel home to
Coombe Cottage from her Collins-street flat.
However, flowers, greetings and presents
reached the singer at the Conservatorium,
and she lunched with Lady Susan Fitz-
clarence and George Allan.
Adelaide’s dispatch:—
This week has seen the deluge, especially in the
southern suburbs, where incessant rain and violent
hail choked and flooded gutters, piled up ice to Such
an extent that it had to be shovelled off the roofs
of houses, and knocked gardens into chaos. Now
householders are taking stock of their sodden ceilings
and rain-marked walls, and wondering how in the
name of housekeeping they are to get their carpets
dry. Nursery gardens that lay in the track of the
storm have practically ceased to exist.
The aeroplane exploit in Egypt that brought Lieut.
Baillieu his M.C. won the same decoration for
Lieut. R. M. Smith, of this State, the second son
of the Andrew Smiths, of Mutooroo, near Broken
Hill. He enlisted with the Light Horse in October,
1914, and changed from that division to the machine-
guns before he finally joined the Flying Corps. “It
was a fine performance on Baillieu’s part,” he wrote.
“He made a most difficult landing on unknown
ground, and successfully got the machine off again,
with a heavy load.” The 60-mile trip back had to
be made at a maximum height of 500 ft., as the
machine refused to climb any higher. You remem-
ber, of course, that they landed to pick up the pilot
of another ’plane that had come to grief near a big
Turkish camp, and that Abdul was nearly on our
man when they hauled him aboard.
A fair at the Frank Fishers’ house at Brighton
added to the credit balance of the local Trench Com-
forts committee on Saturday. Morris dancers and
a spinning exhibition were its chief side-shows.
Lady Downer, who came back to S.A. some time ago,
and is settled with her small son in North Adelaide,
formally declared the show open. By the way, the
Central Trench Comforts committee of this State
slipped a special box for General Birdwood amongst
its Christmas gifts. The last mail brought the
chairman, Mrs. Lendon, a letter of thanks that spoke
of the “grand and self-sacrificing work done by oui
men,'’ and the “exceedingly trying time” they had
in the winter.
Lieut. J. C. Wright, who had enlisted prior to his
appointment as principal of our School of Art, and
was given leave of absence by the Ministry, has
been killed in action, less than two months after he
over to France. After four years’ work at
the Aberdeen School of Arts, Scotland, he was sent
to the Royal College of Art, London, upon the advice
of such men as Frainpton and Clausen, A.R.A s.
There he specialised in sculpture before going to New
South Wales, where he did excellent work. Other
names in the casualty list are those of Capt. Clive
Burden, who was killed by falling underneath a
train at a London station; and Lieut. G. liiddell,
whose grandfather, James Pearce, was for many
years a member of the S.A. Parliament.
There died at North Adelaide Hospital on Wednes-
day, Mrs. Annie Holt.ze, well known in Port Darwin,
and a daughter-in-law of the ex-Botanic Garden
Holtzes. After her husband’s death, in 1913, she
lived for a time in Adelaide, but afterwards went
back to the Territory as teacher under the Govern-
ment Aborigines Department, which post she held
till her death.
One thousand pounds, invested in 5 per cent, war
loan, has been given to the Walsall Town Council by
that persistent donor E. T. Smith. The interest on the
money is to be used by the Council for Christmas
gifts of clothing, bedding, fuel, etc., to “necessitous
persons over 50 years of age, resident in the borough
of Walsall” (Eng.), Mr. Smith’s native town.
Oliver, one of the Josiah Symon sons who has
chosen the navy as his medium for being unpleasant
to the Hun, has got his lieutenancy.
The idea of a national inter-State highway as an
Anzac memorial is being supported by this State’s
Good Roads Association, and at the next meeting of
that body the matter is to be definitely considered.
Mr. Edwards, Adelaide’s new member in the House
of Assembly, was late for the declaration of the poll
on Monday, being otherwise occupied at the police
court, where a t‘2 fine was entered against him for
“being the licensee of premises out of which a
person was seen proceeding at 11.45 p.m. on April 10.”
However he turned up in time to assure the waiting
electors that it was the one and only time he’d made
the personal acquaintance of the police. life oppo-
nent was a returned Gallipoli man.
Lieut. K. K. Wendt, younger son of the Koeppen
Wendts, who was sent to England to train for his
commission after coming safely through l’ozieres, has
been killed in action. lie rejoined his unit, as a
second-lieutenant only last March.
G. H. Prosser, American Consul in Adelaide for the
last 10 years, and managing-director of Wilkinson
and Co., the provision-dealing firm, has resigned both
posts. He is still a young man, and proposes to give
more time to municipal and political life. He has
worn the rabbit-skin’ for Kensington and Norwood,
and is at present an alderman of the city.
Pretty little pierrette Elsa Langley is
wearing the diamond solitaire of Jack Rox-
burgh, son of the J. W. Roxburgh®, of Laun-
ceston, Tasmania. Elsa Langley is the lady’s
stage-name. She is really Elsa Wilhelmina
Laura Landquest, daughter of the A. G.
Landquests, of Windsor.
The Italians obliged with two concerts
last week, with a full orchestra, which
sawed and rattled willingly until conductor
Gonsalez refused encores to Scamuzzi and
Filippini on the ground that the ladies were
to follow. Miss Gonsalez reached for her
high notes from a sheli-jfink frock. Visioni
wore yellow and black butterflies from her
hair to her toe. De Revers w T as marvel-
lously arrayed in shimmering silver, with
weaths of pink roses. Balboni was refused
an encore at first; but the crowd of devotees
positively howled at the jibbing orchestra
leader until the favorite came back again.
The men were loaded with flowers.
Goll and Verbrugghen have been revelling
in Beethoven’s sojiatas. In the Melba Hall
rapt audiences had their souls ironed and
renovated on Monday, Wednesday and
Friday evenings. Two. and sometimes
three, sonatas are dished each night,
arid no breathing or drinking time is allowed
between courses, if there happens to be more
than one. At the first concert the Ver-
brugghen violin practically drowned the Goll
piano, though Beethoven wrote the works for
piano and violin, not violin and piano. In
the later concerts the harmony was adjusted
to perfection. Musical Melbourne professed
to have enjoyed the feast of its life.
Harrie Ireland, who has had a long spell
of bad health, writes: “It was stated a month
or so back in The Bulletin that I was
getting better under medical treatment.
That’s hardly right —I am being treated by
a metaphysician who has really done wonders
for me.”
“With Palms.” That is the official descrip-
tion of the Order of the Croix de Guerre won
at Mouquet Farm on August 2(j last by Cap-
tain Oscar Anglesey Jones, of the 21st (Vic-
torian) Battalion, since dead. He was 25.
His mother is Mrs. F. Jones r of North Fitz-
roy. He enlisted as private in March, 1915,
and sailed as a corporal in May. At tne
Evacuation he was one of the last to leave
and he was aboard the torpedoed Southland.
His captaincy came to him in France last
December.
Lieutenant Alfred Towers, D.C.M.. M.C.,
who helped to chase De Wet with the Vic-
torian Bushmen’s Contingent, and came home
with three stripes and a crown, went from
W.A. Education Department to take a hand
in this war with the 16th Battalion early in
1916. He won, at Mouquet Farm, three
"mentions,” the congratulations of his
general of division, and the D.C.M. Later
he received a commission and the Military
Cross. He is now at Perham Down's,
England.

ABORIGIN
ALITIES
“Wal”: The experience of “Reefer”
with the unfriendly crocodile at Lakekamu
River reminds me of a similar happening at
Kerepunu (Papua). The recruiting vessel Daisy was
anchored off Kerepunu village one morning, the sea
roaring on the outside reef, the lagoon, where they
were anchored, smooth as glass, mirroring- the grass-
clad hills and palm-fringed beach. The tide was at
the turn, the mud flats in front of the village
being diligently explored by a drove of long-snouted
pariah pigs. A group of naked brown-skinned
urchins rolled and tumbled in perfect happiness in
the warm pools of water near the beach. The dinghy
of the Daisy was being rowed ashore, the Chilian
cook and a Kiwai “boy” sitting on the gunwale
aft. Suddenly a black, wicked-looking head arose
beside the stern of the dinghy, the mouth opened,
and the saw-like teeth closed in a vyce-grip on (lie
Chilian and dragged him overboard and to the mud
bottom. The tragedy was so sudden that the skipper
had not even time to lift his rifle. On another
occasion a croc, tried to climb aboard the Govern-
ment ketch Lokohu, but was settled with a .303
befoie it got much above the bobstay, though the
ciew weie almost terrified out of their lives by the
visit. Many natives have been taken off canoes by
the brutes and pigs galore are lost from the
village by the same, cause. On one day I saw a
paddle snapped out of the hand of a “boy” who
was coming off to the vessel to trade fish, and the
same afternoon had the pleasure of potting a 10ft.
6in. reptile that took it into its head to have a bask
on the mud close by.
The Wellington (M.L.) Zoo at present
has a quartette that have done and seen things: a
tooth-billed pigeon that was. captured after a pre-
liminary bombardment when Samoa was seized;
Osman, the leader of Captain Scott's sledge teams ;
Oscar, the wonder dog who saved the lives of
several members of Shackleton’s expedition; and an
Abyssinian monkey, a M.L. regimental mascot who
went from Egypt to France, was under shellfire at
Armentieres and nearly lost his mess number at the
Somme.
“H.V.E.”: The aristocrat in point of
beauty of coloring among parrot-fish is the member
of the tribe known as pig-fish from its thick, fleshy
lips and somewhat-elongated snout. It is a harmony
in scarlet, brown, pink, orange and pale blue; and
in one variety, at any rate, there is a large rounded
spot on either side o.f intense purple-blue. I have
found young pig-fish three or four inches in length
swimming in rock-pools in company with baby
rainbow fish in purple, yellow and pink bands. The
pig-fish is taken occasionally on rocky grounds on
the X. S. Wales coast in fairly deep water, but
doesn’t come into the market in quantity.
* * * *
“Merrigal”: Champion Bay. Swan River
district (W.A.), harbors a centipede that ordinarily
measures 13 or 14 inches and occasionally stretches to
17. He flashes a copper-tinged coat on the plains where
the herbage has got short and scant, and when sud-
denly come upon buckles his tailboard to his head
and with a succession of rapid leaps makes things
lively for the disturber of his peace. Due to this
accomplishment and his hang-dog-like jowl, bushmen
generally are satisfied with a nodding acquaintance,
and that old reptilian expert Davenport Cleland,
though he’d handled most of the Westralian forest
denizens, wouldn’t touch him with a roping-pole.
“Arden Hill” : The popular misconception
that in the realm of pyrotechnic diction the bullock-
driver holds the belt, is due to the fact that coal-
mine wheelers work underground out of earshot of
connoisseurs. A gaudily-decorated arid highly-explo-
sive vocabulary is as indispensable in the equipment
of a wheeler as his safety-lamp and sprag. Without
it the output would dwindle, ' coal stocks would
quickly become depleted, and eventually the Govern-
ment would be compelled to prospect for oil wells
or some new source of energy to keep the wheels of
industry from rusting. Nothing can equal the terrific
verbal onslaught of a wheeler when a full skip gets
A DASH OF BITTERS.
SWAGGY : “Hullo, boss. D'ye keep the pub at Railville now?"
BUNG : “Yes; but before six o'clock closing it kept me."
off the road. Ornamental imprecation may reach a
high standard on the dusty, wide plains, or amid
the carnage of battle, but it’s poor stuff compared
with the mighty Yolapuk of the pit. This rolls on
the air current and sweeps around the brattices like
the breath of Beelzebub; it illuminates the darkness
of the tortuous tunnellings like forked lightning; it
ricochets over undercasts and under overcasts; it
is majestic, brilliant, . stimulating and a national
asset. Long may it reign.

“Bill R." : Those Abus, who periodically
--kite about, big shearing tallies should mention
something about the class of sheep operated upon. I
have been at the game, off and on, since ’B3, and
have tomahawked alongside many of the guns of that
period. Their records were invariably made when
shearing clean-legged sheep, among which were many
bare-bellies and rosellas with only a few chops of
wool along the back. Good scores are likewise
possible among the open-woolled crossbred jum-
bucks, but every big gun kicks back many notches
when he strikes the wrinkled real merino of the stud
stations. The best performance I ever saw put up
was at Bibbenluke, Monaro (N.S.W.), in 1.908, when
Volney Day, a Bungendore crack, peeled 227, 243
and 217 in three successive days—the knock-off bell
ringing- at 4 o’clock on the third day. That was
among woollies admittedly as heavy to shear as any-
thing in the Commonwealth.
“Hay”: In ISS9 a wave of mice just like
the one described by “dial” (B. 26/4/’l7) swept
over Red Hill (S. Aus.), where I was
wheat-farming. I don’t know where they
came from, but swarms of mice appeared suddenly
and invaded all buildings. Tt was at the end of an
exceptionally good summer. In houses and barns
everything that a mouse could gnaw had to be kept
in very close cases or tins. We even had to nail
the harness belonging to the teams in zinc-lined
cases, for the first night the plague arrived they ate
through the linings and tore the straw-stuffing out of
a new set of collars. When the frantic little brutes
got busy at night it was impossible to sleep, for they
over-ran the place in squeaking swarms, attacking
and devouring each other. The cats were afraid of
them, and declined to try to cope with such an
abnormal rush of business. Towards winter the
unpleasant visitation abated, and the rodents van-
ished as suddenly as they came.
“The Old Soldier” : The venison that came
to Perth the other day (B. 19/4/’I7I is another
helping of the importing folly. The red deer is a
very dangerous animal in any settled country where
he is allowed to run at large. The full-grown stags
can easily leap a fence more than seven feet high,
so that no paddocks are safe from them. If a man
tries to drive a trespassing male beast out of the
crop before it has eaten its fill he will certainly
be attacked with the Tore hooves, which are ex-
tremely sharp and will give dangerous wounds. The
stag has a good memory for a cultivation plot
where he has once had a good feed, and when he
returns generally brings his whole seraglio.
* * ■* *.
“Agricola”: “Daybreak” (B. 26/4/T7)
seems surprised about finding a maiden heifer giving-
milk. I saw a well-bred Jersey calf, eight months
old, giving up juice in 1911 on the Mitta (Vic.).
In 1913, at Camperdown (Vic.), W. Kerr owned a
Jersey calf which commenced to milk when six
months old, and at nine months the Agricultural
chemist tested her product. She gave 51b. a day,
6 per cent, butter fat. Her family tree sheltered
champion butter cattle on both sides. Artificial
selection for many generations is no doubt the main
reason, and sucking by other calves is the immediate
exciting cause.
“F.0.C.”: The account had just come
into camp from the store and-Bill was checking it
over. Suddenly he exploded: “Gorstruth! Axe-
handles two bob each now ! This war takes the
bun! ”
“Good Australian”: The United States
Agricultural Department has an immigration depart-
ment for plants and trees, none of which are allowed
into the country until they have been proved free
from disease and desirable additions to the coun-
try’s plant kingdom. St. John’s wort and the
Scotch thistle would never have got into Australia
if we had possessed a plant-immigration depot. The
Americans have introduced some valuable plants of
late years, such as the jujube, a very good, fresh
fruit from China, which might do well in the Vic-
torian Mallee or north in South Australia. The
Chinese pistache nut has also been acclimatised
because it has a variety of uses. The pistache ought
to do .splendidly in the Goulburn Valley (Vib.).
Then there are Chinese chestnuts and persimmons,
both regarded as staple foods. They both grow well
wherever the thermometer does not drop under zero.
Then there is the chayote, a pear-shaped fruit from
tropical America, somewhat resembling a vegetable-

THIRST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The dhurra bags were innocent of grain ;
Our fantasses were empty, dry and hot;
The land had never felt a drop of rain
Since Moses’ host had trod the dreary spot.
The camels grunted, sore-backed as they stood,
Their sweat-glands dripping sweat as red as blood.
Steep, scorching sandhills stayed our blistered feet;
The sun with cruel intent crouched down from high.
Held in a dome steel-grey and hard with heat,
And focussed all his rays on old Sinai,
The riders with cracked lips and blistered faces
Cursed, and grinned, and prayed for an oasis.
The dancing mirage mocks their eager quest,
While phantom lakes above the salt sands hover.
Their burning thirst they conquer with a jest,
And wonder when the blanky war’ll be over.
Says Bill and Jim, and laugh together—
“l pity blokes out Hack o’ Bourke this weather."
SINAI.

“Eddyson”: This explains the, to many
residents of Pigskin, mysterious disappearance of
Henry Searle, of Searle’s Swamp. Searle’s Swamp was,
and is still, I have no doubt, a dreary sweep of
shallow dampness in the flat and barren lands
between Pigskin and Idono, Vic. The country pro-
duces a few reeds and some prodigious bulrushes, and
here and there a contorted and almost leafless gum
that looks like a frantic skeleton escaped from the
crypt in its rags, and crying “Fire!” and, at the time
of which I write, upheld a solitary paling hut with
a rusted iron chimney and two single-paned win-
dows, over one of which was clapped a bark patch.
Here dwelt Henry Searle, hatter, Henry Searle’s
iron-grey, bloated, toothless and bumble-footed cattle-
dog Sam, and Searle’s hook-nosed, Hebraic and
almost-featherless cockatoo “Methusalem,” a vener-
able bird that had almost lost his voice, but con-
tinued to soliloquise in whispers the whole day
long. Searle had no legal claim to Searle’s Swamp,
but it was his fixed conviction that the land and
the waters thereon were wholly his own, that he
held exclusive riparian fishing, shooting, boating
and bathing rights by .virtue of his long residence
SPARING THEIR BLUSHES.
No. 1: “Brother, the sea is glorious; sup-
pose we peel off and take a dip?”
No. 2: “Hush, brother; let us not be weal;
there are females about.”
on the swamp. Searlc’s hut on closer inspection
was found to be a kind of Noah's Ark built on two
large punts laboriously fashioned from hollow logs,
and tethered to a writhing stringy-bark butt with
a bullock-chain. This was provision against the
frequent spreading of the swamp in the rainy
season. “It was either float or flood,” Searle ex-
pained, “ ’n’ iv course I perferred t’ float.” There
was, however, the option of shifting to higher,
healthier and more accessible localities, but when
this was mentioned to Searle he cried: “What, ’n'
leave me swamp? Not me. Me swamp’s all I got,
’n’ I’ll stick to it.” Henry’s great grievance in
life was against unchartered duck-shooters or eel-
fishers who had the audacity to use his
swamp. He readily granted permission to
sportsmen to shoot over his waters if they, asked
him, but the sound of a shot fired by an audacious
scoundrel who had not received the formal permit
of the proprietor brought Searle bounding out of his
hut, and sent him racing round the lake, a large
club in his right hand and vengeance in his heart.
The fires of unlicensed eel-fishers had the same effect,
sending Searle scooting through the night round the
great waste of water in his shirt, inflamed with a
kind of fury. Often the hatter’s charge involved a
run of many miles, but in those cases he took a
bag and a billy with him, and for- hours the pursued
might behold the mad little man scooting round the
edge of the swamp, possibly camping for refresh-
ments by the way, but always taking up the chase
again. As Searle grew older his pride of possession
waxed greater, and his determination to assert ruler’s
rights over Searle’s Swamp became a very aggressive
kind of madness. One morning when the swamp
was at its widest, and Searle’s hut was well afloat,
Henry was brought to his door by the sound of
distant shooting. The insolent poaching miscreants
were at the far end of the swamp, peppering Searle’s
teal on Searle’s waters, and presently they saw the
little man Evading ashore at his eqd. He had a
gun in his hand. For hours after they beheld Searle
chasing round the northern limits of liis swamp. He
camped for lunch and boiled his billy, then resumed
the pursuit with the fatuous zeal of a mad ant. The
pursued moved east along the south shore, Searle
was running west along the north shore. Mean-
while for hours the shooting continued. It was after
4 p.m., and Searle was within a quarter of a mile
of the violators of his sacred rights, when the latter
dumped their ducks into a trap they had in waiting,
gave Henry an ironical cheer, and drove off. Thir-
teen days later a dusty, bedraggled, travel-worn,
little, whiskered old man, carrying a gun, plunged
into the house of a total stranger at Prahran, Mel-
bourne, and behaved in so amazing and menacing a
manner that he was instantly overpowered and
handed over to the police. The house was the home
of one of the men who had been duck-shooting on
Searle’s Swamp, 109 miles away, nearly a fortnight
previous. The little old man with the gun was
Henry Searle, still in pursuit. Authority found
Searle of Searle’s Swamp a rest-house at Kew. It
being beyond question essential to the public weal
that he should reside there permanently, the people
of Pigskin saw no more of Searle of Searle’s Swamp.
“Te Wliare”: Re the statement by Gregory
Mathews that, since foxes have become numerous in
Australia, the lyre-bird has to a large extent left off
building its great stick-nest on the ground among
ferns, etc., instead of placing it. on the top of tall
stumps and other elevated positions for protection.
Long before the advent of brer fox I commonly
found the nest of this imitative but not over-brainy
fowl high up on the ledges of cliffs (a very favorite
site), on tall tree-ferns, canopies of wild vine, etc.
It still nests, however, in many instances —sometimes
right out in open forest —-on logs, etc., very near or
on the ground. One foolish bird built on top of a
low stump in open country within 20 yards of a
main road. Foxes often annex the single chocolate-
brown and grey egg, or the noisy chick, which has
a ringing dinner-call which may be heard over half
a mile away. By-the-way, on top of the arch at the
entrance to Yarrangobilli Caves a lyre-bird builds
almost every year. It hops down sometimes and has
4 o’clock tea with the tourists. I’ve seen specimen
plates, etc., of Mathew’s great book on Australian
birds. It is a magnificent record of our feathered
population, and in manner of production over-
shadows Gould’s wonderful work. But in Gould’s
time (the early ’4o’s) Australia was more or less
terra incognita, and the difficulties which lay in his
path were enormous. Gould’s only assistant, Gil-
bert, was speared to death by the blacks in W.A.
Incidentally, Gould still stands alone among the
world's birdologists.

The mice which are making life a burden
in the wheat-farming country north of Adelaide are
said to be developing a rapidly-fatal disease. Edgar
Waite, of the Museum, diagnoses it as ringworm, and
thinks it will effectively wipe out the pest, but he
warns the public that children may catch the disease
from the mouse per agency of the cat. The Vaughan
Government has offered £2OO to any executioner
who can properly deal with the vermin.
“P.W.” : Re the introduction of rabbits
into Victoria. W. A. Brodribb in his “Leaves from
my Journal since 1835,” reports that with seven
others en route to Corner Inlet he got stranded on
a. sandbank. Some of the party rowed to Rabbit
Island, where they had fine sport shooting bunnies.
It appears that the captain of a whaler had turned
a pair loose, and they soon increased and overran the
limited room. Brodribb met them in 1842.
A BLOKE’S PHILOSOPHY.
[Fob The Bulletin.]
There’s a time of life when the world seems glad;
There’s a time of life when the world is sad;
There’s a time of life when we bend our back
And stagger along on the downward track.
There's a spell, of course, when the blood runs slow,
Like the sluggish ooze of the melting snow;
When you look on the past as one in a dream,
And hours run past as a joyous stream.
There's a day for all when we start the race
With a smile and a laugh at the headlong pace.
There's another day when the prize is dross,
And the soul is dulled ’neath the Swagman’s Cross.
There’s a time o’ year when your nerves will hum
Through rain and racket and Hell’s red rum.
And there's a date when the man with a handy spade
Will tuck you away in your bed well made.
MAOim.AXn. PUKETAPU.
“Hay”: “H.Y.E.” (B. 26/4/17) describes
a flying marsupial niouse. I have seen a variety that
must be of quite a different species. The biggest
family of them I ever struck were playing in a
cane grass flat towards, the south end of the Barrier
flange just inside the N. S. Wales border. The only
other lot 1 ever happened on was not far from Lake
Frome, so I don’t think they can be very plentiful.
These pretty little creatures do not fly or float like
the ’possum mouse, but hop exactly like a kangaroo.
In fact, they are miniature ’roos, except that their
little tails are held erect and ornamented with a tiny
tuft or brush of fur.
* * * »
“Ela” : Ou falling cocoanuts, the remarks
of “To Babarantiko” are absolutely correct.
Many natives receive the nuts on their heads,
but little or no damage results, as a rule,
Brown Brother having a head that would enable
him to weather a fair at Donnybrook. Occasionally
a child is killed, or a dog. Once I witnessed a nut lay
out a missionary on the coast of Papua. The scholars
from the sin-chaser’s factory were enjoying a picnic,
the reverend gentleman attending the orgie to see
that the girls did not throw too many friendly looks
at the young bucks. At sunset he gathered around
him the party, and before wading into the roast pig;
baked yams, turtle steaks and other foods, he led
them in a little hymn, and then proceeded to chant
a grace. He had got through when a nut dropped
and laid him out. It was the most wonderful coinci-
dence I ever saw. He received a mild concussion
that kept him in bed for over a week. Near shaves
are very common, and all the whites have their
yarns to spin of death missed by a few inches. The
bombs fall most frequently at night, consequently few
people are hurt.

“H.V.E.”: Excepting the house or wel-
come swallow, wild birds seldom enter rooms
through open windows, either in search of flies, etc.,
or by chance; but I have snared a scissors-grinder
(restless flycatcher) and a cobbler’s-awl bird (spine-
billed honeysucker) this way. The latter in its
flutterings became enmeshed in strong spider’s web

built in the corner of the room, and eventually
dropped into a basin of water. A boobook owl,
attracted by the light, was another visitor. Prowl-
ing cats sometimes bring in night-birds which sweep
low over the ground in hawking moths and other
insects, such as the spotted night-jar (a rare bird),
the common (or white-throated) night-jar, and the
uncommon owlet-nightjar, a bird of the frogmouth
family—sometimes known as “little morepork.” The
swallow, of course, often dashes in and out again, in
search of a site for its mud-bowl nest. Another
species—the striped diamond bird (Pardalotu afjinis )
—sometimes nests under the roofs or houses,
sparrow-fashion, while the domestic hen ( G alius
domesticus ) sometimes invades the sanctity of the
bedroom, and, after preliminary' heraldry, deposits
her egg in the centre of the counterpane. The wag-
tail (black-and-white fantail) nests occasionally on
the beams and rafters of barns and outhouses, thus
providing a restaurant for bush rats or cats.

“Ivukukuku”: When you have sapi-sapi
shell in Papua you lock it up in a strong-room and
wire the windows. At present prices it is worth
£2O a sack. The slieli is two or three or four
inches long and broad, rough and dirty-looking, with
an oysterish aspect; has a white inside with a small
rim of scarlet. This is the material for the famous
red-shell money of Papua, which even the most
civilised Papuan prefers to coin. It is ground into
coins the size of threepences and sixpences, and sells
at threepence and sixpence apiece. A hole in the
middle allows of stringing. Belts, necklaces, ear-
rings and armlets made of sapi-sapi are to be seen
in every store that caters for native trade; the prices
run to seven or eight pounds of a boy’s hard-earned
wages, and they are very readily paid—except by
stray tourists from the steamers, who wander about
Samarai, pick up a red-shell money-belt, and Care-
lessly offer half a crown for it. When the real price
is mentioned they have a fit and go back to the
steamer. The very latest coinage in Papua is
quinine—especially since the rise - in prices.
A quinine tabloid will buy so much betel-nut, so
much yam or taro, so much green cocoanuts, accord-
ing to the district where it is taken. The best dis-
tricts are those far, but not too far, out. Near the
settlements plenty of quinine can be got; away out,
the gentle cannibal takes it for something to do
with sorcery. The semi-civilised “boy” who has
learned the effect of that small white circle on his
chills and sweats and pains will give astonishing
value for it.
“Wart”: I’ll back the swamp quail to
apply for the baby bonus more often than any other
Australian bird. On two occasions I have found
nests containing 13 eggs. The mountain duck, the
black duck and the teal often lay up to a dozen,
while the widgeon is sometimes credited with a
baker’s dozen. Several birds common to the main-
land and the Speck lay one to two more eggs on the
island than their mainland relations. The swift
lorikeet, for instance: on the mainland a clutch
consists of two to three eggs—in Tasmania three to
five. Has it struck any other observer that the
birds which rear the largest families are not the
most plentiful ? No land bird can approach in num-
bers the gulls, petrels and terns, who lay only one,
or at the most two eggs.

for Wonderful
De Witts puis
When the old is made young' ag'ain and the
weak is made strong that is indeed a triumph.
When a man is so long 1 and so seriously ill that he, his fiiends
and his doctors all give up hope of him ever getting- well again,
and then he is, after all, completely cured, it may easily and
truly be described as remarkable —a great triumph.
“I am over 60 years of age, and for over 40 years suffered
with what I was told was chronic Bright’s Disease. While in the
early days I was able to do a little work, now for the last 20
years I have not been able to work, except at very distant intervals,
and then not more than two or three days at a time; in fact, 1
have had to keep my bed for four or five months at a stretch.
“I have had many illnesses, each in itself very serious. Once
I was so bad that I was given only five hours to live by m.V'
medical man, but just pulled through more dead than alive. I
was so bad that even after I was supposed to have recovered my
friends tried to get me into a hospital, but I was refused admit-
tance as ‘lncurable.’ • ,
“Last March I was taken very seriously ill, this time with
rheumatic fever. Here now, it was thought, was truly the end of
things: there was little hope ever entertained of my recovering. I
was rapidly and surely dying.
“While I was so ill my son-in-law, hearing such remarkable
reports about the cures effected by De Witt’s Kidney and Bladder
Pills, bought a box for me to try. I commenced to take them,
and. to put it very mildly, was amazed to find in two days’ time
that all symptoms of fever had left me. In a week from first
commencing the pills I was able to get up, and I gradually got
stronger. It was not very long before I was able to resume work,
and since I once started I have not lost a single day from work.
Surely this is wonderful after my past experience of years of
unfitness.
“I am getting to be an old man, therefore I consider this case
most remarkable. My name. Arthur Jarvis, is well known in the
district of Battersea, and if anyone should doubt the truth of my
statement and should write or call on me at 32 Frances Street.
S.W., I will give them convincing proof of the truth of my
statement. De Witt’s Kidney and Bladder Pills have given me
a strength that I can truthfully say I have never before
experienced at any time of life.
“They have given me a real pleasure in ljfe, such as I have
not experienced for 40 years.”
The above statement was made in July. 1912. It might well
be wondered whether such a cure could remain permanent. Two
years afterwards Mr. Jarvis said: “Although getting on in years
—I am now (jo years of age—l am going along wonderfully well.
I have boon able to keep going all the year round, and have not
missed a single day from work. I have only got De Witt’s Kidney
and Bladder Pills to thank for this marvellous cure, and I

Nearly everyone knows that thousands of people die every year
from deadly kidney disease, but only a few are aware that this
terrible mortality is the result of sheer neglect.
When people are attacked by the first symptoms of kidney
disease, such as aches and pains in the various parts of the body,
puffiness under the eyes, cloudy urine, nasty taste in the mouth
first thing in the morning, they take no notice whatever. This is
where a terrible mistake is made, for kidney disease will not cure
itself. Neglect means that these symptoms turn into specific com-
plaints such as rheumatism, lumbago, gout, sciatica, cystitis, gravel,
stone, etc., which are the forerunners of deadly Bright’s Disease.
Just a little exposure to cold or damp and you have got a
pain in the back. Agony to bend or move any way, bad headache,
puffiness under the eyes; these mean for certain that you have
kidney trouble. Then you should look out for deadly Bright’s
Disease, which creeps on unawares.
It is positively dangerous to, trifle or play with the most
simple form of kidney disease.
If you have such symptoms as a nasty taste in the mouth first
thing in the morning, constipation, general feeling of weakness
and irritability, aches and pains in the muscles or joints, puffiness
under the- eyes or bad headache, you certainly need De Witt’s
Kidney and Bladder Pills.
Or if you suffer from lumbago, backache, rheumatism, gout,
sciatica, stone, gravel, or pains in the bladder, muscles and joints,
you are foolish to yourself if you do not commence to use these
wonder-workers immediately.
To cure rheumatism or any other form of kidney trouble you
must positively get rid of the cause —the poisonous uric acid.
Remember, to do this a real remedy must pass through the
kidneys and the bladder, and not through the bowels, as most
kidney pills do. When you see the urine change to a muddy bluish
color —a feature which distinguishes De Witt’s Kidney and Bladder
Pills from all others —you know beyond all doubt they have put
their healing touch on the right spot—the kidneys and the bladder.
A wonderful pill straight to the kidneys—that is why De Witt’s
Pills give such amazingly quick relief —every time. A permanent
cure follows in almost every case. Look out for that blue-colored
urine. It means so much.

Somerset Maugham, who has written a
couple of Marie Tempest plays, recently
staged -Caroline," a new comedy, in New
York, written by him while engaged as a
British agent in Switzerland, where he
was under close surveillance of the police
and the German secret-service men. He
made Caroline a comedy in order to relieve
his anxiety, and adds —looking towards his
press agent —“There were times when I had
to hurry the work because I feared I would
be arrested before I could complete it. In-
deed, during the whole time I was writing I
could see a German agent watching me from,
out in the snow.” German agents must
catch cold on some useless jobs.
A queer thing happened after Barry
Lupino had come his awful cropper on the
stage at Melbourne King’s the other Satur-
day. The curtain was rung down, and the
band was playing “Gorsave,” when one of
the Taits asked the prostrate man whether
he thought he could stand up to take a
“call,” and so reassure the audience that he
was not knocked right out. It afterwards
appeared that Lupino was really insensible
at the time, but the magic word “call” must
have reached his brain. He did stand up,
and with his hand at the back of his head
he went through the form of bowing. When
he recovered his senses and was told of liis
action he had not the slightest recollection
of it, nor of having uttered words of pain
as,he lay on the stage.
“Private Boxer” : On her return from the
States Melba announced that she and Charlie
Chaplin had “done an impromptu film,”
which will presently be shown in the cause
of charity, to benefit the boys in khaki. The
songster incidentally mentioned that Chaplin
was a very charming man, also a very good-
looking fellow, who wants to do serious
work —but the picture-managers won’t let
him.
“Peg-Leg” : Apparently it wasn't an easy
job teaching Enid Bennett to be a movie
star. Her director, Tom Ince, states that
60,000 ft. of film were taken to complete the
first two dramas which totalled only 10,000 ft.
when eventually screened. The remaining
50,000, taken for experimental scenes, were
scrapped. That is where the fault lies in
most of our local productions. Every foot of
film taken is put into the completed picture
regardless of quality of photography, acting
or anything else ; and a film that might have
been worth while if the producers had con-
densed it to two good reels, is padded out to
five with the rubbish. Unfortunately the
men who pay the money don’t look at it that
v»ay. I have seen four Australian film mag-
nates become delirious with rage because
their producer had used 500 ft. in experi-
mental work.
“0.K.” : The motor-car, like the telephone,
came as a special boon to melodramatists.
For sudden-death purposes it serves better
than any other vehicle you can mention.
J. M. Barrie uses it in the tabloid tragedy
that Ada Reeve is playing at Melbourne
Tivoli. He .wants his heroine to be sud-
denly left in a terrible fix at the rooms of
the lover she is about to elope with. The
dramatist might, of course, have made the
mail die of heart disease, like the awful]'
embarrassing lover.in one of De Maupassant s
stories. But this would have been highly
improbable in the case of a vigorous young
mail. So he rushes gaily forth to get a taxi,
and is stated to be killed by a passing car.
How much better than making him tall a
victim to a cab or tramcar. The accident
seems more “classy"’ and less unlikely.
