John McCormack announces that he just
loves the people of the U.S.A., and has
become an Amurkan citizen. Also, lie re-
marks parenthetically : “Someone has accused
me of being a pro-German. Heaven forbid .
What a wonderful fight the Allies have put
up on the Somme. We must be <>n our way
to victory and Berlin shortly. fho lettei
was written before the States declared war.
There is therefore a reasonable chance of the
patriotic tenor fighting for the flag lie ha>
just taken to his bosom. W e may >et hear
of the Faust of other days leading the
“Soldiers’ Chorus” in the land where the
grand old air was originally inspired!
“Ancient Prompter"’: A London revue
chorus-girl (Pat Peel on the bills) in the
early days of the war, sang and danced In 1
self into the affections of a young lieutenant
in tin- “Gay Gordons.” Just before going
to the Front the soldier made a will in the
girl’s favor, scribbling it on the form pro-
vided in his army pay-book. The document
was duly witnessed by a brother ofncei.
Last year both officers were killed in France.
The will could not be found, and in its
absence the Probate Court was asked to
pronounce in favor of the revue lady, which
it did. The 305.-a-week chorus girl is now
the possessor of a little fortune of some-
thing over £15,000.
“S.T.S.” inspects an old friend's new suit:
1 have just got the acting-edition of “Experi-
ence,” G. V. Hobart’s play now having a long-
run in nearly every big U.S.A. city. It calls itself
a “Morality Play of To-day,” and follows what is
doubtless the right line for such things—only the
most obvious sentiments, but shown with details that
are unexpected. Love is left on the old farm, villi
Hope as her sole companion; Youth goes out into
the world, led by Ambition, who dies, however,
when Beauty and Pleasure are met. All the while
Experience is of the party, pleasant, shrewd, middle-
aged—a regular actor-manager’s part, lie does not
shy at anything, but introduces Youth to all and
sundry, and draws a moral when a moral seems
necessary. All the characters are Americans of
the most modern type, and in the dress of to-day.
Youth avoids Passion, but only by the help of
Intoxication. (“Of two evils he has chosen the
less,” remarks .Experience.) He is chiefly afraid of
Poverty, but learns that there are worse friends;
and of Work, whom he cannot find again when he
wants him. There are brisk scenes in a cabaret, a
“dive,” a dope-shop, and a gambling-hell, with a
fine “curtain” of the nigger waiter cheerfully hand-
ing sandwiches regardless of the fact that Despair
has just shot himself. Of course at the finish
Youth comes back to Love, and Ambition revives.
The play is like “Everywoman,” but with much
more go in it, arid should be a big success in Aus-
tralia. Youth (Conrad Nagel) and Experience
(Edmund Elton) are the only really big parts, but
in the 10 acts there are about 70 others. Albert
Gran, the first and best Judge Jeffries that Australia
saw*, is Wealth," an elderly man fretting because he
cannot be one of the boys again. Curiously, Gran
had almost exactly the same part—without the
symbolism—in an American play which Nellie
Stewart tried here without much success.
Business, Bobbery, etc
“E. 8.” : Who said that the rabbit was a
curse to the countryV A couple of Satur-
days ago the Braidwood Refrigerating Co.
(N.S.VV.) paid £412 to local trappers for one
week’s catch —2200 pairs were received at
the works on that day. There are also oppo-
sition buyers operating in the district and
doing good business. Expert hunters have
been known to make as high as £l5 in one
week, setting the snares only on five nights
a week. He is a very poor trapper who
cannot make £<> a week just now.
Victoria’s Railway Commissioners seem
to have settled down into a state of calm
approval of the system of petty burglary
which prevails in the Department. Cases
ind packages, down to small boxes of fruit
carried on suburban lines, are opened by
peculating porters or clerks, but complaints
are of no avail. Perhaps the Commissioners
think that an underpaid service is entitled
to perquisites. It comes rough on the pro-
portion of the public that has to supply the
perqs.
». * * •
“D." : What is the most glaring imposition
a business and robbery in Australia to-day?
I think every business man who will admit
my thing will agree that the fur trade works
•If the most scandalous impositions. I can
speak for Melbourne, but it is not likely
there is any conspicuous reluctance in Syd-
ney to sell an unsuspecting customer five
shillings’ worth of bad rabbit-skins for 18
guineas every time the opportunity offers.
The more unconscionable section of the fur
traders do not even take the trouble to
secure good rabbit-skins when they intend
imposing bunny’s overboat upon some simple
dame as the outer garment of a rarer and
more elegant animal. Well-chosen, cold-
climate rabbit-skins make a fairly lasting
stole, muff or robe; bad rabbit-skin wears
patchy in one brief season. Ye ; t when it is
proven that the price of decent furs has been
taken for sickly, summer-crop skins of Brer
Rabbit, plausibly disguised, defendant’s law-
yer can plead, ‘‘lt is for the buyer to be-
ware,” putting it in imposing Latin, as in
a recent Melbourne case, and the Bench
responds: “Nuf sed!” leaving complainant
‘with her bunch of decayed hides and a load
of costs. Why is it that the open spieler who
sells a gold brick is not protected by the
Latin observation which means “Let the
buyer beware!”? No matter how wary the
ordinary buyer of furs is he-she cannot tell
that a nice-looking alleged coney seal is
really a mass of Riverina rabbit-skin that
will presently moult every hair and make
the wearer look like a mange-stricken tyke.
The law, in discovering a moral difference
between the rascal who sells a gilt-edged
brick for a block of gold, and the rogue who
sells a collection of dyed bunny-skins for
an expensive garment of legitimate furs only
corroborates the general belief that it is a
stupendous ass. Many tradespeople not more
conspicuously fraudulent than the furriers
are compelled to give a printed statement
of the ingredients of their special wares.
Why is this law inapplicable to the profes-
sional spielers who forge and utter counter-
feit musquash coats and fictitious seal,
squirrel or mole stoles and muffs?
* * * *
The U.S. makes most of its own -whisky.
• On the morning after, the free-born citizen
only has one query put to him by those who
•notice the size of his head: “Corn or rye?"
But in Australia we stick pretty solidly to
imported juice. During the past couple of
years this country imported £2.000,000 worth
of spirits. The time seems well matured, for
our new Parliament of old Parliamentarians
to try and do something in the, way of getting
the national hooks into that bunch of money.
Even the rabid whisky-hater of the mostly
fat-headed “temperance” leagues and alli-
ances should be able to lend a hand, recognis-
ing that it must be better for the nation, in
the long run. to patronise the local product
than to make a blind selection from the
variety of mixed poisons we obtain from
abroad for our yearly million.
“Square Jane”: “D.F.” (B. 19/4/’l7) is a
long way out in remarking that Pengelly’s
(S. Aus.) use 7000 ft. of Tasmanian timber
per year. They use on the average 5000 ft.
per day of Tasmanian wood, and hold in
stock over three million super feet of timbers.
This firm started by making birdcages, and
now turns out furniture and all kinds of rail-
way carriages, tramcars and motor bodies.
Incidentally, 3000-odd icechests were manu-
factured during 1916, and the contracts in
hand include dining-cars for the East-West
Railway.
“Double R.”: “Adelaide” asks if £34 is
a record price paid for a cow. It isn’t. One
cow-worshipper bought an addition to his
shrine for which he paid £35. This hanoened
at Cook and Son’s sale at East Barron in
March last. Don’t know what breed of
whisky they used at the sale, but it must
have been pretty strong.
THE MARCH OF MARGARINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Time was when none would name it
Save loudly to declaim it.
Belittle and defame it—
Hut now, what change of mien!
Against it none will utter
A word, while most folk mutter
They like it more than butter —•
That dainty margarine!
Proud footmen, once, and pages
Stamped up and down in rages
Like tigers in their cages ;
Fat butters made a scene ;
While nurses, on occasion,
Forgot their proper station,
And asked in indignation:
What did the missus mean !
And cooks, with tearful traces
Upon their comely faces,
At once gave up their places
When offered margarine.
Hut now that peers and princes
Go short of meats and minces,
And each of them evinces,
An interest more keen
In common foods and cheaper,
The coachman and the keeper,
The clerk and crossing-sweeper
Have turned to margarine.
KN GLAND.
W. H. OGILVIE.

Government Notices.
COMMONWEALTH OF AUSTRALIA.
Department of the Navy,
Melbourne, Ist May, 1917.
Tenders for Pumping Plant .and Equip-
ment for the Commonwealth Naval
Dockyard, Cockatoo Island, Sydney.
JTIENDERS will be received, subject to
JL the conditions of tendering, for the
supply and delivery of Pumping Station
Plant and Equipment for the Commonwealth
Naval Dockyard, Cockatoo Island, Sydney,
N.S.W., until 12 noon on Wednesday, 10th
October, 1917.
Plans and specifications may be seen at
the office of the Director of Navy Contracts,
Navy Office, Melbourne; at the Common-
wealth Naval Dockyard, Cockatoo Island,
Sydney; and at the offices of the District
Naval Officer at Brisbane, Birkenhead
(South Australia), Fremantle and Hobart.
Copies can be obtained on payment of a
deposit of £l5, which will be refunded on
the receipt of a bona fide tender, and the
return of the documents.
Tenders are to be endorsed “Tenders for
Pumping Station Plant for Commonwealth
Naval Dockyard, Cockatoo Island,” on the
top left-hand corner of the envelope, and
addressed (if by post prepaid) to the Direc-
tor of Navy Contracts, Navy Otnce, Mel-
bourne ; or to the Director of Navy Contracts,
c/o the Commonwealth Naval Dockyard,
Cockatoo Island, Sydney; or to the Director
of Navy Contracts, c/o the District Naval
Officer at Brisbane, Birkenhead (South Aus-
tralia), Fremantle or Hobart, as the case
may be.
In cases where supply and delivery is
required at Sydney, the prices tendered are
to be c.i.f., Sydney ; the rate of freight is to
be taken as being 80s. per ton from the port
of shipment to Sydney, and if the actual
freight exceeds this amount the excess
charges will be paid by the Commonwealth
Government, upon satisfactory evidence being
produced. If, on the other hand, the actual
freight is less than 80s. per ton, a deduction
will be made in a similar manner.
Tenders to be in all cases on the printed
form, which can be obtained as above.
The lowest or any tender not necessarily
accepted.
JOSEPH COOK,
Minister of State for the Navy.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Before the Australian Parliament opens
what is left of the Labor party will meet to
choose a leader. There is a yarn, generally
accepted as authentic, that when it came to
selecting one after the breakaway of the
Hughes faction, Frank Tudor and Charlie
McDonald polled an equal number of votes,
and the two names were put in a hat. If
this is true, the party’s luck was out when
AFTER SIX.
BROWN : “Too late to get a drink?"
SMITH: “Yes, but you can have a mineral
water or a"
BROWN : “Oh, no, I only wanted a drink —
I’m not thirsty .”
it drew Tudor. He is a harmless, agreeable
little chap who was intended by nature to
be the faithful follower of a strong leader.
As a leader he is a calamity, and it is even
doubtful if his heart was in his work during
the election campaign. When the Victorian
Labor Leagues called upon Ministers to
resign over the conscription issue Russell
refused and Tudor complied. Had he not
done so he would have lost the safest of
seats in Yarra. Ever since Tudor has
seemed an unhappy man. In Vic. practi-
cally all the fighting was left to Frank
Anstey (that other good fighter, Frank
Brennan, being laid up with a bad throat) ;
but Anstey, despite his great gifts, is rather
too erratic for the job of leader. Brennan
has the necessary dialectical skill, Higgs’s
Ministerial experience would stand him in
good stead, and Catts is the best organiser
the party has. But probably the best man
for the position is Charlie McDonald. He
possesses shrewdness and experience; as
Speaker he showed that he was up to every
point of the parliamentary game, and he is
a fighter. Amiability is an excellent thing,
but in politics the Lord is with him who
girdeth up his loins and boggeth in.
[B. J, H. Owen', 75 Glenhuntly Road, Elwood, Vic.]
The elections saw. returned the following
original members of the Reps, who had sat
continuously: Chapman, Webster, Johnson,
Watkins, Bruce Smith, Cook, Kelly, Hughes
(N.S.W.) ; Groom, Bamford, McDonald,
Page (Q.) ; Maloney, Tudor (Vic.) ; Poyn-
ton (S.A.) ; McWilliams (Tas.) ; Mahon,
Forrest and Fowler (W.A.) k Thus out of
the first-elected 75 only 19 are left. Chanter'
(N.S.W.) and Salmon (Vic.) are also back,
but they have not sat continuously.
HARD TO BELIEVE.
MRS. ISAACS: “In de East it is a cusdom to
vosh de feet before endering a temple."
MR. ISAACS : “ Ain’t it marvellous vot
religious fervor vill make de human race
endure .”
Jo Cook drove the first nail into Phew-
sion’s coffin when he said, in the flush of
party triumph, that the sun of Labor had
set behind the hills, never to reappear. The
idea of the cause of Labor having finally
disappeared from political consideration
wouldn’t appeal to more than 20 per cent,
of Joseph’s partisans, anyhow. The other
80 per cent, are personally interested in
keeping Labor’s celestial lamp burning,
though they would like to see quite another
crowd trimming the wick. “The sun of
Labor has set behind the hills, never to re-
appear !” There must be - times when
Hughes would like to be able to summon a
thunderbolt from Heaven to smite his loving
and beloved brother.
Chas. Kingston used to tell another story
of George Reid. The South Australian held
that George possessed an egotism
almost as abounding as his girth, and
thereby hangs the tale. “Gabriel had
blown his trumpet,” said Kingston,
“his terrible, brazen trumpet, one blast
of which rent our heavens and sent
the clouds reeling and eddying like
distracted waifs. He blew another, and
the earth shook, the mountains gaped,
and up out of the dust came George
Reid, shaking the moult from his Gar-
gantuan frame. Again the angel
Gabriel blew a blast splitting immens-
ity, and George Reid clapped his hands
to his ears. Once more Gabriel raised
the glorious instrument to his lips, but
George Reid stumbled forward, caught
the angel’s robe and tugged it. ‘Here,’
he said, ‘that will do. I'm up!' "
The dear old Argus tripped on its skirt
the other day when it credited Hughes with the
statement that the Ministry would promote
“all that is worthy in unionism.” As soon
as he read the report the ex-Labor man, who
is now leading (more or less) the party of
Fat, hastily ordered a messenger to gallop
around to the editor and tell him not to com-
pound a blue pill. What he had uttered
was the soothing platitude that the new
Ministry would bestow its blessing on “all
that is worthy in the community.” Where-
upon the Argus breathed a deep sigh of
relief, and Fat put his axe back in his belt.
The political prophets are all taking
the count. After the great Hughes
maffick. Conscription was forecasted a cer-
tainty. It was badly walloped. On that,
Labor’s chances appeared golden, and Hol-
man went to the Ma State electors with an
aching heart. But Labor was donkey-licked,
and Holman ached no more. Then came the
Maryborough (Q.) election. Labor braced
itself for a bump, but Liberalism went down.
So Labor began to talk confidently about the
future, especially when the Senate scandals
sent Phewsion flying for shelter. Phe horny-
handed stood on the fence and cheered. A
few weeks of jubilation and the I ederal
avalanche swept Labor into the abyss, while
the Bananaland electors embraced the fat
shoulder of the Leg. Council they rescued
from Labor Premier Ryan. There seems to
be a mighty slump in the prophet industry.
If the judgment of the N. S. Wales Court
of Appeal, upholding the verdict in Egan v.
the Broken Hill Miners’ Union stands the
acid of the High Court, a new plank will be
added to the Labor platform. And it will
be one the public can be relied upon to think
very hard about. The appeal turned on
whether a union in its corporate capacity
could be held responsible for the acts of its
officials unless it could be established that
these actions were in pursuance of the pur-
poses for which . the union was _ founded.
Frances Egan, plaintiff at the original trial,
who got £450 damages from the union, un-
doubtedly suffered through the attentions of
the union and use of the boycott, but it was
argued that this mattered nothing since the
union had not been formed to boycott or
persecute anyone—that resolutions of the
union directing the campaign against Egan
were not within the functions of a union
meeting. The Court declined to swallow this,
and refused to assume that a union carrying
on a boycott is doing something for which it
ought not to stand responsibility. After
the High Court has spoken, unions will find
out for certain whether they are liable for
actions in their corporate capacity. If they
are not, quite a lot of people will reckon they
ought to be. If they are, quite as many will
hold to the opinion that discovery of the
truth may do much good.
When Victoria’s Parliament, after fre-
quent urging, passed a Rating on Land
Values Act more than two years ago, the
House of Rich Landlords —which naturally
doesn’t believe in having idle lands rated
on the unimproved value —casually inserted
a little clause compelling municipali-
ties that wanted to apply the measure to
use the State Land Tax Department’s
valuations, which may not be completed till
the war ends, or possibly not this century.
Anyhow, the Department is in no hurry, so
some councils are urging Peacock either to
spur it along or to amend the Act so that
they can use their own valuations. But
Peacock isn’t in any hurry, either, though
as a matter of common honesty and public
advantage the unimproved values rating
principle should have been compulsory long
ago. However, c.h. and p.a. don’t agitate
many politicians these days.
“XXX”: It was in 1892, in the press
gallery of the Maoriland House, that I first
heard R. C. Bruce, whoso death is now re-
ported. He had come back to Parliament
after two years’ absence; and everybody,
knowing his reputation as a Speaker,
trooped in to hear him when he rose in the
financial debate, and listened with keen
enjoyment to his delightfully easy and grace-
ful language. But how hopelessly antiquated
were his political views! “This is a policy
calculated to sap the self-reliance of the
people, and drive capital from the country,”
was the burden of his song—all becoming
very old stuff by that time, for it had been
the battle-cry of the Atkinsonites at the
1890 election, when Ballance swept the
polls. Bruce, before he settled down to
farming in Maoriland in 1877, worked as a
sailor, chiefly in American ships, and he
never lost the breezy, rugged air or trans-
parent honesty of the average seafaring
man.
ANOTHER HERO.
THE KID : “What did you do to win the
great war, father ?”
FATHER: “Me? I—er—I voted for Billy
Hughes."
C. R. Howroyd, who beat Kink O'Malley
for the Darwin (Tas.) seat in the Reps, and
died at the fag end of the fight, had one
decent piece of legislation to his credit—The
Shops’ Closing Act. But after piloting that
measure through he retired from the lime-
light and did nothing of note, as the “popu-
lar” politician understands things of note,
till he resigned.

BILL: “My oath! Pretty 'ot! But they

John Scaddan, ex-Premier of Westralia,
is a native of Moonta, S.A., and at the time
of the Conscription referendum he wired to
Premier Vaughan: “I desire officially to
notify you that from to-day I renounce
South Australia as my mother State unless
Moonta proves to be of true metal.” Perhaps
Moonta. wanted to get rid of its spendthrift
son; at any rate it turned Conscription down
by 1916 votes to 524. But last week, after
S.A. had in general voted for the Fusion,'
Scaddan sent another flamboyant wire:
“Congratulations, your State’s magnificent
response to our country’s call. You may
officially re-enter my acknowledgment of
S.A. as my mother State.”- S.A. made
note of it on the back of an old lOU.
The alliance between Liberal and National
Labor in S.A. took less than a week to
crack. These party labels, unwieldy and mis-
leading, mean that Peake follows Cook,
Vaughan follows Hughes, and Lundie tracks
Tudor. Peake refused any alliance with
Vaughan except for Federal purposes. The
Saturday after the general election saw
several by-elections for the State Assembly,
the most interesting of which was in East
Torrens. Vaughan put up Olifent, who was
a secretary to the U.L.P. in the days when
it urns united ; but not even the uniform of
the returned soldier, and the benefit of the
blessed word “National,” could turn the
trick. It was a straight, three-sided fight,
and each candidate scored over 3000 votes,
with the Liberal' a few hundreds ahead of
each of the others at the finish.
Australia pays for the national imports
and settles with Cohen by (1) borrowing,
(2) exports. This paper assumes that when
the tank runs dry and the mice and weevil
and submarines have finished with the
material awaiting shipment and on the
water, and the former customers begin to
feed themselves, Australia will settle the
weekly bill by deciding to meet the situation
with calmness and confidence. In the inter-
val the alleged fact that Germany is in great
dread of an economic war after the Great
Bash is over, and the unquestionable truth
that every one of the seven Australian Gov-
ernments is shivering with horror about the
day after to-morrow can be taken as encour-
agement to keep on being calm and confident
as heretofore. That tyiy Government should
propose to start paying for imports by the
simple device of not going bung has of course
evaded notice. If Hughes or Cook or Irvine
or anyone like that in the interval of win-
ning the war has an idea about why going
bung ought to be avoided, and can snatch a
few minutes to say something on the subject,
The Bulletin has a suspicion that the time
is just about ripe. How much longer do
they think the fuse will smoulder, even
assuming the war were to finish next week?
THE NATION THAT IS NOT SO SMALL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Lord Derby (British Secretary for War) states
that the war will not end until the United States has
her full fighting force thrown into the scale.
The war goes on. The little nations wait
The coming day of freedom bought with fire.
Not yet we burst the foeman’s guarded gate
Or touch the peace-wreath of the world’s desire.
Close up the ranks! Qnce more unto the breach 1
In days like these only the guns may preach.
The text of hate must be expounded well
In raid and charge and desolating flame.
The road to Heaven is by the edge of Hell
And glory yet shall gild the rays of shame.
’Tis for the little nations. Servia’s pain
And Belgium’s torture wring our hearts again.
Forget the rest. One little nation’s case
That bled and suffered for eight hundred years
Must not be mentioned while we still can face
The Teuton over seas of blood and tears.
Let Ireland wait. When the loud cannon pause
We may evade considering her cause.
What’s that? America must lend her aid
To the last stirring of her sinewed might
Before the German’s fury can be stayed
Or victory crown us in the stubborn fight;
And the small nation which we still deride
Has mighty hosts of kin on Wilson’s side.
Something must happen. Tory souls may fume
And fret and swear the time is not yet ripe;
But this we needs must learn e’er death and doom
Vield room for peace a bitter sword to wipe—
la breaking our victorious pathway through
That all America means Ireland too t
N.S.W.
PAT O’MAORI.

WORMS.
ONE NINE INCHES LONG AND ANOTHER FOUR
INCHES PASSED BY CHILD AFTER TAKING
COMSTOCK’S “DEAD SHOT” WORM PELLETS.
The effective qualities of Comstock’s “Dead
Shot” Worm Pellets have again been abund-
antly proved in the case of the young son
of Mrs. A. E. Bade, of 203 McKillop Street,
East Geelong, Vie. Mrs. Bade wrote us of
her own accord the statement published
below, and has consented to allow it to be
published, and she will have pleasure in
confirming the statements made therein.
Mrs Bade writes: “I have great pleasure
in writing to you to let you know the satis-
factory results I had in your ‘Dead
Shot’ Worm Pellets for my young son, aged
14 years. He has been ailing for some time
and complained of pain in the spine and a
very sore stomach, and often in the night
would start vomiting without any warning
whatever. He also had several fainting
turns. I became alarmed and took him to
the doctor, but the treatment did him no
good. I then thought I would try your
Worm Tablets,' as to me he had every
appearance of worms. I procured a bottle
of them, together with a bottle of Indian
Root Pills, and commenced the treatment.
The second day after taking them he passed
a stomach worm nine inches long, and the
next day one four inches long Since then
he has picked up wonderfully and is twice
the child he was. If he shows any signs
again I will not hesitate to try them, as I
was so pleased with the results. I am most
grateful to the proprietors of these tablets,
as my husband is away on Active Service
and the worry of our boy being so ill nearly
drove me mad. Thanking you again.”
STOMACH WORMS.
A MENACE TO CHILDREN.
Stomach worms are undoubtedly the most
invidious kind- of all. They inhabit the
stomach and inflict great havoc upon their
victim. They deprive the victim of half the
nutriment he consumes, and when the
stomach is empty gnaw the sides of the
stomach, causing such terrible sensations as
to often cause faintness. Aperient medi-
cines have no effect upon them, for when
any purgative is taken they fasten them-
selves to the walls of the stomach by a
groove runn&ng down their side. The best
method of dislodging stomach worms is to
starve the (system and take Comstock’s
“Dead Shot” Worm Pellets. The worms are
weakened by the want of food, and are
induced to tak<e whatever is taken into the
stomach. Comfstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm
Pellets contain the properties which sicken
and disable the worms, and a dose of Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills will clear them
out of the system.
Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm Pellets
are a purely vegetable medicinal preparation
for the eradication of worms in children and
adults. For sale by all chemists and store-
keepers, price 3s. (id. iter packet, or will be
sent post paid at the same price by The
W. H. Comstock Co., Ltd., 23 Lang Street,
Church Hill, Sydney.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
\ ictorian Chief Secretary McLeod lately
incited the Police Department to furnish a report
on proprietary racecourses in the vicinity of the
larra. The sum and substance of the report is that
the abolition ot proprietary racing would be a boon
and a blessing to the community. Wherefore McLeod
says it is “a grave question” whether these institu-
tions should be allowed to continue in existence. How
long it is going to take him to make up his mind
on the subject this page has no sort of idea, but as
lie announced the other day that the number of
racing days would be the same as last year the pro-
bability seems to be that McLeod can be relied
U P?" t?. keep the matter steadily in view. Mean-
while this pagp once more suggests that the time is
more than ripe for the State Governments throughout
Australia' to do the decent thing and get on to this
proprietary racing 'business with a steam-roller. To
speak about sport in connection with proprietary
lacing is to outrage the word, though there are per-
haps two or three clubs which do not come within
the sweeping condemnation. The thing is bad in
principle and worse in practice.
Hitherto in this controversy there has been on one
side a crowd of frantic killjoys who want to root out
racing altogether ; on the other the people who could
see no more inherent evil in a horse race than in
a yacht race. But time and experience have shown
that leaving the matter to a few fanatics and a
crowd of individuals addicted to let-it-slide prin-
ciples is no good. That there is far too much racing
can hardly be disputed. That there is benefit of any
sort in this except to shareholders of a few pro-
prietary clubs, to bookmakers, the breeders of a
poor grade of nag and to a vast crowd of parasites
is believed by no one. The sole justification of racing
is its sporting side. Where this is absent the thing
is a curse. That all the reasonable sporting require-
ments of the population of, say, Svdney and Mel-
bourne ■ cannot be provided at Flemington,
Caulfield and llandwick by clubs which race for
sport and not for profit this paper resolutely declines
to believe.
* * * #
“OX": The prohibition of all horse-
racing in Britain and Ireland suggests that petty
proprietary meetings, at least, should be suppressed
in Australia for the remaining term of the war.
ihe ordinary metropolitan mid-week meeting is a
rendezvous for slackers of the two sorts—spielers
and “mugs.” To stand outside the railway station
and gaze upon the crowd returning from a ponv
course is' to wonder why these things should be. It
is a crowd of scowling anti-conscriptionists, mostly
flushed with drink. A large assortment of wasters
mostly of fighting age. The irony of the evil spec-
tacle is in the fact that these ignoble habitual punters
are commonly known as .“battlers.” They are pre-
pared to shed their last shilling in combat with the
books. They will fight on against odds until their
supply of ammunition fails.- They are eternally
doing in their little bit.
David Brandt, a Sydney parson who
orates periodically about “morals” before the Scotch-
byterian Assembly, has been at it again. “There
are 9000 young men hanging on to and loafing
upon the pony courses of this State.” Any bookie
will lay Davy 100 to 3d. that he is talking through
the top of his tile. Including all the old men and fat
men and young men and females who rush to the
ponies every week the job of finding 9000 altogether
would end nine times out of 10 in making a mistake.
* * * *
The tote handed out its biggest dividend
so far in N.S.W. on Saturday—£76 10s. on Orelad
in the Welter at Maitland. On the 10s.
ticket this represented 152 to 1 ; ‘lke was offering
20’s. As an argument for patronising the machine
when something unexpected is considered likely to
occur these figures establish a strong case. Of
course the punter who goes looking for long prices
is just as certain to baek losers whether he is dealing
with the tote or Ike, the only comfort for him being
that when one of the rough-’uns turn up the rein-
forcement his wad receives from the machine will
cany him on longer than the aid the bags produce.
The machine is being given a pretty raw deal by the
Government in N.S.W., but should it get any-
thing like fair patronage at Randwick this page
reckons the public are going to see some fairly excit-
ing figures when the favorites miss.
*** j *
Will the N.S.W. Government knock off
appointing new Legislative Councillors and
winning the war and all the rest of it for two
minutes and have a look at this totalisator business?
Surely there can be no possible excuse for tolerating
what is going on. Among other things the alteration
of totals is indefensible. At Victoria Park (Sydney)
last week after the first division of the 14.2 handicap
had been run the aggregate was given as 491. Then
it was changed to 488, and later on to 489. In the
meantime alterations were made in the totals on
various nags. Nothing could bring the machine into
greater disrepute than this sort of thing. It is
continually happening.
' * * * *
“Mujilt”: Of course what the totalisator
is doing on Sydney courses is pitiful. Compare a
turnover of £2500 on a day’s racing at Warwick
Farm with what goes on in Maoriland. There was
a two-days’ meeting lately at Timaru, and on each
day £ll,OOO was handled at the machine. Timaru
is a one-horse provincial town. Between a meeting
there and at Warwick Farm there is no point of
comparison in regard to betting, and there would
be at least 10 times as keen a betting crowd at the
Farm. At Wellington (M.L.) autumn meeting
£76,000 was handled at the machine in two days.
Yet there is far less wagering there than on a Sydney
suburban course. Either • the Government ought to
make its mind up to run the totalisator on a decent
revenue-producing basis or wheel the blessed thing
away. No one asked to have it set up for the pro-
motion of humor.
* * * *
“Trot”: I am only waiting to see what
happens when an embezzling cashier like the man (B.
10/6/’l7) who dumped £2500 into Ikey’s bag at
Randwick about Spurn and confided £l2OO to
MacShortodds about Cetigne arrives in front of the
totalisator and demands £1750 tickets on Stumer-
ilda. Of course one result will be to throw all the
arithmetic into chaos, but if another is not to set
every punter in sight searching his pockets for a
quid to deposit on the good thing, then the dull thud
at the end will be robbed of half its horribleness.
The Adelaide Cup meeting showed what
the totalisator can do under moderate conditions.
The S.A.J.C., through giving up its first day for the
Federal election, had to run the Cup on an off-day,
and was struck by a hailstorm on its last afternoon;
so the machine handled only £44,000 (before the
war it used to take half as much again). The Gov-
ernment’s cut at 5 per cent, (double what it was
before the war) was £2200, and the Jockey Club,
at its old 7$ per cent, rate, got £3300 less than
usual, but still a useful help towards the stake-
money. Winners had £38,500 out of the £44,000
divided among them. There were no sensational
dividends, because form was very consistent and the
public found not much difficulty in picking ’em.
However, £lB 18s. was handed out once, and the
backers of seconds did fairly well. The machine
divides up exactly on the lines lately advocated by
The Bulletin, one-fourth of the money available
going to backers of the second horse. Dividends
worked out this way: Ist, £7; 2nd, £6 165.; Ist,
£5 1?.; 2nd, £4 (in a field of eight) ; Ist, £3
16s. ; 2nd, £8 10s. (in a field of nine). Incidentally,
the odd coppers which the tote does not pay out
amounted in the three days to £2OO, which is
apportioned among charities.
* * * *
The double Derby winner Mountain King
got a big advt. as a sire at Randwick on Saturday.
Two of his family were winners. The first to
materialise was the Rajah of Pudukota’s King
ilostyn, which annexed the Hurdle Race after argu-
ment with a fair field. But a more attractive achieve-
ment was that of Mountain Lassie, which landed
the Two-year-old Handicap, six furlongs. This filly
streaked along the straight in fine style, and won
well in lmin. 15sec. This speedy, good-looking
young lady has not been knocked about in her
youth, and the writer puts her down among the
Derby possibilities for next year. She was bred
by her owner, John McDonald, at Mungie Biindie,
and is trained by Dick O’Comjor, who led Burradoo
in as a winner at Canterbury Park a week earlier.
Lady Swynford, the runner-up, won a race at Moore-
field lately, and did well to get so close with
Bst. 61b. on her back. She was conceding 121 b.
to Mountain Lassie. As the other place-getter, Nada
Light, is also a filly, the colts came to the conclu-
sion that they had been put in their places.
MacROBERTSON, the lolly king, practising
croquet.
It has been several times mentioned on
this page that Felix Booth’s nag Quarale was better
than most people believed. Further evidence in sup- '
port of this theory was produced at Randwick on
Saturday, when the judge detected Quarale (Bst.
31b.) half a neck ahead of Karanaud King (Bst.
91b.) in Tattersall’s Winter Stakes. Up to the dis-
tance it looked as if the King was running City
Tatt.’s Cup over again. Quarale is owned by an
uncle of Sam and Tony Hordern, who. have also won
events worth mentioning in these parts. The moke
has now won seven races this season, four being at
Randwick. Bombita seems to be a fraud. This
particular pommy has not won so far in Australia,
though he has run two or three good races. Still
he must have shown trainer Booth something out of
the ordinary lately, as he was served up a warm
favorite for the Winter Stakes, and occupied the same
position a week earlier in Newcastle Cup.
* * * *
Two imported prads spent a busy after-
noon at. Randwick on Saturday, Conquistador taking
the Flying and Snow King the Stakes. The first of
these originated in Yankeeland, coming to Australia
with the Wheatstone collection. He was sold for 190
guineas, has won races with welter weights aboard
in Bananaland, -and was twice in a place at New-
castle Cup meeiing. Snow King, by the once great
racehorse Willonvx, was backed to win a hatful at
long prices, and though the crowd he beat was not
the best in the world Snow King may be heard of
again. The nng is owned by two ladies. One of
them is Mrs. Keith, in whose name Quinn’s Post, a
winner at Canterbury Park the other day, also runs.
* * * *
_Clapator, a pony, was asked a big question
at Mudgee (N.S.W.) on Saturday. There were
five events on the programme, and Clapator com-
peted in three, all run over four furlongs. First
he won the Opening Handicap by two lengths, start-
ing at 4 to 1 against; an hour later he secured the
Pony Club Handicap at 3 to 1; in the last race
of the day he was beaten by a length. Clapator’s
weights were Bst. 51b., Bst. 121 b., and 9st. slb.
Probably the next to be heard of him will be having
a fly round Sydney courses.
* » * *
“Spreader”: The owner of Maoriland’s
champion Desert Gold must be losing his mozzle,
otherwise the mare would not have been started in
those two races she got beaten, in. Hitherto he had
been getting the business of placing his nags down
to a fine art. Lowry bought Bobrikoff and Balboa
from his trainer Davis after their ability to hurry
was beyond dispute. He bred the M.L. Cup winner
Downfall, about the luckiest moderate that ever
floated home in front of a good field. There have
been some very lucky moderates in front at the end
of that race, too. Desert Rose, another of Lowry’s,
was a good mare, but owed a lot of her success to
judicious placing. Lowry’s name figures more among
the owners of small cup and big handicap winners
than among the proprietors of “classic’’ winners.
He has been racing many years, but never had a
nag, except Desert Gold, that could mix it with the
mokes bred by Stead, Clifford or Ormond.
Private J. D. Anderson, of Ballarat, writes
to tell how his cobber Dave Ross, a corporal in the
same battalion, won the D.C.M. at Pozieres: “Dave,
who used to put the weight and toss the caber at
Highland sports, is as strong as a lion. He was on
outpost duty when six Germans popped out of a shell-
hole and went for him. His three mates could not
reach him, but he stuck to his post and went for
the Huns. Pie bayoneted five and closed with the
sixth, who during the struggle bit his nose, ear, chin
and a finger nearly off. Then lie grabbed the Hun,
lifted him up, banged him heavily on the ground,
picked up his bayonet, and —well, pick the winner.
On Melbourne Boat Race day, Neville
Fairbairn Armytage, who rowed with Geelong Gram-
mar in 1915-16, strolled into the- Town Hall recruit-
ing depdt with the colors of his old school flying,
and enlisted. He is 20; one of the South Yarra
Armytages. As soon as the news reached his
pal, Eric Donald Bowler, 18, of Annandale
South Station, Holbrook (N.S.W.), he concluded that
his place was in the same vicinity. So he enlisted.
The lads were in the school’s 1915 football team,
and they will go into the artillery together.
* * * *
Back from the greatest rush he had ever
been in, Charlie McMurtrie, a many-times representa-
tive New South Wales llugbyite. Charles fled to the
war nearly two years ago, and returns minus a lump
of shoulder-blade. Coming to Sydney from Orange in
1908, he got a place with the Wallabies who toured
Britain that year and part of the next. Later on, he
followed the money, and went over to the League, and
again distinguished himself. A place was found for
him among the Kangaroos of 1911-12 for the tour
of England. On that trip McMurtrie played in only
seven matches and scored three tries. Being a cove
who just simply had to have a punch when anything
was doing, he happened to be twice ordered off the
field. The first time was against Hunslet, for which
he was suspended for one week. The second occasion
was at Swinton, in Lancashire, when the local cham-
pion wrestler tried to hurl Mac. through space with
the assistance of the “flying mare,” Rastlin’ being a
game of McMurtrie’s, the Swinton player had his wind
knocked -out. A month later McMurtrie had his
shoulder hurt at Runcorn (Cheshire), and was out of
action for the rest of the tour. Before going to "the
Front McMurtrie played for Balmain, and he declares
there is a shove in the old kit yet.
* * * *
“Musketoon”: Just about the time a
Sydney parson was dealing out language to sport of
all sorts (B. 17/5/’l7), this is what the Rev. L.
Dawson Thomas, C.E. clergyman, was saying at
Dtibbo (N.S.W.) at the funeral of jockey D. Pink,
who had been killed by a fall off the racehorse Grey
Sim, at Tottenham, Forbes: “Many persons had a
wrong idea of men who followed the sporting pro-
fession. I have met many good and worthy men con-
nected with sport. If a man leads a clean iife, it does
not matter what profession he follows; he is worthy
of the honor and respect of every citizen of the com-
munity.” This sounds better than the other fellow’s
rant. The dead jockey left a widow and a couple of
youngsters, and already Western sports have sub-*
scribed enough cash to ensure placing the widow in a
little business of her own.
* * * «
“0.K.”: Something more than anti-con-
scription sentiment is charged against the football
bal-rackers of the Labor strongholds in Melbourne Ijy
the lecruiting sergeants. Five of them who were
told off to harangue the crowds at Fitzroy, Colling-
wood arid South Melbourne the other Saturday told
the same tale of rowdy interruptions and personal
hostility. At Fitzroy and South Melb. the orating
Bill jims were actually assaulted, more or less.
Truculent shirkers threatened to deal with them,
and the recruiters' left to escape the “deal.” The
verdict of the sergeants is complimentary to race-
course punters as compared with the other sort of
“sports.” Those who follow the horses are said to
be “gentlemen” beside shirkers in the football line
of enthusiasm. Which ought not to be so very
surprising as it appears. Football crowds are
notoriously intolerant .of plain facts: Good bar-
rackers are bad losers. The average habitual punter
is a philosopher who will listen to reason—after the
race, but the football barracker is a different sort of
ass. He fastens his mind to his club colors or party
dogma, and reckons it his duty as a man to howl the
opposition down.
* *• * #
Yes, glory be, the Church gets a cut along
with the rest, and a fairly healthy cut, too, some-
times. The puppy, Beauty’s Pride,'which landed the
Metropolitan Stakes at Liverpool (Sydney), last
week, is the property of a great sport who wears a
clerical collar above black. The pup was backed for
a good solid wad at the call of the card, before a
course had been run, 100’s to 4 and later 7 to 1
straight out being taken. It is stated that this was
due to a trial. of Beauty’s Pride with the seasoned
hound Achieved, which went within a whisker of
winning last Waterloo Cup. The pup did not
beat Achieved, but did so well that those in the
know girded up their loins and went in pursuit of
Mo forthwith.
* * * *
In her earlier courses Beauty’s Pride did
not show to great advantage, wherefore there were
good odds offering on each flutter. But on the second
day she snuffed out Comedy Chief, the Derby winner
and favorite for the stake, whereupon all Israel sat
up with a jerk, and took notice of what happened
next. The next two courses she won easily, and by
the time lunch was ready the final began to look
a cert. The: puppy was taken for a walk by her
trainer during the smoke-o, and on being brought
back to hemkennel promptly leaped at a large piece
of steak-which someone, had left there during her
absence. But the steak was captured by the trainer
before it vanished, and conveyed to the stewards for
analysis or something. If there was anything else
but meat it didn’t go down, and the pup won bril-
liantly. Beauty’s Pride, whelped in October, 1915, is
by White Hope from The Bride. It was her first
appearance in public.
*. * * *
“Mffrkah”: At Rooty Hill (N.S.W.)
Plumpton the other day I inspected the hare about
to be chased for its life in the last course of the
day; time, 4 p.m. It was “sitting,” cramped, in
one of a series of small coops, into which it had
been driven at 9 a.m. Therefore, for seven weary
hours this unfortunate animal had waited, wet and
cold (there had been a heavy rainstorm) for its
turn. The uproar of shouting bookmakers and whin-
ing dogs must have scared so timid an animal nearly
to death. What chance had it in the unequal contest
which followed? It hadn't one in a row of figures.
Once a hare is put into an enclosure, though it may
survive a dozen courses, inevitably it is caught. It
must be a peculiar brand of logic that permits this
inhuman business of coursing while prohibiting
cockfighting, where, at any rate, the odds are equal.
* * * *
It was a pair of feathers again at Syd-
ney Stadium on Saturday—Sid Godfrey' and Tommy
Ryan. In a previous conflict at Brisbane Thomas
found it was all over when he asked what happened
while the 11th chapter was being read. On Saturday
Godfrey went in to make it happen some more, and
was met by' a Ryan who was mostly absent when
Sid knocked, but returned promptly at the right
interlude. The verdict went to Thomas. Perhaps
Godfrey is the better of the pair, but the habit he
has fallen into of punishing the atmosphere is not
going to win him many fights.
The best judge of Dave Meekin’s chance
against Herb McCoy at Brisbane Stadium on Satur-
day was a Bilijim who passed him up a lump of
hardwood in the eighth breather. David’s munitions
consisted of a left flick to the nose and a hook that
mostly failed. As lie presented these with his head
down McUoy spent a profitable evening. After cas-
ually uppercutting the foe in the ninth round Herbert
was able to enjoy the pleasures of solitude.
“Mujik”: So the uncle of “Victo” (B.
17/5/’l7) walked from Back Creek station to Rigs-
dale, near Goulburn, 108 miles, in 32 hours, did-lie?
Well, he was a pretty good doer. But he can’t beat
my uncle Bill. The old man got -from Kigsdale to
Back Creek in 31 hours. Per boot? Well, he says
so anyway. When I mentioned that the world's record
fqr a 24 hours’ stroll was 130 miles he said that was
done on a flat track by a trained athlete and didn’t
count, anyhow. And when someone said uncle might
have abandoned his feet and ridden seven mokes in
succession he got as wild as a brumby'. By heavens,
they were made of good stuff, those old chaps!

From “depot orders” of a Bilijim training-
camp somewhere in Britain: “Increase of strength of

mules, 22 horses. Decrease in strength: 1 mule
having died is struck off. Absent without leave: 1

“Musketoon”: Tell “Ponto” that when a
rabbit is shot on the forehead from a head-on posi-
tion (B. 10/5/’l7), the reason a hind leg is usually
broken if the ground be hard is because he “goes
back” sufficiently when the charge gets him that the
hind leg nearest the ground snaps when the con-
tact takes place. If the shooting takes place on well
green-grassed land the grass forms a cushion for the
recoil, and the leg will not snap. I have seen this
proved in Forbes district (N.S.W.).
* * * *
“V.”: Ilock-fishers complain of the grow-
ing scarcity of snapper. Say 10 years ago the rock-
hopper was pretty sure of a good red-fish or two any
time between 4 o’clock and sundown off good feed-
ing rounds. In those clays the fish would scale up
to 181 b. or over; now the majority caught from the
shore are between 51b. and 81b. Immature snapper
(the red-bream or “cocky” of line fishers) are taken
wholesale in tidal lakes, inlets and estuaries, and
as a result the supply of fully-developed fish wanes.
In Wallaga Lake (N.S.W.) gins from the aboriginal
mission station have taken nearly 200 red-
bream in the course of an afternoon. The
“bull-tow” is another destructive factor. This
engine consists of a line buoyed with corks and
armed with from 200 to 300 baited hooks set in
the vicinity of good grounds. When in biting mood
snapper are not particular as to bait. I have taken
them freely with the tail ends of sweep, rabbit-flesh
and fish entrails; but squid, or “star-fish” (imma-
ture devil-fish), crab, mullet and mutton fish arc
the most attractive foods. With a rising tide snap-
per may be occasionally taken off rocky beaches
even with the summer sun directly overhead, but the
ps.vchological hour for the fisherman is early' morning
or late evening.
* * * *
“Ossian” : In the placid waters of Station
Creek, the rivulet ,in North Queensland upon which
the mining town of Chillagoe is found, there is a
rare kind of fish. It is palled locally the rifle fish,
deriving the name from the fact that it shoots a
pellet of water at its prey. The fly that wings its
wav at an altitude of 18 inches to 2ft. above the
surface of Station Creek falls stunned when struck
and is promptly gobbled up. The submarine pro-
vides some amusement for the reel and rod fisherman
by its vigorous bombardments, directed at his float.
Ihe curiosity is easily caught, and its pound or two
of white flpsh makes good eating. Is it known else-
in Ai-strnlia?

Why Have Grey Hair?
When a simple, old-fashioned and harmless
recipe will correct it.
Few people know that grey hair is not a
necessary feature of age—that it can be
avoided without resorting to hair dyes A
very old, home-made remedy will turn the
hair back to a natural color in a few days.
It is only necessary to get from the chemist
two ounces of concentrate of tammalite and
mix it with three ounces of bay rum. Apply
this simple lotion to the hair for a few
nights with a small sponge and you 'will
soon have the pleasure of seeing the greyness
disappear. This recipe is perfectly harmless,
is neither sticky nor greasy, and has given
perfect satisfaction for man3' generations to
those in possession of the secret. — Advt. [4

Having written off half the capital,
which was represented only by a shock-
ingly bad bargain, arid paid off the
last of a small 7 p.c. (formerly 8 p.c.)
debenture issue, the d : rectors should now
be able to pay good dividends so long as
they can get the right people to handle the
business for them. Originally the co. was
formed to run a cafe that young Sydney
went to when it wanted to do the thing
properly. . Subsequently it bought a hotel
license with leasehold attached; then, dis-
covering that it was easier to make money
out. of liquor than food, it let the gilded caf6
to its old boss, and nowadays merely runs
the pub. Its assets are—Leasehold and
plant, £7972; stocks, £2037; sundries,
£923. Liabilities on March 31 were £2207.
The position is thus still poor. The co.
may make money: but if it doesn’t its
assets are worth very little indeed.
*** # #
Ghoiseul Plantations, Ltd. —This is
the plantation co. that nearly takes your
breath. away. Every other Islands co. moans
interminably about the indisposition of the
native to bog in and earn money to buy
things he doesn’t want. This one tells the
other story once more:—
The year under review has been one of quiet,
steady progress, the seasons having been good and
the supply of labor adequate... .The position as to
native labor continues satisfactory, the total during
the year having increased from 1141 to 1241; the
new engagements being 342 and the time-expired
men paid off and returned to their homes being 242.
The co. was promoted by Burns, Philp and
Co. to make money (1) for itself by growing
copra, and (2) for 8., P. and Co. by pro-
viding Island trade. Britain had her own
ideas with regard to the importation of in-
dentured labor in her part of the Solomons;
Germany had hers. Apparently this co. liked
the German ideas best; anyway, it went into
the German part of the Solomons in 1912
and arranged with the Government to buy
12,500 acres. The British military authori-
ties are completing the survey; and presum-
ably the title will be all right, though how
the co. will get on if'the German gets back
to those old possessions of his the manage-
ment probably doesn’t care to think. Per-
haps it is consoling itself with the reflection
that, if the German is the old German, he
won’t care how many indentured natives are
brought over from New Guinea, or anywhere
else, so long as the islands are developed. It
was that, of course, which sent the co. to
the German part of the group in the first
place; and it was that, too, which is mainly
responsible for the fact that the co. has now
3949 acres planted and another 723 acres
wholly or partly ready for planting—far
swifter progress than the B. P. subsidiary
cos. have been able to make in the British
Solomons.
The co. spent £18,116 last vear, and made
three calls of £5 each = £15,000. In the
current year it expects to have to make four
calls = £2O per share, which will make the
£lOO shares paid to £BO and leave onlv
£20,000 to call. But the older part of the
plantation is now almost ready to give a
first harvest; so the capital will probably be
sufficient.

There is something wrong in the State of
Damnark when a drinking-shop that cost
five or six thousand to build and ought to
oost a couple of hundred to pull down can
make a profit for the landlord and the
lessee of six or eight thousand a year, while
a fine residential pub like the Pacific can
only make a loss of £1092. And this paper
isn’t sure that the true loss was not more
flian £1092; it all depends unon whether
enough was written off to provide for depre-
ciation, and there are grave doubts about
that. The freeho’d and building stand in
the books at £44.280: the furniture and
plant at another £12.740. The share capital
is £35.000: £20.000 is owing on debentures
and overdraft and other liabilities have now
pushed up to £"699. as asrainst £4931 12
months ago. War, dear liquor and provi-
sions, 6 o’clock closing, an unfavorable
season and a few other things are blamed
for the bad result. Maybe somebody else
could make a profit. Anyway, there' it is
and the shareholders must be feeling sorry
for themselves.

'T'HIS charming selection—perhaps not intrinsically a very
great composition —has spread the sweetness of its
haunting strains over the whole world. It possesses a depth
of feeling so elusive that frequently the finer effects are
lost. In its execution the expression is its all.
With a Beale Player-piano you will
find that it is not at all hard to play
music with characteristics similar to
the “Barcarolle.” It is an instrument
essentially responsive to one’s best
musical thoughts, and is so sympa-
thetic that complete, soul-satisfying
rendition becomes almost instinctive.

SYDNEY.
BOOTS have come back again and no mistake.
But such dreams of boots, up-to-date,
dainty, delightful, fit accompaniment to the
flounced taffeta frocks of the day, which
combine smart modernity with old-world charm.
Paris and London both are now tripping to work
and play in short-fronted, high-heeled, suede-
topped boots like the Bostock style here illus-
trated.
It is a smart little boot, there is no doubt
about that, and every bit as comfortable as it is
smart. The beautifully fitting last on which it is
made sees to that, and lots of women, who have
never imagined they could wear a short-fronted
style, will be amazed to find how comfortable it
is, how jolly it makes their feet look.
Then for those who want something different,
who prefer shoes perhaps, there is a fine range of
styles to select from, all as up-to-date and charm-
ing as this model, and all .safe to wear well and
feel as peculiarly, one’s own as if they had been
made to measure.

A New Synthetic Substance
For the Soles of Boots and Shoes
Will outlast two pairs of Leather Soles.
Not Rubber. Not Leather. Not Rubberised Leather.
It is Neolin. An Entirely New Substance.
Neolin was born of necessity, the mother of
invention.
Never was tho need of the world so great
for something better than leather for the
soles of shoes.
You know how shoe prices have soared in
the past five years.
And you may have heard how shoe manu-
facturers have had to fight —especially since
the motor car came —for their share of the
world’s supply of choice hides.
You will understand, then, why manufac-
turers have welcomed Neolin wdth open arms.
As has always happened when an economic
pressure becomes almost too hard to bear,
science came to the rescue with a new syn-
thetic substance —better than leather for the
soles of shoes.
Its name is Neolin, and it comes to fill an
old want in a new and a better way.
Shoe manufacturers, through their salesmen,
are telling the Neolin story now to shoe
dealers all over Australia.
They are taking orders for the season to
come.
They are endorsing Neolin wholeheartedly
and with enthusiasm.
And why should they not ?
There is nothing they could say of the
choicest leather that they are not justified in
saying with added emphasis of Neolin.
And Neolin has advantages and virtues all
its own.
If it were merely as good as good leather,
that would be enough to make it noteworthy
—because the supply is inexhaustible.
But imagine a substance which is not rubber,
which is not leather, but which is better
than rubber, better than leather, and which
is waterproof as well.
That is Neolin —a watertight shoe sole that
is also light and flexible—that gives a glove-
fitting sensation to the sole of the foot even
when the shoe is new.
We are entrusting the introduction of Neolin
to the shoe manufacturers and dealers of
Australasia.
Shoes fitted with these fine soles will come
to you through your regular trade channels.
The task of soling the shoes in Six Austra-
lian States is too vast to be accomplished in
any other way.
These announcements are merely to acquaint
you with the great tasks of distribution which
these manufacturers and dealers have under-
taken.
All we ask of you is that you will show your
goodwill by mentioning to your dealer the
interest you must feel in Neolin, and make
personal investigation by inspecting a pair
of Neolin-soled boots or shoes.
Every Neolin Sole and Half-sole is stamped Neolin. To be sure of getting the genuine Heolin, mark that mark-stamp it on your memory.
Fleolin
Better than Leath'
the trade symbol and registered name of a changeless quality product of Goodyear now obtainable from
The Goodyear Tyre 8 Rubber Co. of Australasia Ltd.

USE PINE TAR FOR COUGHS AND
COLDS.
A SPECIALIST’S ADVICE.
Chronic coughs, catarrh, throat and
bronchial troubles that resist all ordinary
remedies usually yield at once to the sooth-
ing, healing and stengthening influence of
the medicinal properties contained in tar
and pine. All these properties, as well as
several other valuable remedial agents, are
found in a syrup made by dissolving half a
pound of sugar in half a pint of hot water,
and then adding 2oz. of ordinary Utrate of
tar. Stir thoroughly, pour into a pint
bottle, and, when cool, it is ready for use.
One or two teaspoonfuls of this simple and
inexpensive cough syrup will quickly over-
come a bad cough, and that annoying tick-
ling in the throat will disappear almost
instantly. Children should take from a
quarter to half of the above quantities sev-
eral times a day. The fact that titrate ' of
tar is entirely free from opium, morphine,
cocaine, heroin or other poisonous habit-
forming drugs to which scientists and
physicians so strongly object, is doubtless
one of the reasons why its use is becoming
almost universal. — Advt.

CHANDLER
SIX
Goes 6725 Miles Over Bad
Roads Without Costing
Penny for Repairs!
PRICE
£490.
“I heard a really remarkable story recently about
a really remarkable performance of an equally remark-
able car,” writes an Atlanta (Georgia) man. “Dr.
J. W. Livingston, of New York City, told me the
story. I refer to Dr. Livingston as being from New
York City, when, as a matter of fact, the doctor
simply has his headquarters in the Big Village and
trots from one corner of these old United States to
the other, and sometimes, if the notion strikes him,
he ambles up into Canada for a change.
“The last time Dr. Livingston passed through
Atlanta he purchased a brand-new Chandler Six.
Then the travelling bee got into his bonnet and he
burned the trail from here to Canada. But I am
getting ahead of my story, and I’ll let Dr. Livingston
tell it. I quote him accurately, I think, but I can-
no make it so entertaining because I cannot draw
the vivid picture that he did in his pleasantly charac-
teristic manner. The doctor said
“ ‘I left Atlanta in my new Chandler and travelled
through the Virginia mountains to Washington, D.C.
“ ‘From Washington I went to New York and up
through the New England States. Business called
me back to New York, and I spent a day there and
left immediately for Buffalo. From there I went to
Hamilton, Ont. Then I went to Montreal, thence to
Quebec, to the Thousand Islands. Upon my arrival
there I returned bv exactly the same route to Atlanta,
and when I rolled'into the Southern Saxon garage my
speedometer registered 6725 miles, and I hadn’t spent
a penny on the car except for gasoline and oil.
“ ‘lf that isn’t a remarkable record I never heard
of one. I have owned more costly cars than the
Chandler, but I have never owned a better one. I
wouldn’t sell my Chandler for $lO,OOO if I thought I
couldn’t buy another one.’ ”

IN BANANA LAND
“Cubbie”: Legislative Councillors and
their friends have been offering up incense
for their escape from obliteration and for
the return of Phewsion. On Sunday there
was a thanksgiving service in one of the
city churches aud a public meeting was also
held to gloat over “the triumph of loyalty.”
When Brentnall, M.L.C., was pouring out
praise to Providence for the defeat of the
Labor party at the Federal elections he
omitted to explain why Labor had been
allowed to win the Rockhampton by-election
on May 12. Perhaps the Phews crowd had
been found out by Providence during the
week.
Some day a Brisbane lawyer who knows
his job will incite a client to purchase some
local icecream, aud to then serve the __man
behind the counter with a claim for £17,000
damages on account of damage done. to the
interior. When a health-inspector visited a
few shops the other day he got helpings of
alleged cream which on examination proved,
in five cases, to be inhabited by micro-organ-
isms ranging in number from 9,450,000 per
cubic centimetre to 51,975,000. What hap-
pens to anyone who swallows 51,975.000 of
these animals was not explained, but the
Beak said he was going to put down the
practice of selling zoological collections
wrapped in frozen liquid, so he handed out
fines of £lO and £2O. The real cure for the
dirty restaurant and the cream adulterated
with microbes is the cancelled license.
A promising scholastic and athletic career
has been cut short by the death on active
service of Alex. Noble, of Paddington, Bris-
bane. A local Grammar School boy. and a
student in science at Queensland ’Varsity,
Noble was 21 when he climbed on to a
limber of the Field Artillery two years ago.
lie could bend an oar, too, and splashed for
Queensland in an inter-State eight.
“P.”: David Hardie, M.D., lectured the
Presbyterian Assembly recently on 'The Pre-
vention of Venereal Diseases.” Said he: —
To prevent the spread of the disease there should
be education, both from a moral and public health
point of view. In giving instruction, distinction
should be made between those who had reached the
age of adolescence and those who had not done so.
As to the suggestion that a certificate of good
health should be required before parties were allowed
to be married it did not follow that the contractors
would remain clean after marriage. ... It was for the
Presbyterian Church L • decide whether it was advis-
able to insist on deni*: ding a clean certificate before
performing the maru.cge contract, but in view of the
fact that Queensland had such, a splendid radical Act
he thought it uould he a mistake for the clergy to
insist on such a certificate.
Although Queensland has such a “splendid
radical Act,” it is none the less notorious

WEARIED CLUBMAN: “Well, I suppose you need it. You have to listen to yourself like

that Brisbane in the last two years has
simply reeked with disease. Putting a
Bill through Parliament and administering it
are two very different matters. In my opinion
if the churches demanded clean health-certifi-
cates before perform'ng the marriage cere-
mony it would be to their eternal credit; but,
as a matter of fact, this is not a matter for
the Church at all, but for the State, No
marriage license should be issued by the
Registrar without the previous production of
clean certificates. Better infinitely the
matrimonial disqualification of a few. hun-
dred infected persons than the production of
crops of tainted weaklings.
The Lowland Woman.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bird-bright were my eyes with watching, as
beseemed a mountain-dweller,
Where the besom of the ice wind scours the
clefts, a mist-expeller,
Wise was I in wide earth places, chinks in
rocks a ml eagles’ nes ts;
And the canny Alpine mosses clinging to the
granites’ crests.
Half the world my window showed me, past
the pines in columns gloomy,
Stunted firs the heights have mastered, aud
a plainland far and roomy.
Over there a glittering hamlet; over there a
rimless sea,
Till the woman left the lowland and came up
the hills to me.
Iu her eyes were rushy meadows, kine
among the dusky grasses;
Weeds in midstreams greenly swaying, where
a swallow’s shadow passes,
On her bosom ruddy earthstains, in her
hands a valley rose ;
On her lips a store of perfumes, garnered
where the lily grows.
Slow she moved, like cloud at morning, when
the south wind idly dreams,
And her presence breathed of woodlands and
of diowsy sunlit streams,
Not of edelweiss nor gentian, strangers of
the upper air,
But of violets damp in corners; pools set
round with maidenhair;
Dancing lights on cabin windows, glow-
worms burning in the dark ;
Treading down the crackling needles, 'twixt
the pine boles, brown and stark,
Moving like a doomed to-morrow from the
earth-valves and the lea;
With a red rose in her fingers she came up
the hills to me.
For a space we spoke of valleys, for she liked
my mountain eyes.
And I set within her fillet one pale bloom of
edelweiss,
And I drank of earth-bees’ honey in the
hiving of her lips ;
Towns we talked instead of mountains, and
instead of oceans, ships ;
But the upper air grew chilly, and she
shivered from my breast,
With my token on her forehead, down
towards the vale she prest.
God has set a moon this evening like a ghost
between the trees.
And the plain is white and splendid, out
towards the calling seas,
And the light of Heaven’s garden through
its golden oriels flows ;
But my heart is valley-tethered to the
whisper of a rose!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, May 19, 1917.
My Dear Thabgoaiindah, —
The business of filling the vacancies in the
Legislative Council is Keeping a number of
people in the partial limeiigut. A newspaper
proprietor and a defeated Labor feenator
are said to be favorites; and every mail
brings the Premier so much correspondence
on the subject that the alleged paper famine
appears to be a myth. In the meantime the
cultivation of flowing beards is a thriving
industry, for no hairless jaw a chance
to wag in our ancient House of Whiskers.
A movement has been started to provide
a home for discharged wounded soldiers who
have left hospital before being completely
cured. The notion is a good one; but as
the public started with enthusiasm some
months ago to get a Returned Soldiers and
Sailors’ Residential Club, it might be well to
finish one thiug before getting on to another.
A site has been bought for the club, the
foundation stone is to be laid on May 24
and about £5OOO is wanted in order to hand
the show over free of debt. In the circum-
stances the home for the nearly-cured might
wait.
Mrs. J. Muntz Adams, sole female in-
cluded among the artists who received com-
missions to paint portraits of notables of
the Australian Parliament has been
appointed chief instructor at the Art
School of the Central Technical College. She
was trained at the Melbourne Gallery School,
studied under Julian and Delacluse in Paris
and Herkomer in London. An exhibition
of her work is on view at the Technical
College.
The committee of the 31st Battalion Com-
forts Fund entertained Mrs. Toll (wife of
Colonel Toll, D. 5.0.) at a farewell tea in
the Botanic Gardens yesterday afternoon.
The lady left amid many regretful goodbyes
and a keepsake.
Pte. Bruce MacGregor, of the A.A.M.C.,
is back in Brisbane after 2\ years of active
service. Before donning the uniform be
pushed a pen on the Daily Mail. In the
true Anzac way be will say nothing about
himself, but is eloquent over the doings of
others.
Sara Allgood o’ my Heart was at home
at the rooms of the Allies’ Babies’ Clothing
Society yesterday morning. The committee
presented her with a beautiful basket of
roses laced with green ribbons; and Mike,
the Irish terrier, was decorated with an
emerald bow. Each guest brought along a
small shirt, a pair of bootees or some other
infant garment to be sent later on to little
refugees; and cash also materialised.
Mrs. Gilbert Wilson, our own stately
soprano, who was to have been heard at
the Musical Union concert on May 29, has
decided not to sing again in public until the
war finishes. Like many a mother she puts
a good face on things, but the strain of the
long anxiety is beginning to tell.
Died on May 11. Frederick Holmes
a’Court, son of the late Lord Hytesbury. As
a young man he held a commission in a foot
regiment; but for about 40 years he was a
resident of Queensland, where he worked as
a miner in the North and afterwards settled
down to the simple life near Mt. Chalmers.
His brother, C. G. Holmes a’Court, is clerk
of the legislative Assembly.
A massive bronze honor-board containing
the names of 305 past and present scholars
of the Toowoomba Grammar School has just
been erected in front of the Brisbane Post
Office. But why at Brisbane Post Office?
Hasn’t Toowoomba a prior right to the
bronze tablet containing the names of
Toowoomba boys?
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Exit, at close on 80 years, Ben 11. Burton,
of Clayfield (Brisbane), formerly a well-
known officer of the State Railway Depart-
ment. The service was in its babyhood when
Burton started as foreman of works at
.Tondaryan in ’6B, rising to traffic-inspector
a few years later. After a spell in another
service he went on the rails again in ’95
as chief traffic-inspector, a job which he held
until he retired about nine years ago. A
son is in the Chinese railway service.
Toowoomba is mourning the passing of
two of its best-known townsmen: Fred
Borton, sen., one of the pioneers of the
Darling Downs, who had managed to survive
the fervor of the Queensland sun to the
brief age of 92; and Walter Pengally, an
ex-Maorilander, who roamed a lot before
settling in the Downs city.
“Billy Blue”: Re William Knox D’Arcy,
the lucky lawyer -of Rockhampton, who has
gone t’other side at 68. He came to Queens-
land as a youth in 1866, was articled and
admitted to the “lower” profession, and put
up his sign in Rockhampton. When the
Morgans struck gold they engaged young
D’Arcy to find the cash to work their mine.
He found £5OO, and a friend found another
monkey. The Morgans sold out, but D’Arcy
hung on to his quarter-share in the mountain
of gold, and will no doubt pan out a million-
aire. His son Frank married one of the
Raring girls, a family who has at its head
Lord Ashburton.

The Colonial Bank of Australasia Limited
THE FORTY-EIGHTH REPORT OF THE DIRECTORS OF
THE COLONIAL BANK OF AUSTRALASIA LIMITED.
Presented to the Shareholders at the Forty-eighth Ordinary General Meeting, held at the Bank, 126
Elizabeth Street, Melbourne, at noon on Monday, 30th April, 1917.
REPORT.
The Directors beg to submit to the Shareholders their Forty-eighth Report, with a Balance-Sheet and
Statement of Profit and Loss for the Half-year ended 31st March, 1917, duly audited.
After providing for expenses of management, bonus to staff, interest accrued on deposits, rebate on
bills current, tax on note circulation, income taxes, land taxes and making provision for bad and
doubtful debts, the net profit amounted to .. .. .. .. .. .. .. £27,024 2 9
Brought forward from 30th September, 1916 .. . . .. . . . . - ■ 5,483 12 2
£32,507 14 11
Your Directors propose to pay out of the half-yearly profits the following dividend: —
On Preference Shares at the rate of 7 per cent, per annum . . . . ■ .. £10,641 10 9
On Ordinary Shares at the rate of 7 per cent, per annum . . . . .. . • 4,733 5 6
The balance of the half-year's profits they recommend to be applied as follows: —
To Reserve Fund (making it £280,000). . .. .. .. .. .. .. 10,000 0 0
~ Officers’ Provident Fund .. . . . . . . . . . . .. .. 1,000 0 0
„ Increase amount at credit of Profit and Loss Account by £649 6s. 6d.,
making it .. .. .. .. .. . - -. . • • • ■ • 6,132 18 8
£32,507 14 11
The Directors desire to place on record their regret at the death of Mr. Selby Paxton, which
occurred on the 20th November last, after an illness of brief duration, and to express their appreciation
of the sterling service he rendered to the Bank with his ability and energy during the long period he so
ably occupied the position of general manager.
Mr. James Wilson, the assistant general manager, has been appointed by the Board to succeed the
late Mr. Paxton as general manager.
Owing to ill-health, Mr. J. C. Duigan, one of the auditors, was unable to act this half-year. The
Balance-Sheet is therefore signed by his co-auditor, Mr. John Bishop, only.
The dividend will be payable at the head office on and after the Ist May, and at the branches on
receipt of advice.
The Forty-eighth Ordinary General Meeting of Shareholders will he held at the head office of the
Company, 126 Elizabeth Street. Melbourne, on Monday, the 30th day of April, 1917, at noon.
Bv order of the Board,
Melbourne, 20th April, 1917.
J. WILSON, General Manager.
