The unconventional lady curer who lately
has been restoring Harrie Ireland to health
and spirits was aggrieved by a Bulletin
statement that the long-suffering actress is
still “under medical treatment.” It is meta-
physical doctoring that has wrought the im-
provement in patient Harrie, she insists. The
lady practitioner is a metaphysician, if you
please, and the Association she belongs to
demands that no credit shall be. given to
“medical science,” as the term is generally
understood. Harrie Ireland’s case is very
interesting, anyhow, and those who know
most about it are the most impressed.
“Staff-sergeant”: Dig up a King’s Regs,
(page 112), and you will find that field pun-
ishment (known as “Crucifixion”) is a legal
award for an O.C.tomake. After that, root out
Field Service Pocket Book and page 211 will
tell you that an offender undergoing F.P. No.
1 may be (o) kept in irons, (1>) attached
by straps, irons or ropes for not more than
two hours in one day to a fixed object. Must
not be attached for more than three out of
four consecutive days, or for more than 21
days in all. 1 have no knowledge of any
Australian soldiers having had to undergo
F.P. No. 1 during this war, but the Tommies
are tied up occasionally.
Members of the Permanent Artillery, the
Engineers, A.S.C., and Instructional staff
seem to have a very real grievance against
the Defence Department. Some who are
anxious to go to the war are not allowed to
quit their present sphere of usefulness, and
all of them are kept on their <>ld salaries
whilst other public servants have had their
pay raised in accordance with the increased
cost of living. Military staff clerks are much
better off', though they do the same work as
the permanent men. The complaint is only
another illustration of the muddle and mix
in the Department where Pearce sometimes
saves the shillings and other times leaves the
pounds to be squandered.
At a recent Red Cross fete five guineas
were spent on the hire of red carpet for the
vice-regal feet, and for other similar extras,
while the hard-up vocalists—all well-known
professionals—received nothing for three
guineas’ worth of music each. Three years
ago Queen Mary publicly stated that she
would not attend any function whatever
unless the artists were paid a minimum fee,
of one guinea. Won’t somebody ask the
Australian Exes, to lay down the same
rule?
“Snell”: Burnet Grey, who put up some-
thing of a record in being a writer of very
respectable verse and a Melb. suburban coun-
cillor at the same time, has added himself to
the lengthy list of Bulletin contributors at
the Front. Burnet is one of three Grey
brothers in khaki. One passed out at the
Somme; another, still battling at the West
Front, has been wounded four times (catch-
ing on the fourth occasion a dose of shrapnel
that left him paralysed for two months), but
each time has been promptly drafted back to
the fighting-line as soon as he felt his feet
under him.
The Northern Rivers of N. S. Wales,
where the Win-the-War party is impreg-
nable, is worried over the erosion of the
banks of the various waterways, and big
expense will be needed to protect them. So
immediately the elections were over a con-
ference was held in Sydney to settle who
was to find the money. The first idea was
that the people who owned the damaged land
should pay, but this, naturally, was howled
down. Then it was proposed that the trad-
ing-steamer cos. should shell out. This was
received with loud applause, until someone
pointed out that the cos. would only hit up
the freights. Then someone suggested that
the shires should foot the bill, tut he was
hooted down in quick time. Then it was
moved that the State bear the expense. A
few timid persons objected that this wasn’t
the way to Win the War, but they weren’t
listened to, and it was unanimously agreed
that the ear of the Government be chewed
immediately.
“Croson” makes a complaint and a sug-
gestion :
Isn’t it about time the promises of preference to
returned soldiers were put into practice? These men
don’t all want to go on the land, even though a
gaudy price has been paid for it; some of them
wouldn’t know what to do with the richest farm
ever bought at a boom-price from a rich landlord ;
but when they apply for railway work which they
can do, and are told, as some of them have been in
Victoria, that they must be under 40 and pass a
medical examination, it is time somebody got busy at
a job of chucking out. If a man is young enough
and fit enough to fight, provided he is not in-
capacitated he is quite fit for most railway jobs.
There must surely be a lot of porters, shunters,
goods-clerks and the like who have gone away and
have not yet returned —why are their places not filled
by returned men? If the recruiting people devoted
half their time to righting the grievances of re-
turned men, they would find that the other half of
their time would yield better results than their whole
time docs now.
“8.D.5.”: When the war is over I'm
going to settle the prickly-pear with field-
guns. Turpenite, an explosive invented by
the French, is of .so deadly a nature that
they won’t use it while fighting in their own
country, the reason being that it destroys
all vegetation and nothing will grow on the
soil affected for eight years. Surely a dose
of turp. should give our old friend the real
spike.
Norman Bayles, the elect of Toorak, had
his tie disordered the other day by railway-
expert Johnson. Bayles got in front of the
Commission as a critic of railway finance,
and speedily became so bogged that Johnson
grew sarcastic. But that didn’t bump
Norman out of the way. He opined next
that the absence of any fund for the renewal
of permanent way, etc., was iniquitous.
Whereupon Johnson :
In ’O7 Parliament set up two funds called the
Railway Interest and Reserve Fund and the Raihvav
Additions and Improvements Fund, which the Act
declared to be permanent. In ’O9 Parliament “bor-
rowed” the money from the Commissioners and said
“We will pay you interest.” In T 2 they collared
the money outright to the tune of £72,000.
Bayles (weakly) : “That is quite correct”!
Then the engineer got slowly down and
scraped him off the wheel.

COMMONWEALTH OF AUSTRALIA.
Department of the Navy,
Melbourne, 17th May, 1917.
Tenders for the Supply of Soluble
Chocolate to H.M.A. Naval Estab-
lishments, Sydney, N.S.W.
TENDERS are invited, subject to the
conditions of tendering, until 12 noon
on Wednesday, 20th June, 1917, for the
supply and delivery of Chocolate to H.M.A.
Naval Establishments, Sydney.
Tender Form, Specification and full parti-
culars may be obtained on application at the
Office of ‘the Director of Navy Contracts.
Navy Office, Melbourne; the Victualling
Store Officer, Royal Edward Victualling
Yard, Pyrmont, Sydney; or at the Naval
Staff Offices, Birkenhead (S.A.), Brisbane.
Fremantle and Hobart.
Tenders are to be endorsed “Tender for
Chocolate” on top left-hand corner of
envelope and addressed (if by post prepaid)
to the Director of Navy Contracts, Navy
Office, Melbourne; or the Director of Navy
Contracts, c/o one of the above-mentioned
addresses, as the case may be.
Tenders to be in all cases on the printed
forms, which can be obtained as above.
The lowest or any tender not necessarily
accepted.
JOSEPH COOK,
Minister of State for the Navy.

To Make Ends Meet—ls/-
Most every thing
that goes to make
Big Ben the good
clock he is has
doubled and
trebled in price.
He found it difficult
the past year to keep
his cost to you at the
old figure and still
make ends meet.
To add to this, the cost
of his passage overseas
has been raised. It was
necessary for him to
increase his price. For
the present and till fur-
ther notice, he’s selling
at 15/-.
Big Ben is seven inches tall,
well built, handsome, punctual.
He has a big, easily-read dial—a cheer-
ful,deep-tOned voice— large, comfortable
winding keys—a light running movement
with watchlike escapement—an alarm
that calls steadily for five minutes or

The Inky Way.
“Catchline” : Their mother’s name will be
carried into battle by two of the three sons
of the late Jennings Carmichael (Mrs.
Mullis), the Australian writer of some sym-
pathetic little verses who, fallen on dark days
through no fault of her own, died in London
in February, 1904. She was originally a
hospital nurse, and her fragments of rhyme
attracted a band of admirers. In 1907
an Australian in London found the
children by diligent search, and later they
were brought out to Melbourne. They were
then 14, 11 and 8. The eldest enlisted with
the Wireless Signallers in 1915. He is
now after the Turk. The second lad is in
camp, and the third son must wait until he
is a little older before signing on.
“HAMER” MERCER says farewell.
“Masetto”: Melbourne journalism of 50
years back provided more solid entertain-
ment than in these libel-haunted times. In the
early ’6o’s an Age par charged the Herald
with a lie and termed its author “a Parlia-
mentary flunkey.” Geo. Levey, then editor
of the Herald and an M.P., took this to
himself and retorted in a fitting manner.
Thereupon editor G. P. Smith, of the Age,
talked ominously of pulling somebody’s
nose. Soon after Levey met his opponent
near his office (then in Elizabeth-street) and,
twitting him with his threat, struck him
with a riding-whip, threw the weapon away
and set to with his fists. Smith, although
the bigger man, was getting the worst of
the scrap when the contestants rolled into a
bootmaker’s shop. The tradesman pushed
them out into the street, and some of the
Age staff hauled their chief off. The same
night Levey took his place in the House,
while his rival scowled at him from the press
gallery.

Adelaide University thinks it has got some
real poetry at last in exchange for the Bun-
dey Prize, founded in memory of the Judge
in 1913. The policy of setting a subject
brought in cold, machine-made rhymes until
the war was listed. Then Leon Gellert,
once of S.A. Education Department, but
now a sergeant of the 10th. came to light
with a series of high-explosive sonnets and
lyrics.
THE SONG AND THE SINGER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My poet wrote: “Ah! blissful days
Where Healesville’s golden surges rise.
Ah! sweet, warm nights when hidden ways
Brought thee to me with starlit eyes.
No more of joy can love reveal.
When other flames illume the night,
In all their glory, dear, I feel
Thy subtle warmth, and see thy light.
There came to me a thought of awe:
“Here’s one that recks not mortal law,
A lover lion-like and free.
Ah! Lord, send such a love to me.”
My poet wrote: “I scorn the lays
Of puny circumscribed life.
Give me my foe, and tumbled days,
The wounds and fever of the strife.
I rive with scorn their little laws,
Their measured rules I laughing try,
And throw them from my limbs as straws,
For I must live, or I will die.
With beating pulse I read his line,
And whispered, “Here is the fine,
Exalted soul. He seems to speak
From some wind-polished mountain peak.
My poet wrote: “The open way,
The great sea racing ’neath the sky,
These keep me drifting night and day.
My tent is broad, its roof is high.
By writhing plain and mountain crust
I chase the eagles on the wing.
Nought will from my unburied dust
But restless vines and creepers spring.
I said: “Here is the stir that shook,
And oped the wide world like a book,
The poet-soul implanted in
Some bearded, hook-nosed Bedouin.
At last I saw a little man
In down-heeled slippers and a fez,
Who hastened with a painted can,
And watered all his cabbages.
There rooted in a wee backyard,
By stings of heat and storm forsook,
Is living still my splendid bard.
His wife she has a famished look.
’Tis said, what’s learned in suffering
The animated poets sing.
But with a keener note is trilled
A gnawing instinct unfulfilled.
VICTORIA.
EDWARD DYSON.

A Gallant Anzac Praises
Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery
Private Jack Molloy, 1097, C Company,
19th Battalion, felt it his duty to leave a
permanent billet to enlist in defence of the
Empire early in 1915. He was in the famous
Suvla Bay landing, and was wounded in an
attack shortly afterwards. After lying some
hours exposed to Turkish machine-gun and
rifle fire, the stretcher-bearers found him and
carried him down to the clearing station,
whence lie was shipped from Gallipoli Penin-
sula to Malta. He was three months in
Floriana Hospital, and returned to Australia
on the Kanowna. This g'allant Australian
writes:—•
“When I left Aus-
tralia with the 19th
Battalion, sth Brigade,
on June 25, 19*5, I
packed in my kit a
bottle of ‘New Dis-
covery,’ because I had
been suffering while in
camp from catarrh of
the throat. I cured
myself completely with
this bottle. I could not
get ‘New Discovery’ in
Egypt, but I had some
sent to me at Malta,
where I was lying
wounded in Floriana
Hospital. I have always
regarded ‘N e w Di s-
covery’ as my only
friend in the medicine
line. I always keep it
near at hand.
“Yours faithfully,
“J. MOLLOY.’’

Scots of the Riverina.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
boy ran away to the city from his
I home at Christmas time —-
They were Scots of the Riverina, and
to run from home was a crime.
The old man burned his letters, the first and
last he burned,
And he scratched his name from the Bible
when the old woman’s back was turned.
A year went past and another; and the fruit
went down the line.
They heard the boy had enlisted, but the old
man made no sign.
His name must never be mentioned on the
farm by Gundagai—
They were Scots of the Riverina with ever
the kirk hard by.
The boy came home on his ‘•final,” and the
township’s bonfire burned.
Ilis mother’s arms were about him, but the
old man’s back was turned.
The daughters begged for pardon till the old
man raised his hand—
A Scot of the Riverina who was hard to
understand.
The boy was killed in Flanders, where the
bravest heroes die.
There were tears at the Grahame homestead
and grief in Gundagai;
But the old man ploughed at daybreak and
the old man plought t’ the mirk —
There were furrows of pain in the orchard
while his housefolk went to the kirk.
The hurricane-lamp in the rafters dimly and
dimly burned :
And the old man died at the table when the
old woman’s back was turned.
Face down on his bare arms folded he sank
with his wild grey hair
Outspread o’er the open Bible and a -name
re-written there.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.
Tobacco.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The men in the galley sat playing poker
listlessly and listening to the rain on
the tin roof. They had been out of
tobacco for nearly two days, and all the salt
had gone out of life. It was even money
that the creek between them and the station
was already in flood and that the cart with
supplies would not get through ; but the
shortage of beef and flour was a small thing
compared with the want of tobacco.
Curran, the head-driller, who was said to
go to sleep with a pipe between his teeth,
lay on a bunk in the corner with his eyes
closed and tried to blot out time. lie was
afraid to eat his dinner lest it should sharpen
the craving inside him. The others sat
about moodily, some biting on the stems of
cold pipes, some cursing monotonously at
their cards, and all showing signs of ragged
nerves. They had tried many kinds of sub-
stitute, from tea to brown paper, but had
found no lasting satisfaction in any of them.
“Never mind,” said the cook, ' with an
attempt at cheerfulness. “Young Andy’s
bound to get through with the dray. That
boy wouldn’t let half a dozen floods stop'
him.”
Curran open his strained eyes like a man
in pain.
“The young beggar doesn’t smoke,” he
muttered. “His troubles about getting
through!”
“He’ll do it all right—take it from me,”
the cook repeated, going to the door.
The world outside presented itself as a
wilderness of dripping mulga, above which
the tall derrick reared its head like the mast
of a ship. So pervasive was the rain that
even the wood seemed as if it might dissolve
like sugar at any moment. It would have
been a relief to the men in camp if they
could have worked, but it had been impossible
to keep a fire going in the engine, for the
stacked firewood was soaked through and
through. Neither could they kill time by
reading, for every paper was crammed full
of advertisements of pipes and tobaccos,
tobaccos nutty and aromatic, tobaccos cool
and life-giving, tobaccos whose fragrance
made their heads swim in intoxication. Then
there were the solid, chunky pipes, with soft,
desirable curves.... The cook, in order to
stop this morbid pre-occupation had collected
all the papers surreptitiously and hidden
them in a corner under the bread-tin.
Suddenly the heavy, splashing sound of a
horse’s feet was heard, followed by a rock-
ing of wheels.
“It’s Andy, sure enough,” said the cook. .
“What did I tell yer?”
They tumbled over one another in their
haste to get to the door just as the mud-
covered dray came crawling out of the tim-
ber. Andy, with a sack arranged in tri-
angular fashion for a head-covering, was
sitting on the shaft, a cheerful grin on his
bright young face.
“Got through just in time,” he announced.
“Another hour and I’d have been blocked.
It’s beginning to run a banker and no error.
The current at the crossing nearly carried
oP Plummer off his feet, but he stuck to it
as if he was out to win a bet. Logs and
branches coming down, too. .. . It’s going
to be a proper flood “this time."
lie was a raw-boned youth of about 18,
but quick and capable, and just now he
was proud of his exploit. The cook was
dragging away the sacks and sorting out the
kerosene and stores.
“Where’s the tobacco?” he asked suddenly.
Andy pulled himself up with a jerk.
“Ain’t it in the box with the loose stores?”
“No,” said the cook emphatically. “It
just ain’t.”
“There were 12 plugs,” Andy floundered.
“Six light and six dark. I saw them taken
out of the caddies myself, and tol’ the store-
keeper to carry them out and put them in
that box. I’m dead sure I tol’ him.”
He suddenly had an impression of being
surrounded by a circle of wolfish eyes, and
his throat became dry. The silence of the
other men was horrible.
“It was that fool of a storekeeper,” he
repeated. “I was busy getting beef from the
butcher’s shop. ‘Wrap them in paper and
put them in the box,’ I tol’ him.”
Curran broke the silence with a roar.
“You damned young fool! I believe you
done it on purpose !”
The other men looked as if they were
liable to jump on him at any moment and
tear him limb from limb. But for Andy their
heaviest curses added nothing to the inten-
sity of the situation. All the way home he
had been priding himself upon getting
through, and now lie had to face this The
cook was ransacking the dray as if he could
not believe the evidence of his eyes, and
amid the general uproar Andy slunk away
to take the harness off the horse and turn it
loose.
Half an hour later the cook found him
gloomily saddling his lean mare at the back
of the galley.
“Where are you going?” he asked. “You
can't get back to the station now.”
“I’m going across country to Bindi,” said
Andy doggedly. “It’s only 15 miles, and
Redd’s Creek ain’t such a proposition as the
other one.”
“You’ll never do it!” said the cook.
“Won’t I?” Andy flashed. “Then you’ve
seen the last of me. Do you think I’m going
to sit in that galley with the other blokes
poking mullock at me?”
The cook watched him ride away down
the slippery ridge, every inch of him drip-
ping rain, and then turned back to the gloom
of the galley to get dinner for the others.
“Queer young beggar!” he muttered. “And
I’m game to bet he hasn’t had a feed since
before sun-up!”
But Andy rode on Avith a bitterness in his
heart that made him forget the Avant of
food. He did not understand this erotic
craving for tobacco, but he kneAv that the
camp would be an utterly impossible place
for him unless he got it. A youthful sense
of the world’s injustice flooded his soul, but
his jaAv was thrust out aggressively. He
would sooner be drowned in Redd’s Creek
than fail in his mission.
“I’ll slioav them,” he muttered, though he
did not quite know what he meant to show
them unless it was his unconquerable
tenacity.
It was 15 miles to Bindi, across country
that Avas a maze of emu-bush and mulga
scrubs. Andy Avas wet to the skin, and there
was no heat in his blood except that of
moral indignation. Cold runnels of water
trickled from his hair and made a goose-
flesh progress down his spine. Dripping
branches flapped in his face. And the mare
slipped continually in the black mud, plough-
ing on again spiritlessly Avith drooping head
as if she were as careless as Andy himself
about what happened to her.
When they reached Redd’s Creek it was a
swim for man or beast; but the water had
spread wide over the flats and the current
was not strong. Andy forced the mare into
it, and at the third attempt she plunged
forward Avith a grunt, as if she had com-
mitted her soul finally to the deep. Andy
swam behind her, holding on to her skimpy
tail, and wondering what strange happiness
lay in a dirty-looking plug of black tobacco.
Half an hour later the Bindi stockmen,
sitting on the verandah of the men’s hut,
saw a limping horse and draggled rider
crawl out of the wet bushes above the yard.
Until they came quite close the two figures
seemed to have merged inextricably, but at
the gate the rider separated himself from the
saddle by an effort of will.
“It’s young Andy !” said the Bindi over-
seer. “Now what the devil?”
Andy slouched up with an appearance of
casualness.
“Is the store open ?” he asked. “The boys in
the Collabri camp are out of tobacco.”
A large bead of water hung on the end of
his red nose and every part of him seemed
to ooze Avater. In spite of himself he could
not control the chattering of his teeth.
“Strip your duds off and get in front of
the fire,” said the overseer. “Tobacco be
damned ! Get three fingers of rum inside of
you.”
“No, thanks,” said Andy; “I gotter get
back before it’s too dark.”
“What? To-night?”
“Sure thing,” said Andy. “And I want
the loan of a horse. My oP mare’s done in,
and she’s got a strained shoulder into the
bargain.”
The other men crowded round him and
ridiculed the idea of going back that night,
but Andy was immovable as a rock. There
was a fanatical intensity in his grey eyes.
His Avill was tautened by the conviction that
if life Avas to remain bearable he had to
get back with the tobacco before the men
Avent to sleep. There was only one horse
in the night-paddock, a hammer-headed
brumby that had not long been broken, and
in a few minutes he had it saddled and was
plodding back again, the tobacco and a few
new pipes slung round his neck in a strip of
American cloth. He could not trust them
even to his saddle-pouch.
Time, that evening, ceased to exist for
Andy. In the middle of the creek he lost
contact Avith the brumby, which struggled
out to the other bank ahead of him and made
off into a belt of sandalwood. The advent
of thick darkness found him pushing along
on foot, Avondering if he could pick up the
brumby’s tracks in the morning and rescue
his neAV saddle. But even that mattered
little. Nothing Avas important except the
safe delivery of the tobacco.
He lost himself a dozen times, and found
his way back to the right track by a series
of miracles. In the torpid recesses of his
mind the only lh'e thought was a curiosity
about the substance he Avas carrying. Why
had he never tried it before? As a boy he
had smoked roots in the creek-bed. but they
had only made him sick. Perhaps it was the
memory of those roots that had caused him
to be abstinent. He had a hankering after
most of the insignia of adolescence, such as
spurs, whips and shaving-tackle, but he had
never really Avanted to own a pipe.
The darkness of eternity was suddenly
pierced by a light. Andy blundered up to
the door of the galley on feet that seemed to
belong to someone else and pushed open the
door with triumphant violence. For a moment
the glare of the slush lamp blinded his eyes.
Curran Avas lying on the bunk reading a
weekly paper, while the other men were
uproarious over poker and the cook whistled
as he brewed coffee at the fire. They looked
up at his entrance and the reek of tobacco
caught him in the throat.

“It’s young Andy,” said Curran. “Lord,
what a water-rat you look, too 1. .. . What
made you go off in such a flaming hurry?
The plugs was there all the time. They was
packed inside a tin and the cook took" them

cutting off some strips from a plug, lit his

VANCE PALMER.
A Lover of Peace.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
rpHE big guns were doing their best to
justify their existence, but Smithson,
undisturbed by the noise of their busy
day, or an atmosphere that smelt of dust
and spent chemicals, stolidly cleaned his rifle
and fixings.
“Gorstruth!” he remarked suddenly. “I
’ad an norrible dream larst night! I dreamt
the war was all over, and a feller says to
me: ‘You’re goin’ back to Ostralyer, and
goin’ to be discharged.’ ”
“What was ’orrible about it?” asked
Dickson.
“If you ’ad somethin’ to face when you
got back, an’ you dreamt all about it. so as
you fell in a cold sweat, you’d find it
’orrible.”
He put aside his rifle and felt leisurely for
his tobacco and pipe.
“It was all the wife’s fault,” he said
confidentially. “If it ’adn’t ha’ been for
’er” He paused. “She was always an
argumentative sort of woman: the sort of
woman who, if you argued with ’er, would
argue and argue until she got ’er own way,
an’, if you didn’t argue with ’er, 71 simply,
take it, and then argue and argue to make
you admit she was right, or just because she
was sure, by the way you didn’t discuss the
matter, that you took no interest in your
wife’s affairs. An’ she was the sort of
woman, too, who was convinced that a man
made money just for ’is wife to spend. She
was all right at first, of course: women
mostly are. You don’t expect a baby to start
walkin’ round as soon as it’s born ; but a
woman gets ’er stride quicker than a baby.
She was the sorter woman ’oo gets the t.p.
’abit bad, too, buying things, mostly, just to
’ave them. When it took ’er first it was
good-bye to decent meals. First it was a
sewing-machine, an’ I used to look at the
damn thing an’ try to fancy the good, solid
beefsteaks that was in it, and that I was
eatin’ them. She said it would be cheaper,
because she could make ’er own clothes. But
it wasn’t, because she ’ad to ’ave stuff to
make ’em with, and it being dead easy, she
was always makin’ them. An’ each time she
’ad a new dress made she ’ad to go some-
where to show it off, because she said it
would ha’ been a waste of money if she
didn’t —and that cost money. When she’d
paid off the machine she mortgaged it again
to pay a deposit on a pianner, so I ’ad to
pay the instalments on two things; an’ then
We ’acl to ’ave pieters for the drawin’-room—■
expensive pieters; some of them cost ten
bob each, and a lot more for the framin’
an’ parties fer peojfie to ’ear the pianner *,
and, because of them, we ’ad to get a
gramophone, because the pianner didn't go
round, so to speak. Later, she ’ad to get a
drawing-room suite —red plush all round the
edges, only the backs used to twist round
after a time, if you leaned against them —
all because kitchen chairs didn’t look nice
in the drawing-room. After a time there
wasn’t a thing in the ’ouse that wasn’t
mortgaged, most of it for something else,
and there were so many t.p. instalment
people coming on Monday that when the
grocer called ’e was too late. Yet, when I
asked ’er if we couldn’t get some decent
meals on time payment she went wild,
saying I was always grumbling, and thought
of nothing but me stomach. But she got
scared ’erself about the t.p.; and, to improve
things, she made me insure me life for a
bob a week in case I should die before the
things were paid off.
“Some say it’s cheaper to move than to
pay rent. It isn’t. It may be for people
who haven’t the time-payment habit and
don’t ’ave to cart round a vanful of
mortgages. Moving’s an expensive thing;
an’ even the new credit you can get don’t
last long, and the agents coming after the
instalments soon find you again. But I
drew the line when she found that some
removers were prepared to shift you on the
time-payment system. I’d stood it quiet,
but I went off the handle. An’ on Friday I
blew in the insurance money and the pianner
instalment, and I’d just started on the new
Italian bedstead when me wife came to look
for me —another nasty ’abit of ’efs—and
dragged me ’ome.
“When a landlord an* a ’ole lot of other
people got tired, and all the mortgages whs
cither sold up dr taken away, I could ’ave
felt glad if it wasn’t for the wife. She
said it was all through my drinkin’ ’abits;
an’ she didn’t like goin’ back to plain
furniture, which we paid for. She was
always sore about it an’ wantin’ to argue.
What she used to say, mostly, was that if
I’d been a decent husband I’d ha’ waited
until I was in a position to provide her with a
pianner, and all the rest, and had myself
decently insured, before I asked ’er to marry
me. Which was pretty ’ot, seeiu’ —well, you
know!
“I’d got dead sick of seeing all my money
goin’ on things you couldn’t eat, and I forgot
to tell the missus about the new award. The
increase was pocket money, anyway; but
she found out. She said I’d been deceiving
’er all my married life—l was the meanest
man on earth —I was a liar and a thief ’oo
took the bread out of my children’s mouth.”
• “I wouldn’t ’ave no woman talk to me like
that!” said Dickson.
“You ain’t married,” said Smithson.
“Well, after that she’d see a heading ‘A New
Award’ in the paper. She wouldn’t read
what it was about, or say anything about
it to me, until I’d come to ’and over me pay ;
and then she’d start. ‘Tryin’ to deceive me
again!’ By that time it’d be too late to
look up the newspapers an’ show ’er it was
the boiler-makers or something, an’ she was
’ard to convince.”
“Whv did VOU argue with ’er?” interrupted
nickin “Fd”—
“If you could keep out of arguing with
mv wife vou could be sure of getting back
from ’ere'' without a scratch. I’d just sit
smoking me pipe takin’ no notice, and she’d
grab me pipe to make me talk. If I tried
to get it from ’er she’d scream ‘Murder!’
and p’raps the police would be sent for by
neighbors ’oo thought me a brute. An’ if
I tried to get away she’d ’ide me clothes
where I couldn’t find them. An’ once, when
I’d kept from answerin’ ’er all the evenin’,
she ’id me pants so as I’d 'ave to argue in
the mornin’ before I -went.”
“I’d ’ave got out fer good,” commented
Dickson. . .
“That’s just what I did. It was the limit
when she went to my boss to see if what I
said about an award not affecting me was
right. It gave ’im an impression that I
was a cove who spent a lot of his money oil
beer; and the boss, being a feller who hated
booze, reckoned me a boozer, and sacked
me. The idea spread round and made it
’arder for me to get work. .
“The missus was broken-’earted when I
left. She ’ad no one to argue with. She'
felt it ’er mission to make me do me duty j
as a good woman she couldn’t let ’er ’usband
go all wrong without sendin’ ’im to gaol so
as’e'd see the error of’is ways. . ,
“She was the noble-’earted, lotig-sufferni,
affectionate wife ’oo wanted ’er ’usband to
return to the bosom of > family. In spite
of ’is ’abit of ’owing bills, an’ only gettin
furniture oh time-payment which e never
paid, an’ letting ’is insurance policies lapse
so as ’is children would be unprovided for,
an’ drinkih’ beei’ an’ making rows, an’ ill-
treatin’ ’is wife, she realised she was ’is
wife and owed a duty to ’im.
“She did ’er duty well. The bosses at the
places I worked at heard the sad story as
she argued with me by the job, urgin’ me to
come ’ome an’ be good; an’ they mostly told
me that if I couldn’t attend to me work I
was no use to them ; an’ every ’ouse I stayed
at they ’eard it. I stayed at a lot, too, to
dodge ’er. If she got ’er money she reckoned
I must be makin’ much more, and if_l didn’t
she reckoned I ought to be at ome so as to
make what I did earn go as far as possible.
When she got very annoyed with me she’d
sool people I owed money on to me.
“So I ’ad to go ’ome, just because it
became too ’ot; an’ a man, especially i when
work’s slack, can’t keep two ’omes goiii. I
was sore, of course; an’ she wanted to argtte
with me because I seemed to ’ave lost all
affection for ’er, in spite of all she’d done for
me !”
He paused and commenced to cut his
tobacco slowly, with his eyes fixed moodily
on the ground.
“I’d have settled ’er 1” exclaimed Dickson,
de “Tlmt’s what I did,” said Smitlisoh. “Do
you blame me?”
Dickson was moved by a sudden excite-
“Wliat 1 You—murdered ’er?” he whis-

looked at him quickly.
“No! Didn’t I say I ’ated what I’d ’ave
to face in Sydney! No, I enlisted.”
A shell blew pieces of the vicinity to bits,
filled the air with dust, and pungent smoke
and flying fragments, and sent Dickson fly-
ihg for cover. When he reappeared from his
shelter he saw Smithson, still calmly cutting
tobacco, very little disturbed,
“Phew!” he breathed. That was a close
go!”
“Pooh!” retorted Smithson. What s a
little thing like that to a man oo s been
through ail I ’ave? You oughter ave got
married before you came to the war, son >
Anyway, it’s tucker and shelter, without haw
mg to battle for it—l mean, really battle for
it; and the wife gets ’er money without you
being able to spend it before she can get it.
And, even if she doesn’t, she can’t get at a
man to rouse about it. An’ you’ve got a bit
0 f money yourself, and not too much time to
spend it—why, Lord! you can get blind
drunk ’ere with a bob!”
He looked dreamily at the opposite bank
0 f the trench. "It’s ’Eaven, that’s what it
jg j” said. “An’ I dreamt it was all over
an d we ’ad to go back !”
